By Blood and Bloom

by Anonymous

Summary

Rio Vidal has had a bad couple of months. Her girlfriend of four years cheated on her and then promptly left her for some young thing, and her creative juices seem to have dried up completely. She has to deliver something to her publisher in the next six months, but inspiration has completely deserted her, just like stupid Carol.

Then she receives a letter. And an inheritance from a grandmother she never even knew existed. In fucking Maine. But hey, her life already sucks, so maybe a trip to middle of nowhere Thistle Grove, Maine is exactly what she needs?

Notes

There is this account on Instagram called Tiny Houses Perfect that I absolutely love. The idea for this story came to me after I saw one of the posts there and the house featured looked so good, like it had come straight out of a witchy story. I just kept thinking about it and an idea popped up. And then it's just been downhill from there.

You can check it out here: https://www.instagram.com/tinyhousesperfect/p/DIZ0_O7MKJA/

Chapter 1: One

The envelope didn’t belong.

Rio Vidal spotted it immediately, wedged between a crumpled electric bill and a glossy pizza flyer that still had grease on the edges. It was heavy, thick with something more than paper. Cream-colored. Sealed in wax. Her name written across the front in looping, deliberate script: Rio M. Vidal.

No return address. No stamp.

She frowned. Her life didn’t include mystery letters. Lately, it barely included anything at all.

The apartment was a disaster. Empty coffee cups. A sketchpad on the couch, open to a blank page she’d stared at for two straight hours. An open Word doc titled Something With Meaning blinking with zero words typed. She was on month three of the worst creative drought of her life, and if one more well-meaning fan asked on Instagram when the next volume of Wolf & Violet was coming out, she might scream.

And of course, there was Carol.

Carol, who had not only cheated on her with some twenty-two-year-old barista with a half-shaved head and a septum ring, but had done it in their apartment. In their bed. Carol, who packed up her side of the closet and left a sticky note that read “You’re brilliant, Rio. Just not… enough for me.”

Rio cracked the wax seal with her thumbnail and unfolded the letter, heart pounding with something halfway between dread and numb curiosity.

To Miss Rio Vidal,

I write on behalf of the estate of Eliza Vidal, your grandmother. Per the terms of her will, you are named as the sole heir to her property in Thistle Grove, Maine. Enclosed is the deed to the cottage, located at the edge of the woods near the old well. While the home has remained unoccupied for some time, it is yours, should you choose to claim it.

We trust you’ll know what to do.

Warmly,

Adelaide Morrissey, Executor of the Vidal Estate

 

“Grandmother,” Rio said flatly.

She didn’t have one. Or at least, she hadn’t thought she did. No family, not really. She’d grown up bouncing from foster homes to group homes, the name Vidal clinging to her like a stray label no one bothered to peel off. She’d always thought her last name had just been something a tired social worker had randomly chosen for her as an abandoned baby.

She’d never heard of Thistle Grove, let alone an Eliza Vidal.

She read the letter again, slower this time. The words should you choose to claim it echoed in her head like a dare.

She was still staring at it when her phone buzzed.

Alice Wu lit up on the screen.

Rio answered. “Hey.”

“You sound weirder than usual. What’s going on?”

Rio let out a short laugh. “I got a letter.”

“Ominous. From?”

“Apparently… my dead grandmother. Who left me a cottage. In Maine.”

A beat. “You don’t have a grandmother.”

“That’s what I thought.”

“You’ve got to be kidding me.”

“I’m not. I’m holding the deed in my hands right now. And the keys to it. It says Thistle Grove, which sounds like a place where people disappear into the woods and reemerge twenty years later with antlers and riddles.”

“Oh, absolutely haunted. I’m picturing cobwebs and taxidermy. Are you going?”

“I think so. I mean, there’s nothing keeping me here. I’ve got nothing holding me here. No relationship. No working plot. And I’m starting to think the apartment is haunted by Carol’s ghost, or worse—her taste in music.”

Alice sighed. “Okay, first of all, never speak of Carol’s playlist again. Second of all, if you go and get murdered in the woods by a cult, I will come resurrect you just to slap you. And third… yeah. Maybe you should go.”

“Really?”

“You’ve been drowning, Rio. Maybe this is weird. But maybe weird is exactly what you need?”

That landed.

“Yeah…” Rio nodded to herself, eyes on the envelope again. “Yeah, okay. I’ll get my stuff together and buy a plane ticket, I guess. I’ll text you when I get there.”

“You’d better. And send pictures. If it turns out there’s some sexy lumberjack or haunted librarian involved, I want all the details.”

Rio smiled for the first time in what felt like weeks. “Deal.”

~*~

Portland smelled like salt and rain.

Rio wheeled her suitcase through the rental lot, the sea wind knotting her dark hair into something wild. She tossed her bag into the backseat of a compact gray SUV and unfolded the printed directions Alice had insisted she bring—“Phones die, especially in murder towns.”

The drive from Portland to Thistle Grove took her off the interstate and deep into the pine-stretched wilderness of inland Maine. The roads narrowed, the GPS signal grew patchy, and mist began to settle low along the treetops like it had been waiting for her.

She passed through one blinking stoplight town after another. Gas stations with signs for maple candy. Antique shops closed for the season. An ice cream stand shaped like a giant wooden cone, boarded up for winter.

And finally—after miles of nothing but forest and gray sky—a crooked wooden sign appeared by the roadside, partially swallowed by moss:

Welcome to Thistle Grove. Population: 1,268

She took a slow breath, rolled down the window, and let the pine-heavy air wash over her.

The trees felt closer here.

Like they were watching.

Waiting.

~*~

Thistle Grove looked like it had been forgotten by time—and liked it that way.

The main road curved gently into town, lined with crooked brick buildings and hand-painted signs. A hardware store with a porch swing out front. A diner with lace curtains and a chalkboard menu that read Clam Chowder Today, Just Like Yesterday. A shop called Moon & Salt that sold crystals and secondhand books. Most of the storefronts were dark, windows fogged from the cold and condensation.

Rio pulled into a gravel parking spot near a tea shop, the only place still lit up like it meant it. Inside, the warm glow of Edison bulbs flickered against wood-paneled walls. A bell above the door jingled as she stepped in.

The air smelled like cinnamon, pine, and something faintly metallic—like old keys or blood oranges.

A woman stood behind the counter, serving a plate of something warm to a customer. She had wild silver curls, round glasses, and earrings shaped like tiny brooms. Her cardigan was buttoned all wrong, and she looked like someone who wouldn’t just talk to her plants, but expect them to talk back.

She looked up. “Well, hello there.”

Rio smiled, a little awkward. “Hi. I was hoping you could help me. I just got into town—”

“Oh, I can see that. You’ve got the fog on your boots and confusion in your eyes.”

Rio blinked. “I… okay.”

The woman leaned her elbows on the counter, studying her. “Let me guess. You’re a Vidal.”

That gave Rio pause. “How did you—?”

“Sweetheart, you’ve got Eliza’s mouth. And that walk—like you’re about to bolt or punch someone, but haven’t decided which.” Her smile softened. “I’m Lilia, by the way. Lilia Calderu. I run this place when it lets me.”

Rio took that in slowly. “Okay. Sure. Well, I just inherited a cottage? From my—apparently—grandmother, Eliza Vidal. I’ve never been here before.”

“Oh, honey. No one ever comes to Thistle Grove unless something brings them.” Lilia tilted her head, eyes glittering. “Do you know where the cottage is?”

“I was hoping someone could give me directions. All I got is ‘edge of the woods, near the well’”

Lilia’s smile curved. “You don’t find that house. It finds you.”

Rio exhaled. “Is that a thing here? Cryptic answers and small-town riddles?”

Lilia let out a barking laugh. “Oh, I like you. You’re Eliza’s granddaughter, alright. You’ve got her spark and her patience. Take the main road out of town, follow the fork toward the woods. When the trees start to lean in, you’ll see a narrow lane with no name and a crooked fence. That’s her.”

“Her?”

“The house,” Lilia said, like it was obvious. “She’s particular. And she hasn’t had anyone live in her since Eliza passed. Might be a bit moody.”

Rio stared.

Lilia gave a wink and reached under the counter, pulling out a small cloth pouch tied with twine. “For your first night. Cedar, salt, and a little lavender. Keeps the worst of the dreams at bay.”

Rio took it slowly. “Do I want to know what kind of dreams we’re talking about?”

“Depends what the house wants to show you,” Lilia said, her voice quiet now. “But don’t be afraid. She called you for a reason.”

The bell above the door jingled again as Rio stepped back out into the mist, pouch in hand.

The fog had thickened. The air had that heavy silence to it—like something was listening.

Rio looked down at the pouch, then toward the road.

“Okay, Eliza,” she muttered. “Let’s see what the hell you left me.”

~*~

Rio followed the strange woman’s directions. The road narrowed the further she drove. Asphalt gave way to packed dirt, then gravel. The trees grew thicker on either side, bare branches reaching across the lane like fingers laced in prayer. Fog curled low to the ground, and her headlights barely cut through it. It didn’t feel like a road so much as a memory someone had forgotten to finish painting.

Rio kept glancing at the directions Lilia had scrawled on a scrap of brown paper—although really, they hadn’t been much. When the trees lean in, you’ll see it. 

She was just about to mutter something about how incredibly unhelpful that was when the trees actually leaned in.

It wasn’t a metaphor. Not anymore. The pines and oaks actually bent slightly toward the road, as if drawn forward by some long-forgotten instinct. The wind had gone still. The radio had gone static. Even her phone had lost signal three turns ago.

And then she saw it.

At the end of the lane, nestled against the edge of a wood so dark it might as well have been night, stood the house.

Or what was left of it.

It looked like it had grown from the earth itself—twisting and weathered, with pale grey shingles that had curled at the edges and windows like sunken eyes. Ivy clung to the walls like veins. The porch sagged, one corner slightly caved in. The gate hung askew on a rusted hinge. It was the kind of house you’d expect to find in a ghost story, not a legal will.

Rio parked the rental and stepped out slowly, boots crunching on frostbitten gravel.

Up close, the house was no less eerie. The vines weren’t just ivy—some of them looked like flowering nightshade and creeping fig, all tangled together in impossible ways. Moss had overtaken the stone path. The wood was dark with age, but not rotten. Just… old. Like it was holding its breath.

Rio stood in front of the porch, staring up at the second-story window. For half a second, she thought she saw someone there—a flicker of movement. But when she blinked, it was gone.

“Jesus,” she muttered. “What the hell, Eliza.”

She put a hand on the railing. The wood was damp, but warm. Almost like it was alive and recognized her.

She hesitated. Then climbed the steps.

The front door was painted black, cracked and worn. No keyhole. No handle. Just a smooth iron plate carved with a delicate floral pattern—roses and thorns and something curling beneath it. She reached for it—

The door creaked open on its own.

Rio froze.

She waited for some animal to scurry out. For some sound from within.

Nothing.

Just the smell of woodsmoke and dried herbs. And something else, beneath it all. Something like lilac, or old paper, or… memory.

Rio stepped over the threshold, bracing herself for dust and decay. But what hit her first wasn’t rot—it was warmth.

Soft and dry, the air inside the cottage smelled like cedar and cloves, with a faint undercurrent of something floral. It wasn’t what she expected. No mildew. No stale emptiness. Just… a lived-in stillness.

She stepped fully inside, her boots tapping against polished hardwood. To her left was a small sitting room, lit by the fading grey light outside filtering through lace curtains. A fire—an actual, contained, glowing fire—crackled in a stone hearth. That gave her pause.

“Maybe it’s on a timer,” she muttered, though she saw no signs of modern tech anywhere. No thermostat. No cords.

Still, the room was charming—antique furniture with deep cushions, bookshelves filled to the brim, a worn rug in muted tones of gold and green. Someone had clearly taken care of it. She assumed maybe a caretaker came by every so often, though the will hadn’t mentioned one.

The kitchen was tucked into the back, cozy and efficient. A collection of ceramic mugs hung on hooks above the counter, each one mismatched but clearly chosen with purpose. The cabinets were painted a soft sage green, the kind of color that felt like exhaling.

She opened the fridge on a whim. Empty, save for a single glass bottle of milk and a dish of what looked like homemade jam. No expiration dates. No labels. The kind of thing someone would make for themselves without thinking about it.

Rio let the door close and stood in the quiet, scanning the space again.

“Looks like someone still lives here,” she said, unsure if it was meant to be a joke.

Everything felt untouched and well-kept—but not sterile. Nothing was covered in sheets or dust. A book lay open on the arm of a chair. A folded blanket waited at the foot of the stairs. The cottage didn’t feel abandoned.

It felt expectant.

Still, she shook it off. Maybe Eliza had a housekeeper. Maybe someone from town checked on it after her death. Maybe she was just so used to disaster that anything short of it looked like magic.

She ran a hand through her damp hair and kicked off her boots by the door. The floor was warm beneath her socks.

As she climbed the stairs to explore the rest of the cottage, she didn’t see the way the fire burned a little brighter. Or how the curtain at the kitchen window stirred without wind. Or how, behind her, the door clicked shut with something like relief.

~*~

The stairs creaked beneath her step, but they held. The banister was smooth and worn, the kind of wood polished by generations of hands. Halfway up, she paused and glanced behind her. Still alone.

The second floor was narrow, with low ceilings and slanted walls. There were only two rooms—one bedroom, one small study—and a bathroom tucked between them. She peeked inside the latter first. The clawfoot tub was pristine. The tile looked old but clean, and the mirror over the sink was fogged with age at the corners, like it had seen more than it should’ve.

The bedroom was unexpectedly lovely.

A full-sized bed with a wrought-iron frame stood against the far wall, dressed in a patchwork quilt and faded linen sheets. A dresser with glass knobs sat across from it, and a tall window looked out over the woods. When she stepped closer, Rio realized she could just make out the edge of the town from here—a scattering of rooftops and a church steeple in the distance.

She opened the closet and found a handful of old coats and shawls hanging neatly in a row, each one smelling faintly of rose and tobacco. She let the door swing shut and rubbed her arms. The house was warm, but there was a strange tightness in her chest. Not fear—just that quiet, uncanny sense that she’d stepped into someone else’s story.

The study across the hall was lined with shelves. Books everywhere—spines cracked, some handwritten, some leather-bound, a few stacked sideways. A rolltop desk sat beneath the window, and on it, a single notebook. The cover was worn. A fountain pen rested across the top.

She didn’t touch it.

Instead, she exhaled and backed out of the room, closing the door gently behind her. Later, she told herself. When she wasn’t so damn tired.

Back downstairs, she slid her boots on, grabbed her jacket, and headed for the car. The sun was already sinking lower—how had it gotten so late?

She reversed down the narrow drive and turned toward town. This time, the road felt less eerie. More like it was giving her space.

The town proper was quaint in the way every New England postcard tried to be. A main street lined with brick buildings, hand-painted signs, and a few people closing up shop for the day. She found a small grocery store, the kind where the cash register was probably older than she was, and wandered the narrow aisles for bread, coffee, peanut butter, and a few cans of soup.

She was paying when she remembered: no service. No signal. No internet.

She turned to the cashier—a teenager with green nail polish and a nose ring. “Hey, is there a hardware store or something around here? Or anyone I could talk to about getting internet set up?”

The teen blinked slowly, then jerked her chin toward the back of the store. “You want Lilia. She’s probably still at The Hollow.”

“The Hollow?”

“It’s a finer…Teashop. It’s weird. You’ll see it.”

Rio raised a brow. “And she… does internet?”

“She knows everybody,” the girl said with a shrug. “If you want something done around here, Lilia’s your best bet.”

“Right,” Rio said, grabbing her bags. “Thanks.”

The bell over the door jingled as she stepped out into the dusk. The air smelled like woodsmoke and distant rain. She loaded the groceries into the car and stood there for a second, staring down the street.

The Hollow.

She wasn’t ready for it, but she was going anyway.

~*~

The Hollow didn’t look like much from the outside. Faded blue paint, a flickering porch light, and a hand-painted sign that read The Hollow Tea Shop—though it smelled more like diner grease and cinnamon than any teahouse Rio had ever walked into. Now she recognized it as the place she stopped at to ask for directions earlier.

Inside, it was… charming in that chaotic, homey way. Booths with mismatched cushions, tables dressed in floral oilcloth, and a chalkboard menu that offered both “Lavender Lemon Scones” and “Bacon Double Cheeseburgers.” At the far end of the room stood a long counter, and behind it—wearing a bright red apron and an unapologetically large crystal necklace—was Lilia Calderu.

She spotted Rio instantly. “Well, look what the fog dragged in.”

Rio gave a half-smile and let the warmth of the place settle around her. “Told you I’d find it.”

“Mm-hmm,” Lilia said, wiping her hands on a dishtowel. “And did it let you in?”

Rio blinked. “The house? Yeah. It was unlocked, actually.”

Lilia hummed in that same mysterious way she had before, like the words weren’t really about what they sounded like. “Then it’s yours, sure enough. Sit. I’ll get you a cup of tea. Or coffee. Or whiskey.”

“Tea’s good,” Rio said, sliding into a booth near the window. “And… weird question, but is there someone around here who installs internet? I’ve got no signal and no idea how I’m supposed to get work done.”

Lilia poured hot water into a floral teacup and brought it over herself, setting it down with a clink. “Internet, hmm? That’ll be Tommy Finch. Lives up on Rook Hill, but he only comes into town on Thursdays. Don’t trust anything that buzzes, that one, but he’ll wire you up if you ask nicely.”

“Thursday,” Rio said, sighing. “That’s three days.”

Lilia grinned. “Perfect amount of time to stop buzzing yourself. Might even help that writer’s block you’ve been dragging around.”

Rio’s eyes narrowed. “Did I say I was a writer?”

“No, darling,” Lilia said, already turning back toward the counter. “But it’s written all over you.”

~*~

Rio had just taken her first sip of tea—sweet and herbal, surprisingly comforting—when the bell over the door chimed.

She didn’t look up right away. It was just someone coming in. Another local. Another stranger.

But then the air shifted.

It wasn’t anything dramatic. No gust of wind, no lightning bolt. Just… a stillness. A pressure behind her eyes. The tiny hairs on her arms lifted like they were being called to attention.

She turned her head—slowly, instinctively.

The woman who stepped inside didn’t look like much out of the ordinary. Long dark coat, boots wet from the fog, a canvas bag slung over her shoulder. Her dark hair was twisted up messily, like she hadn’t thought much about it. She didn’t glide or prowl or flick her gaze like she owned the place. She just moved—quiet, certain, present.

But Rio felt her before she saw her.

A pulse behind her ribs. A thrum beneath her skin. Something old and strange and rooted in a part of her that didn’t have words.

The woman’s eyes swept the room and paused—just briefly—on her.

It was only a glance, but it landed like a fingerprint on glass.

Then she turned to the counter and said, without looking back, “You’re out of lemon balm again.”

“Yes, I know,” Lilia called over her shoulder. “You don’t have to point it out.”

The woman gave a soft huff of amusement and slid onto a stool, shaking the mist from her coat. She peeled off her gloves finger by finger, efficient and practiced.

Lilia turned toward Rio with a smirk. “That there is Agatha Harkness. She’s got opinions about herbs and no manners to speak of.”

“I’m sitting right here, you know,” Agatha said mildly.

“You’re always right there,” Lilia replied. “Doesn’t make you polite.”

Rio blinked, still watching. Something in her bones was humming.

Agatha turned slightly, catching her gaze for real this time. Her eyes were an impressive shade of blue, like she was looking at a bright summer sky.

“New in town?” she asked, casual but not unkind.

Rio opened her mouth to answer—and hesitated.

There it was again. That pull. Like recognition without memory.

“Yeah,” she said finally. “Just arrived earlier this afternoon.”

Agatha tilted her head, eyes narrowing just the slightest bit. “You must be a Vidal.”

Lilia cut in before Rio could ask how she knew. “She’s staying up at the old Vidal house.”

Agatha’s expression didn’t change. But something in her posture did—a small shift, a flicker in the shadows.

“The house let you in?”

Rio blinked. “It was unlocked.”

Agatha’s lips curved—not quite a smile. “That’s not what I asked.”

~*~

Chapter 2: Two

Chapter Notes

I appreciate all the kudos and comments. Please stick with me as I’m still introducing this world. I promise things will start to make sense soon 🥹

The bell above the door jingled, followed by the impatient thud of boots on old wood. Agatha Harkness didn’t need to look up to know it was Billy. No one else stomped like that and still called it a “grand entrance.”

“Did you rearrange the tinctures again?” he asked, peeling off his scarf dramatically and tossing it onto the counter. “Because I swear I labeled the passionflower correctly this time and someone—someone—put it in with the digestive bitters.”

“That someone was me,” Agatha said calmly, crushing dried lavender buds in her mortar. “And I’ll do it again. I told you I have my own system, teen.”

Billy groaned like she’d personally ruined his life. “You’re chaos incarnate.”

Agatha arched an eyebrow. “And you wear more eyeliner than sense.”

“Rude,” Billy muttered, before ducking into the back room.

The apothecary was quiet again, scented with sage and citrus peel and the low crackle of the space heater in the corner. A customer browsed the shelves in silence. Outside, fog clung to the windows like breath on glass.

Agatha returned to her work, grounding the lavender into a fine dust. Her hands moved out of habit, decades of muscle memory in her bones, her blood. She’d always been good with her hands—blending, binding, making.

It was everything else she was bad at.

She was halfway through mixing a headache draught when it hit her.

A thrum.

Soft. Subtle.

But unmistakable.

The air in the shop shifted—pressure dropping, something stirring beneath the surface of reality. It was like a forgotten chord being plucked, faint but resonant, vibrating through her bones.

She stilled.

Outside, the fog thickened.

The customer turned toward the front window and muttered something about the weather, but Agatha didn’t hear her. Her chest was tight all of a sudden, breath caught on something old and half-remembered.

No spells. No sigils. Just presence.

Someone had arrived. Someone powerful.

Agatha’s fingers curled around the edge of the counter. Her gaze drifted to the front door, though she didn’t know why. Not consciously.

Billy reappeared with a jar of dried rose hips, frowning at the silence. “Did something happen?”

Agatha blinked once and shook herself loose from the moment. “No,” she said, too quickly. “Nothing.”

But she didn’t return to her mixing.

She just stood there, the scent of lavender thick in the air, her thoughts spiraling like smoke.

~*~

The customer finally left with her elderberry syrup and instructions to drink it with lemon peel and honey. Agatha watched the door swing shut, then returned to the counter with her usual briskness. She hated lingering. Lingering gave feelings too much room to stretch out and grow teeth.

Billy, perched on a stool with one leg hooked over the rung, was still watching her. Closely.

“You felt that, didn’t you?” he said.

Agatha didn’t look up. “Felt what?”

“The shift.”

She exhaled through her nose. “You’re being dramatic again.”

“You felt it,” he insisted. “Come on. My teeth felt it. Like a tiny earthquake or something.” He paused, leaning forward with that witch-born curiosity she usually found endearing but today, for some reason, just grated more than usual. “What was it?”

Agatha didn’t answer. Instead, she reached for the bundle of rosemary hanging above her head and began to strip the leaves.

Billy made a sound in the back of his throat—half-frustration, half-knowing—and let the silence stretch. But then, softly: “It’s her, isn’t it?”

That made her pause.

She didn’t say yes, but she didn’t deflect either.

Billy sat up straighter. His voice dropped, suddenly reverent. “The second Thread.”

Agatha gave him a warning look. “Don’t.”

“But it’s true, isn’t it?” His voice was low now, almost a whisper. “The way the air changed? Like something ancient woke up.”

Agatha went back to stripping rosemary. “You’ve been reading too many of your mother’s books.”

“Whoever it is, it’s not just a random stranger, is she?” he pressed. “You felt it, Aunt Agatha. I know you did.”

She turned on him suddenly, not angry—but there was a sharp edge in her voice. “And what do you think happens now, Billy? That we light a bonfire and chant under the moon? That the Threads just snap back into place like we’ve been waiting for a prophecy to come tap us on the shoulder?”

Billy blinked, caught off guard by the bite.

Agatha took a breath. Rolled her shoulders back. “Go inventory the teas. And leave the old magic where it belongs—in the ground. Forgotten.”

He didn’t argue. Just gave her a long look before slipping into the back room, quieter than usual.

Alone again, Agatha stared out the window at the fog curling low across the cobblestones. The sensation still hummed under her skin, low and insistent, like a secret knocking to be let out.

She told herself it meant nothing.

But she didn’t believe it.

~*~

Agatha locked up the apothecary with stiff fingers. The fog had thickened while she’d been inside, painting the narrow street in silver and shadow. Her boots clicked against the damp stone as she walked, the hem of her coat brushing her calves, her thoughts too loud inside her head.

The Hollow was only a few blocks away, tucked into the crook of the main square like it had always belonged there. She’d been feeling off since that weird power shift in the air earlier this afternoon, and she thought that maybe some of Lilia’s chamomile tea would work wonders on her mood.

The little bell over the door chimed as she stepped inside, warm air wrapping around her like a shawl.

The Hollow was warm with the scent of cinnamon and scorched coffee, honey and thyme. Late afternoon light slanted in through fog-fogged windows, catching in the steam that rose from mismatched mugs and chipped teapots.

She felt it before she saw her.

Not just a shift in the air, but a ripple down to her marrow. A sudden knowing that pressed against her ribs like an old bruise. Power—but quiet, submerged, unfamiliar. Old magic, thrumming just beneath the surface like a heartbeat under water.

Her eyes surveyed the space until they landed on her.

The woman sat at the counter as if she didn’t quite belong to the scene—boots damp from mist, denim jacket dark at the shoulders where the fog had settled.

Her eyes swept the room and paused—just briefly—on Agatha.

It was only a glance, but it landed like she had actually touched her skin.

Agatha took a deep breath. Then she turned to the counter and said, without looking back, “You’re out of lemon balm again.”

“I know,” Lilia called over her shoulder. “No need to point it out.”

The woman gave a soft huff of amusement. Agatha watched her like she was trying to remember a name she’d never learned.

Lilia turned toward the woman with a smirk. “That there is Agatha Harkness. She’s got opinions about herbs and no manners to speak of.”

“I’m sitting right here, you know,” Agatha said mildly, still watching the woman.

“You’re always right there,” Lilia replied. “Doesn’t make you polite.”

The woman—Rio, her name would come later like a whisper remembered—blinked, still looking in Agatha’s direction. Her eyes were a hazel somewhere between forest and chocolate, and something in them sharpened when they landed on her again. Recognition flickered—of what, neither of them knew.

Agatha felt the pulse of it low and strange, deep in her chest.

“New in town?” she asked, casual but not unkind.

The woman opened her mouth to answer—and hesitated.

There it was again. That pull. Like recognition without memory. Like standing on the edge of something and realizing you’d already jumped.

“Yeah,” she said finally. “Just arrived earlier this afternoon.”

Agatha tilted her head, studying her more openly now. “You must be a Vidal.”

The name hung in the air like a key turned in a lock.

Before the woman could speak, Lilia cut in. “Eliza’s granddaughter. She’s staying up at the old Vidal house.”

Agatha’s expression didn’t change. But something inside her did—a subtle shift, like shadows pulling in tighter.

“The house let you in?” she asked.

The woman blinked. “It was unlocked.”

Agatha’s lips curved. “That’s not what I asked.”

She watched the woman process that. The flicker of confusion, the furrow between her brows. Then the wary amusement that rose to cover it.

“I’m sorry,” she said, tilting her head. “I think I missed the part where houses are supposed to want things.”

“Oh, they want plenty,” Lilia said, scraping a bit of foam off the top of someone’s latte. “Just not always what you expect.”

The woman raised an eyebrow, then glanced back at Agatha. “Is everyone in Thistle Grove like this?”

Agatha shrugged. “Only the interesting ones.”

The woman huffed—half-laugh, half-sigh—and turned back toward her tea.

Agatha didn’t look away.

Everything in her wanted to. She wasn’t supposed to feel things like this anymore. Hadn’t allowed herself to in years. Not since her mother had vanished into mist and memory. Not since her Nicky…

And yet—

She reached into the pocket of her coat, almost without thinking, and pulled out a small muslin satchel. It had lemon balm, feverfew, and a little chamomile. She slid it across the counter.

“For the headache,” she said.

The woman frowned at it. “I didn’t say I had a headache.”

“I know.”

Agatha turned to go.

At the door, she paused, her fingers brushing the handle. The fog outside clung thick to the windows now, turning the light gauzy and gold.

She looked back once.

The woman was watching her, brows furrowed.

And Agatha felt it again—stronger, this time. The snap of something old and inevitable, stitched into the very air between them.

She said nothing.

Just stepped out into the mist and let the door swing closed behind her.

Outside, the air was damp and cold. Fog wound its way through the streets like something sentient, curling around lamp posts and creeping under doors. It should’ve been familiar. It was familiar. But everything felt…different now.

Agatha stood still for a moment – her reflection ghosted back at her in the glass of The Hollow’s front window — sharp cheekbones, dark coat, ocean-colored eyes.

That woman. Rio.

She hadn’t needed a name to recognize her. Her magic whispered like an old friend.

Because the power humming in her bones hadn’t been stirred by a stranger—it had known her. Known her like a storm knows its eye.

A tremor of something—fear, maybe, or anticipation—crawled down her spine. She didn’t like this feeling. Didn’t like what it meant. The Threads hadn’t been reunited and tangled in over a hundred years. And now—

“You okay?”

Billy’s voice snapped her out of it. He stood just behind her, with his hoodie pulled up, the strings yanked so tight only a sliver of eyeliner-rimmed eyes peeked out. He carried a cinnamon roll in one hand and a lukewarm coffee in the other, looking entirely unbothered by the existential crisis she was clearly having.

“I’m fine,” Agatha said, a little too fast.

Billy stared at her for a second. “You’re doing the broody stare thing again.”

“I don’t brood.”

“Aunt Agatha, your brood has a brood.”

She narrowed her eyes at him. “Shouldn’t you be at the apothecary?”

“You locked it.”

Agatha exhaled through her nose. Right. She had.

Billy looked at her over the rim of his coffee. “So. Come on. You felt it too, right?”

She didn’t answer, but she didn’t need to. He knew.

Billy’s grin curved like mischief. “Three Threads, back in the same place at the same time. It’s happening, huh?”

“It doesn’t mean anything,” she said flatly, turning to walk back toward the apothecary.

He followed, of course. “Uh, yes, it does. That’s the whole point of the Threads. They pull. They twist. They tangle.”

“I’m not interested in getting tangled.”

“You say that like it’s a choice.”

Agatha paused mid-stride. The street was quiet, save for the wind hissing through pine. The town looked the same as it had yesterday. And yet… every shadow felt deeper. Every flicker of candlelight in the windows felt sharper, more aware.

Agatha shot him a look. “Don’t.”

“I’m just saying—”

“Don’t say anything. Don’t do anything. Don’t go poking around where you don’t belong.”

Billy raised both hands like she’d drawn a knife on him. “Hey, I’m just the queer apprentice with a dramatic flair and a mild tendency toward chaos. You’re the one that’s been pretending that you’re not powerful and part of this for the past, what? Twenty years?”

Agatha turned sharply. “I’m not pretending.”

“Sure you’re not,” Billy said, taking another bite of his cinnamon roll.

Agatha didn’t respond. Because what was there to say?

He wasn’t wrong. She had closed herself off. Sealed the cracks, bricked up the door, thrown away the key. Because when the magic got too strong, it burned. And when you let people in, they left. Or worse—they disappeared. They died.

She looked back down the fog-slick street toward The Hollow. She could still feel it—that vibration in the air, faint but steady. A tuning fork struck deep in her ribs.

Three Threads, once again stitched into the same story.

~*~

The next morning, the bell over the door jingled as Rio stepped inside the apothecary. Agatha didn’t look up immediately, though she could feel her presence as soon as she crossed the threshold. There was that same shift in the air—quiet but undeniable. It made the hairs on the back of her neck prickle.

The woman’s footsteps were tentative but purposeful. Agatha focused on the dried flowers in front of her, trying to ignore the subtle stirring inside her.

“Didn’t think I’d see you here,” Agatha finally said, her voice smooth, almost casual. She didn’t need to look up; she knew it was her. She’d known the moment the door had opened.

“I could use a few things,” Rio said, her tone lighter than it had been the night before, but still tinged with curiosity. “And I figured if I’m going to be here for a while, I should get familiar with the town and stuff.”

Agatha nodded slowly, running her fingers along the jars of herbs behind the counter. “You’ve got to get your bearings when you’re new to town. It’s not the easiest place to settle in.”

Rio let out a small laugh. “Yeah, that’s kind of what I’m figuring out. I thought this would be a quiet escape, but I didn’t know I’d be walking into… well, all this.”

Agatha couldn’t help but glance up at that, her eyes flicking over Rio briefly. “All this?”

“The town, the house… there’s something about this place. I don’t know if it’s the quiet, or just the weirdness of it all, but it feels like… I don’t know. Never mind,” she shrugged with a self deprecating laugh.

Agatha’s gaze lingered, her fingers pausing on the herbs. She knew exactly what Rio was talking about—what they were both talking about, though neither of them had said it directly.

“You might be right,” Agatha said, her voice even, but something flickered in her eyes. “Thistle Grove does have a way of making you feel like that. The town can be… particular about who it lets in.”

Rio’s eyes widened slightly. “First the cottage, and now it’s the town that lets people in or not? Lilia said the same thing yesterday.”

Agatha shifted slightly, the faintest tension in her shoulders. “Lilia likes to spin stories. But there’s some truth to it. The house, especially. It’s not like any normal house.”

A pause. The weight of the unspoken words seemed to hang in the air between them.

Rio finally broke the silence, her voice softer now. “I thought I was just here to get away from everything. To start over.”

“You can try,” Agatha said with a half-shrug. “I hope it works out.”

Rio hesitated for a moment, her eyes still on Agatha, searching for something. Agatha met her gaze evenly, but inside, something was stirring—something she didn’t want to acknowledge. She didn’t want to let herself feel it, especially not now, when everything felt so… uncertain.

“I guess. I’m Rio Vidal, by the way. Didn’t get to introduce myself last night,” Rio said.

Agatha held her gaze a moment longer, then broke it, her fingers resuming their rhythm behind the counter. “I know. It’s nice to meet you.”

Rio’s eyes lingered for a moment longer, then she turned toward the shelves, the brief moment of connection hanging in the air between them. Agatha couldn’t shake the feeling that this wasn’t just an ordinary encounter—there was something more to this woman, to her presence, something she couldn’t explain.

“Anything in particular you’re looking for?” Agatha asked, her voice now more neutral, trying to shift the conversation back to something simple, something normal.

Rio turned back to her, offering a small smile. “Lemon balm. I’m still trying to get used to this place. Figured it’d be good for… everything.”

Agatha raised a brow. “You don’t strike me as someone who needs calming.”

Rio gave a short laugh. “Maybe not. But I could use a little help unwinding.”

A pause. And then, Agatha couldn’t resist. She leaned in slightly, lowering her voice just a touch.

“Maybe you should try lavender instead. Works wonders for a restless mind.”

Rio raised an eyebrow, clearly intrigued but still holding something back. “Lavender, huh?”

“Trust me,” Agatha said, her lips twitching into the faintest of smiles. “It’s one of the few things I know really well.”

The silence stretched between them for a moment as Rio considered the lavender suggestion. Agatha’s gaze flicked back to the shelves, her fingers trailing over the rows of herbs, all while she could feel Rio’s presence lingering like a magnetic pull. The air around them hummed with something almost unspoken, something that Agatha couldn’t shake, no matter how hard she tried to push it down.

“So, what’s this place like? Thistle Grove, I mean,” Rio asked, her voice soft, like she was still testing the waters between them.

Agatha exhaled slowly, leaning back against the counter, trying to look casual even though the tension in the room was palpable. “It’s a small town. People know each other, but they don’t always want to. Most people come here looking for something quiet, but they get more than they bargained for.”

Rio tilted her head, eyeing her curiously. “Like what?”

Agatha hesitated for a moment, considering how much to say. She’d never been one to talk about the strange magic that ran under the town’s skin, under her own. But there was something about Rio—something that made Agatha wonder if maybe, just maybe, it was time to stop hiding.

“You’ll find out soon enough,” Agatha finally said, offering a cryptic smile. “It has a way of catching up with you.”

Before Rio could respond, the bell over the door jingled again, and Agatha’s attention immediately shifted. She didn’t even need to look up; she could feel it—the shift in the air that made her spine tingle, the subtle but unmistakable feeling that the threads were almost whole again.

Wanda.

Her best friend and the one person Agatha had always been able to rely on, even when everything felt impossible. But more than that—Wanda was the third Thread. And one hell of a powerful witch.

The door swung open wider as Wanda stepped into the shop, a gust of wind and the unmistakable scent of lavender trailing behind her. She was laughing as called out a greeting over her shoulder to someone outside, her usual carefree energy filling the space before she even spoke.

“Agatha! I swear you never stock enough of—” Wanda froze the moment she crossed the threshold, her eyes snapping to Rio with a force that was almost too sharp.

“Holy fuck.”

For a moment, the world seemed to still. Rio’s gaze flicked up to Wanda, confusion flickering in her eyes.

Wanda didn’t move, didn’t speak for a heartbeat. Then, slowly, she turned to Agatha, her voice low and filled with something Agatha couldn’t quite name. “You feel it, don’t you?”

“Not here,” Agatha said under her breath, her voice low, but firm. The familiar knot of unease twisted in her stomach, and her fingers tightened around the edge of the counter. “We’ll talk later.”

Wanda’s mouth twisted in annoyance, but she didn’t argue. She wasn’t stupid; she could read the tension in the air. “Fine,” she said, her gaze flicking toward Rio with a subtle edge. “But you know what this means, right?”

Agatha’s gaze hardened. “It means we don’t talk about it here.”

Wanda gave a reluctant nod before turning her attention to Rio, a warm smile plastered across her face as she stepped forward. “Sorry about that. I’m Wanda. Agatha’s best friend—and partner in crime, sometimes.”

Rio raised an eyebrow, still puzzled but polite. “Nice to meet you. I’m Rio.” She glanced at Agatha, who seems to stiffen at the introduction, her mouth pressing into a thin line.

“Rio is Eliza Vidal’s granddaughter,” Agatha explained quickly “She’s staying at the old Vidal house.”

Wanda’s eyes widened for a split second before she recovered, though the look on her face was anything but casual now. Rio caught the brief flash of something—awe, recognition, but mostly disbelief.

“Right,” Wanda says, the smile now strained. “That place has been empty for what, over ten years? Well, I hope it’s treating you well.” She gave Rio a tight-lipped grin, but the expression didn’t quite reach her eyes. “And if you need anything, don’t hesitate to ask.”

“Thanks,” Rio said, giving Wanda a curious look, but Wanda was already backing away, her gaze flicking toward Agatha once more.

Agatha cleared her throat. “You’ll want the lavender balm for relaxation. And tea for sleep.”

Rio nodded, glancing at the jars of herbs on the counter, still a bit unsettled by the entire exchange. Something in the air felt too thick, like a door she hadn’t yet opened.

“Thanks again,” Rio says, her voice a little quieter than before.

Agatha’s gaze lingered on her, studying her with an unreadable expression. There was that feeling again, like something sas pulling at her insides, something just beyond her grasp.

“Take care of yourself,” Agatha said softly, more to herself than Rio. “It’s on the house,” she added when Rio asked how much it was. “Just a welcome to Thistle Grove present.”

Rio nodded and offered a small smile. As she headed out the door, Wanda let out an audible breath, her whole body going rigid once the door closed behind her.

Agatha didn’t even look at her. She was already turning to put away the stuff she used to make the balm for Rio, her fingers moving mechanically, as if the conversation never happened.

But Wanda’s eyes narrowed, and she watched Rio leave with an intensity that makes Agatha’s skin prickle.

“You feel it, don’t you?” Wanda’s voice was a near-whisper. “The Threads. All three of us. Jesus Christ, it feels like my magic is about to burst out of my skin.”

Agatha’s expression hardened  instantly, her jaw setting. She didn’t  answer right away. When she finally does, her voice is cold, low. “It doesn’t matter.”

Wanda scoffed. “It always matters, Agatha. Always.”

Agatha’s hand tightened around the jar of tea, her fingernails biting into the porcelain. She didn't want to deal with this. Not now. Not yet.

And definitely not with Rio Vidal.

Wanda wasn’t so easily dismissed. She stepped closer, her eyes gleaming with intensity, her tone lowering but still pressing. “You can’t just ignore it, Aggie. All three Threads are back in Thistle Grove. After all these years…”

Agatha’s jaw tightened, her back going rigid at the mention of the Threads. Her fingers flexed as if to steady herself, to hold onto something real. Her pulse quickened, but she was careful not to show it. “Wanda,” she said, her voice low and warning. “I’m not doing this here.”

Wanda didn’t relent, though. “You can’t just pretend it’s not happening, not with her already here. The moment I walked in… I felt it. I know you did too.”

Wanda leaned in, her voice quieter now, but laced with urgency. “Do you even know what this means, Agatha? After all these years, the Threads are finally together again. Do you know what that feels like?”

Agatha’s heart started to race, her breath coming a little faster. She could feel it, too—deep in her bones. That pull. But she’d been running from this for decades, burying it, hiding from the magic that once consumed her.

“I said not here,” Agatha hissed through clenched teeth. She was trying, so hard, to keep it together, but the weight of the situation pressed in, heavy on her chest. She couldn’t let it happen. Not now. Not when she’d worked so hard to keep everything buried.

Wanda’s expression softened, but only slightly. She crosses her arms, eyes narrowing, still unwilling to let go. “I don’t get why you’re fighting this. Why are you still holding back? You’ve got so much power, honey. Together, we hold… god, so much power. This is what has been written, what we’ve been waiting for. I didn’t think it’d happen to us, because I thought it’d died with Eliza but—”

Agatha finally met Wanda’s gaze, her expression hard as stone. “I’m not waiting for anything. I’m done with this.”

There was a moment of silence, the air thick with unspoken tension, before Wanda gave a frustrated huff. “Fine,” she said, turning her back on Agatha, though she was not done. Not by a long shot. “But you can’t deny what’s happening. It’s bigger than us. You know that.”

Agatha watched as Wanda made her way to the door. When she turned back, her expression was more serious than before. “I’ll let it go for now, but I’m not going to let you pretend this isn’t happening forever.”

Agatha’s lips curled into a thin, tight smile. “And I’m not going to talk about it. Not yet.”

Wanda opened  her mouth, but Agatha held up a hand to stop her. “Just let it go, Wanda.”

Wanda’s shoulders sagged with frustration, but she nodded. “Fine. But we both know you’re not going to be able to ignore it for long.”

Agatha turned away, her fingers gripping the counter, her mind racing. She’d worked too hard to keep this buried, to keep her magic in check. She didn’t want to be part of any mythical story, she didn’t want to have anything to do with this.

But now, with Rio Vidal in town, everything was changing. Her magic thrummed under her skin.

And Agatha had no idea how to hold it back. Not anymore.

Chapter 3: Three

The first thing Rio noticed when she woke up was the quiet.

Not the oppressive kind, like the sterile hush of her old apartment in LA, but a deeper, more deliberate silence. The kind that wrapped around her like a thick quilt, comforting and just a little strange.

Rio blinked up at the ceiling, still half-tangled in the heavy, floral-print blankets. Morning light filtered through the sheer curtains, dappling the old wood floors with watery gold. The air smelled faintly of something earthy and sweet—cedar and maybe the remnants of the cinnamon tea she’d brewed the night before.

She sat up slowly. Her muscles ached from the travel, and her neck had a crick in it from sleeping in a bed that wasn’t quite broken in—but it was the best rest she’d had in weeks.

The kitchen had been stocked with the basics last night after a quick, slightly surreal trip to the local grocery store. She’d made a simple dinner, read a few pages of a dog-eared book she’d found on the shelf upstairs, and then passed out fully clothed.

Now, everything felt… real. Settled.

Her bare feet hit the creaky floor, and she padded to the window. The vines were still there, curling over the edges of the porch roof like skeletal fingers, but the garden below—wild as it was—looked peaceful in the morning light. Dew glittered on the overgrown grass, and birds chirped somewhere nearby.

Rio wrapped her sweater tighter around herself and wandered into the kitchen.

It still smelled like home.

She hadn’t expected that. Not here. Not in a strange, vine-choked cottage left to her by a grandmother she’d never met. But there was something about the warmth in the wooden counters, the sunlight spilling across the tiled floor, the faint clink of wind chimes outside the back door… It all felt lived in.

Welcoming.

The thought gave her pause.

It hadn’t looked welcoming from the outside—not with the sagging porch and the ivy clinging like a warning. But inside, it was like the house had recognized her.

Nope. Not going there, she told herself, yanking open the fridge and pulling out the cream for her coffee. She was too tired for ghost theories or fairy-tale metaphors. This place was just… old. And weirdly well-kept. Probably by some hidden local caretaker. That made sense.

Mostly.

She carried her mug to the writing desk tucked into the corner of the living room. She’d cleared it off last night—half expecting to find dust, mice, or a long-forgotten taxidermy collection. But instead, there had been a vase of dried lavender and a single, carefully folded note.

Welcome home.

No signature. Just those two words, in faded ink.

Rio had tucked it back in the drawer, trying not to read too much into it. Trying not to feel too much. But now, as she sat down with her coffee and glanced at the empty page in her notebook, something twinged in her chest.

She was here. She had left everything behind—Carol, the city, the apartment filled with all the wrong memories—and now she had this house, this strange town, this fresh page.

Time to start something new, she thought.

After all, she had no deadlines, no expectations. Just a stubborn house, a borrowed kettle, and maybe—if she was lucky—the tiniest flicker of a new beginning.

~*~

She left her mug in the sink, tugged on boots she’d found by the door—well-worn, dusty, just her size—and stepped outside.

The air was cool, damp with dew, and carried the faint scent of earth and pine. Behind the cottage, a narrow path wound its way into the woods. Not much more than a gap in the undergrowth, really, but it called to her. She followed without thinking, like something old and half-remembered was pulling her forward.

Birdsong threaded through the branches overhead, soft and sporadic. The trees here were tall and thick, their bark mottled with moss, their leaves still wet from the morning mist. It felt like stepping into another time—older, quieter, expectant.

She passed a rusted-out wheelbarrow leaning against a tree, half-swallowed by ivy. A broken-down fence. A few flat stones arranged in a circle, their meaning long since forgotten.

Everything about the woods felt lived-in, but not by people.

The deeper she went, the more she noticed it—that odd awareness in the air. Like the trees were watching. Like the path itself was guiding her.

She stopped near a crooked birch, her breath puffing visibly. “Okay,” she said aloud, voice quiet but steady. “I’m officially being weird.”

But the woods didn’t laugh. They just breathed.

A few more steps and she found a small, half-collapsed bench tucked beneath a maple tree. She sat, arms wrapped around her knees, and looked up through the canopy. Light filtered through in broken shafts, illuminating motes of dust that danced like fireflies.

It was the first time in months she felt… still.

Not content, exactly. Not happy. But still. Like something inside her had quit its restless pacing.

After a while, she stood, brushed off her jeans, and turned back toward the cottage. She had things to do. Groceries to unpack. Maybe go into town and see about getting someone to hook her up with Wi-Fi.

Back to civilization.

Or… what passed for it in Thistle Grove.

~*~

The bell above the door chimed softly as she stepped out of the apothecary and into the pale afternoon light. She clutched the small brown paper bag of herbs to her chest, still trying to make sense of what had just happened.

The redhead—Wanda, apparently—had looked at her like she’d just risen from the dead. Eyes wide, mouth half-open, the color draining from her face before rushing back twice as bright. And Agatha—cool, unreadable Agatha—had cut her off with nothing more than a look.

The silence between the two of them had been deafening.

Rio let out a breath and shook her head, boots crunching against gravel as she crossed the street. “Weird as fuck,” she muttered under her breath.

This town. This cottage. These people.

She wasn’t in the mood to go back to the cottage just yet, and her stomach rumbled in agreement. She had enjoyed The Hollow yesterday, and after the morning she’d had, a sandwich and something with caffeine sounded like a decent plan. Maybe she’d even break out her sketchpad, see if anything creative came out of this fog-soaked witch-town vibe.

The Hollow was exactly as she remembered it—quirky signage, crooked windows, and the faint smell of baked bread and something citrusy drifting from inside. The place was quiet when she walked in. No Lilia today, just a teenage girl with a nose ring and dyed blue hair scrolling through her phone behind the counter.

“Seat yourself,” the girl said, without looking up.

Rio nodded, slid into a booth near the window, and dropped her bag onto the bench beside her. She ordered an avocado BLT and a black tea, then pulled out her sketchpad and pencil from her satchel. It had been months since she’d drawn anything that didn’t feel forced or half-dead, but now, something in her fingers itched.

She sketched without really thinking. The curve of a vine creeping across a windowsill. A coffee cup ringed with condensation. The shadow of a woman’s jawline, sharp and elegant, almost accidental.

Her pencil stilled.

She frowned down at the page.

The woman in the sketch had her hair pinned up and a knowing tilt to her mouth. Eyes like a tumultuous ocean.

Agatha.

Rio cursed softly under her breath and flipped to a new page.

Outside, the light was beginning to shift—slanting golden now, pouring in through the window in long, stretched beams. She blinked, glanced at the clock. Late afternoon already. She hadn’t meant to stay this long.

The bell over the door chimed again.

And suddenly, the air changed.

Rio didn’t look up right away. But she felt it—like static across her skin, like the moment before a storm. That same hum from the woods. From the cottage.

She lifted her eyes.

Agatha stood in the doorway, shoulders wrapped in a dark coat, cheeks a little flushed from the cold. Her gaze swept the café, pausing when it found her.

Rio swallowed and looked down at her sketchpad, suddenly aware of how quiet the place had gotten.

Agatha approached with deliberate calm, each step unhurried, boots making a soft tap on the wooden floor.

“You again,” she said when she reached the table.

Rio lifted her chin, tried for casual. “I could say the same.”

A hint of a smirk touched Agatha’s mouth. “You’re bleeding Thistle Grove all over your sketchpad.”

“I guess,” Rio chuckled, self consciously.

Agatha didn’t ask if she could sit—she just did, sliding into the booth across from Rio with the kind of quiet confidence that felt like it belonged to someone who never asked for permission. She folded her coat beside her, dark hair damp at the ends from the mist outside, and leveled her gaze at Rio as if they’d been mid-conversation already.

Rio blinked, pen paused over the edge of her sketchpad. “You don’t strike me as the type to do small talk.”

“I’m not,” Agatha said, voice dry. “But I make exceptions. Occasionally.”

“Lucky me.”

Agatha tilted her head, something like a smile tugging at the edge of her mouth. “You have that look.”

“What look?”

“The one people wear when they don’t know whether to leave or stay. Thought maybe I’d save you the trouble of pretending to be busy with your tea.”

Rio gave a soft huff, leaning back in the booth. “Wow. You’re charming.”

“I’ve been told. Usually not as a compliment.”

Rio snorted despite herself. “So what, you just go around sitting with strangers?”

“You’re not a stranger,” Agatha said. “You’re Eliza Vidal’s granddaughter.”

The statement landed like a stone tossed in still water—soft, but echoing outwards. Rio blinked and dropped her gaze to her tea, fingers tightening slightly around the cup.

“Right. You said that yesterday.” She tried to sound casual. “That’s still weird, by the way. How did you know?”

“This is Thistle Grove,” Agatha said. “We all knew of Eliza Vidal and you look remarkably like her. And people remember things. Especially things that have to do with the Vidal house.”

Rio looked up, narrowing her eyes slightly. “You mean the creaky old house with vines swallowing the porch?”

Agatha gave a shrug. “The one and only.”

Rio studied her, uncertain. “Did you know her?”

“Eliza?” Agatha looked away for a moment, as if weighing the truth. “Yeah.”

“That’s vague.”

“I specialize in vague.”

Rio rolled her eyes and reached for her sketchpad again, tapping the edge with her pen. “Well, I didn’t come here for nostalgia, if that’s what you’re wondering.”

“I wasn’t,” Agatha said evenly. Then, after a pause: “So what did bring you here?”

The question hung in the air a little too long. Rio didn’t answer right away, distracted by the low hum in her chest again—the one that always seemed to thrum a little louder when this woman was near.

She exhaled slowly. “I needed a change.”

“From what?”

“From… everything.” She gestured vaguely. “My girlfriend of four years dumped me a couple of months ago, my publisher is waiting on pages I can’t seem to write, and my apartment has become a temple of old takeout containers and shame.”

Agatha blinked. “That’s… refreshingly honest.”

“I don’t have the energy to pretend otherwise.”

There was a flicker of something in Agatha’s eyes then—recognition, maybe. Or sympathy buried deep beneath years of distance.

“I’m from Portland, originally,” Rio added, quieter now. “Oregon, not Maine. But I’ve lived in LA for a while. I never met Eliza. Never knew she existed. Never even knew the house was mine until some lawyer tracked me down.”

“It’s been empty for a long time,” Agatha said. “Surprised it let you in.”

Rio frowned. “You and Lilia keep saying things like that. Like the house is alive or something.”

Agatha didn’t reply, just wrapped her hands around her tea and took a long sip.

“I mean, yeah, it was unlocked,” Rio muttered. “Maybe weird, but I guess it’s a small town. Maybe someone figured it was okay to leave it unlocked.”

Agatha gave a faint, unreadable smile. “If you say so.”

Rio leaned forward, voice dropping. “Seriously. What’s the deal with this town?”

Agatha’s gaze sharpened just slightly. “You’ll find out soon enough.”

“Cryptic. Again.”

“Better than lying.”

There was silence between them for a moment. Just the soft clink of cutlery and the wind brushing the windows.

Then Agatha asked, more softly this time, “So you’re staying? For a while?”

“I think so.” Rio’s voice was quieter too, almost reluctant. “I don’t have anywhere else to be.”

Agatha nodded. “Then you’ll need firewood. And thicker coats.”

Rio arched a brow. “What are you, the town’s welcome committee?”

Agatha smiled at that—just barely. “Hardly. But I know how unforgiving this place can be when you’re not ready for it.”

Rio’s expression flickered at the sudden sincerity. But before she could say anything else, Agatha was already sliding out of the booth, gathering her coat.

As she stood, she glanced back over her shoulder. “Be careful with the woods.”

Rio stared. “Okay, that’s the second time someone’s said that to me.”

Agatha paused, something unreadable in her gaze. “It’s easy to get lost out here.”

And then she was gone.

~*~

The Hollow’s signal wasn’t perfect, but it was just enough for Rio to place a few online orders before heading back to the cottage. She scrolled through listings of winter jackets and fleece-lined leggings, biting her lip as she added a few things to her cart. Wool socks, a good pair of boots, those ridiculous but probably necessary thermal underlayers.

Thistle Grove clearly hadn’t gotten the memo that she was an LA girl through and through.

She hit “purchase” with a sigh and slid her phone into her coat pocket. Outside, the sky was already starting to shift toward dusk—burnt gold bleeding into gray. On her way home, she stopped at the small hardware store where a sleepy-eyed teenager with a nose ring sold her a bundle of chopped firewood and gave her a crash course on how not to burn the cottage down.

She stacked the wood by the hearth as soon as she got in, then stood there for a moment, staring at the empty fireplace. It had been a long time since she’d needed warmth like this. Real, crackling warmth. The kind you couldn’t switch on with a remote.

The next few days passed in a sort of suspended rhythm. Quiet. Steady. Cold.

She walked into town a few more times, grateful for the fresh air and the excuse to stop by The Hollow again for coffee or soup, even if Agatha didn’t show up. She didn’t go back to the apothecary—not yet—but she thought about it more than she liked to admit. Her new coats arrived. So did the socks. She was, for the first time since arriving, starting to feel… not quite at home, but something close to it.

By Saturday morning, a local technician finally made it up the winding road to the Vidal house, grumbling about signal strength and “weather interference” as he installed a small satellite dish on the side of the house. The moment the Wi-Fi connected, Rio practically kissed her router.

She FaceTimed Alice that afternoon from the kitchen, still bundled in a sweater and thick socks, with a steaming mug of coffee cradled in both hands.

Alice answered on the first ring.

“Oh my god,” she said. “You’re alive. I was two hours away from calling the National Guard!”

Rio smiled sheepishly. “Sorry. It’s been a week.”

“A week?! Rio! You sent me one text! ‘Got here alive, I think. TTYL.’ What does that even mean?”

“It meant I got here alive,” Rio said, sipping her coffee. “And that I would talk to you later.”

Alice stared. “You thought you’d talk to me later. Babe, what if you got murdered by some New England psycho?”

Rio snorted. “No psychos. Just cranky apothecaries and a woman who runs a teashop-slash-diner with zero respect for labeling.”

Alice narrowed her eyes. “Apothecary?”

Rio shrugged. “It’s a weird town. Cute, but weird. And there’s this house, Alice. You should see it. It’s… something.”

“I’ll believe that when you actually show me photos. And videos. You do remember how to send those, right?”

“I had no internet all week. I just got it set up an hour ago. My only contact with civilization was when I went into town for a couple of hours”

Alice gasped. “No internet? For five days? Who even are you?”

“Apparently someone who owns flannel now.”

“Damn. She’s changed.”

Rio laughed, soft and real, and leaned back in her chair. The warmth from the fire still lingered on her skin. “I think I kind of needed the quiet.”

Alice softened on the other end of the screen. “Yeah. I figured you might.”

They talked for another hour—about nothing and everything. About Carol (briefly), about Alice’s newest obsessions (a horror-themed pottery class), and about whether Rio thought she might actually stay in Thistle Grove longer than just a couple of weeks.

Rio didn’t have an answer to that yet.

But she did glance out the window at the snow-dusted trees, at the curling chimney smoke, at the half-finished sketch of the apothecary that sat on the kitchen table beside her coffee.

And for the first time in a long time, she didn’t feel like running.

~*~

By the time the sun dipped behind the trees that Saturday night, the chill had turned sharp. The kind of cold that bit through her coat and settled into her bones, no matter how many layers she wore.

Rio had planned to stay in, maybe sketch a bit more, maybe organize her references for the graphic novel she still couldn’t bring herself to start. But when she looked around the warm, flickering quiet of the cottage, something restless sparked in her chest.

She needed to be somewhere else. Even if it was just for an hour. Even if it was just to remember what people sounded like when they talked to each other over drinks.

The only bar in Thistle Grove was a narrow place on Main Street with a crooked neon sign that read The Silver Mare. She’d passed it earlier in the week, made note of the single dartboard and the dusty jukebox in the corner. It looked like the kind of bar where nothing ever happened.

She tugged on her thickest coat, ran a brush through her hair, and told herself she was just going for one drink. Maybe two. Just enough to feel like herself again.

The place was half full when she walked in. Low lighting. Warm wood. Locals scattered around, most of them bundled in fleece and flannel. A fire crackled in the corner hearth, and a few people looked up, curious, but not unkind.

Rio made her way to the bar and ordered a whiskey neat, the heat of it steady and immediate when it hit her throat. She was halfway through the glass when she heard the door open again behind her.

And then she felt it.

That same strange shift in the air she hadn’t been able to name. Not wind. Not static. Just… something.

She didn’t have to turn around to know who it was.

Agatha Harkness walked in like the cold had nothing on her—black coat fitted close, hair unpinned and falling in dark waves down her shoulders, the bar’s dim lights catching on the edges of her cheekbones. She spotted Rio almost instantly.

Rio blinked, tried to act casual as she turned on her stool. “Fancy meeting you here,” she said, trying for wry.

Agatha’s lips twitched as she stepped closer. “You’re everywhere lately.”

“You’re the one who walked in after me.”

“And yet it feels like fate.”

Rio arched a brow, caught off guard by the flirtation—or maybe just how easily it came. “You come here often?”

Agatha shrugged out of her coat and draped it over the back of a nearby chair. “Sometimes. When I can’t sleep. When the house is too quiet. When Billy’s being insufferable.”

Rio lifted her glass, brow quirking. “Billy?”

Agatha’s lips twitched like she already knew what Rio was wondering. “My one employee. Seventeen. Wears too much eyeliner. Thinks sarcasm is a love language.”

Rio smiled, letting herself relax an inch. “Not your husband, then.”

That earned her a low laugh, quiet but real. “God, no.”

Rio tried not to read into the way her chest loosened at that. “Sounds like a character.”

“He is,” Agatha said fondly, despite herself. “He’s also Wanda’s kid. And my godson.”

“The redhead from your shop?”

“That’s the one.”

Rio hummed, settling deeper into the moment, into the firelight and Agatha’s voice, into the warmth unfurling in her stomach that had nothing to do with the whiskey.

“And you?” Agatha asked, tilting her head. “What brought you here tonight? Restless?”

Rio hesitated, then nodded. “Something like that. Cabin fever, maybe. Or maybe I just wanted to remember what it feels like to be around people who don’t exist on a page.”

Agatha’s expression shifted slightly—interested now. “Writer?”

“Graphic novelist.”

Agatha smiled. “Let me guess—moody loner with a tragic backstory?”

Rio mock-sighed. “You’re frighteningly good at this.”

“Hazard of the trade,” Agatha murmured.

Rio turned more fully toward her. “What trade is that, exactly?”

Agatha paused, her fingers circling her glass of wine. “Let’s call it… the quiet observation of people.”

Rio studied her, thoughtful. “That sounds like a fancy way of saying you’re nosy.”

“That too,” Agatha said, and her smile widened just a fraction.

Rio tilted her glass toward Agatha. “To nosy women, then.”

Agatha raised hers in return, the glint of amusement still dancing in her eyes. “The backbone of every small town.”

They drank, and for a few moments, silence settled between them—not uncomfortable, but laced with something heavier. Something almost anticipatory.

Rio set her glass down, fingertips brushing condensation from the rim. “So what about you? Always lived here?”

Agatha leaned back in her chair, one arm slung casually along the back of it. “Born here. Left when I was eighteen. Came back… a few years later.”

There was something in her voice—an edge dulled by time, but not forgotten.

Rio didn’t press, but she asked gently, “Was it hard? Coming back?”

Agatha’s eyes flicked toward the fire, then back to her. “It was… necessary.”

A pause. Then Agatha countered, “And you? What finally made you come check out the cottage?”

Rio gave a quiet laugh. “Honestly? I didn’t even know it existed until like a week ago.”

Agatha blinked. “Seriously?”

Rio nodded, resting her chin in her hand. “Got this letter out of the blue. Said I inherited a property in some town I’d never heard of, from a grandmother I never knew. Eliza Vidal.”

Agatha’s expression didn’t change much, but her eyes sharpened, just slightly.

“I was going through a breakup,” Rio continued, tracing a droplet of condensation down her glass. “Four years down the drain. She cheated and left me, I imploded, and then this envelope showed up like some kind of cosmic dare. So I packed a suitcase, shut off my phone, and here I am.”

Agatha leaned forward a little, her voice softer now. “And how’s it going so far? Small town life, creaky cottage, weird locals?”

Rio gave her a wry smile. “Honestly? It’s… not what I expected. The cottage looks like it’s been abandoned for decades, but the inside’s kind of cozy. Comfortable. And the town’s definitely weird, but in an offbeat, maybe-I’ll-get-used-to-it way.”

“You sound surprised.”

“I’ve always been a city girl. I guess I thought I’d hate it.” She looked away, briefly. “Or that it’d spit me back out.”

Agatha’s gaze didn’t waver. “But it didn’t.”

“No,” Rio admitted. “It didn’t.”

There was a beat of quiet before Agatha asked, “You planning on staying long?”

Rio opened her mouth to answer—and then paused. “I don’t know yet. I think I just needed to breathe for a while. Figure out what comes next.”

Agatha nodded slowly, like she understood more than she let on. “Well… Thistle Grove’s good for breathing. And for figuring things out.”

Rio tilted her head, watching her. “Speaking from experience?”

Agatha’s mouth curved just slightly. “Something like that,” Then, after a moment, she added, “I’m getting fries. You want anything?”

Rio shrugged. “Fries are good.”

“You learn a lot about a person by how they eat them.”

Rio raised an eyebrow. “And what have you learned about me so far?”

Agatha stood, one brow arching. “Still figuring that out.”

And with that, she turned toward the bar, her coat trailing behind her like ink in water.

Rio watched her go, a little breathless and a little warm, and wondered—not for the first time—just what the hell she had walked into in this strange little town.

~*~

Agatha returned a few minutes later with a basket piled high with golden fries and two small glasses of something amber. She slid one toward Rio without comment and sat down again. Her sleeves were rolled to the elbows, revealing pale forearms dusted faintly with freckles.

Rio reached for a fry. “So. What’s the verdict?”

Agatha popped one into her mouth, chewing thoughtfully. “Not bad. Crisp. Salty. Could be hotter.”

Rio gave her a look. “I meant about me.”

Agatha smirked. “Still too early to say. But you pass the fry test so far.”

Rio leaned back in her chair. “Whew, that’s a relief. I was starting to worry.”

They ate in companionable silence for a few minutes, the sounds of the bar humming softly around them—muted conversation, clinking glasses, a song from the jukebox that was probably older than both of them. Outside, the wind picked up, rattling the windows.

Agatha glanced at her, her voice a little quieter now. “So. You’re a graphic novelist.”

Rio nodded. “Trying to be again. It’s been… hard lately.”

“Writer’s block?”

“Among other things.” She pushed her glass gently in a slow circle on the table. “It’s like the stories are still in there somewhere, but I can’t get to them. Like someone turned off the faucet and walked away with the key.”

Agatha didn’t offer platitudes. Just nodded slowly. “Maybe you don’t need the key.”

Rio arched a brow. “What do you mean?”

“Maybe it’s not about forcing the stories out. Maybe it’s about letting something in.”

There was a beat of quiet. Rio looked at her more closely then, something shifting behind her eyes.

“You’re a strange one, Agatha Harkness.”

“I’ve been called worse,” Agatha replied lightly, but the smile that followed was a real one. Tired at the edges, a little wistful—but real.

They finished the fries, talking around the edges of themselves. Rio learned Agatha had grown up in Thistle Grove but didn’t volunteer much else. She asked if Rio had seen the lake yet and mentioned a bakery that sold the best honey cake in the state. Her voice was low and even, easy to fall into.

By the time they parted ways, the sky outside had gone velvet-dark and the cold had sharpened. Agatha walked her to the door, her hand brushing lightly against Rio’s as she reached for the handle.

“Careful on the roads,” she said. “The ice likes to sneak up on you.”

Rio’s breath fogged in the air as she turned to look at her. “Thanks for the fries. And the mystery advice.”

Agatha’s lips curved. “You’re very welcome.”

And just like that, Rio stepped out into the cold, her heart knocking a little faster in her chest and the stories in her head stirring for the first time in weeks.



Chapter 4: Four

Agatha watched the door swing closed behind Rio, the little brass bell above it giving a single, lonely jingle.

She stood there for a long beat, eyes still fixed on the empty space where Rio had just been. The air still held her warmth. That strange tug she’d been trying to ignore all week was louder now, humming just under her skin.

It wasn’t just that Rio was beautiful. (She was— aggravatingly so, in that sun-warmed, California way that Agatha couldn’t help but be incredibly attracted to.) It was something else. Something deeper. A sense of being recognized. Of being seen. And that was what unsettled her.

She shook it off, retreating to her booth and picking up her glass. The drink was too sweet, but she sipped it anyway. She leaned back in the booth, exhaling slow and steady. Wanda had been right about one thing—there was no denying that the Threads were stirring again. She felt it in her bones, that old, dangerous magic, curling up from the depths like smoke through floorboards.

She had buried it for nearly two decades. Packed it down like ash, sealed the cracks. And now—

Now a woman with warm hazel eyes and a sketchpad had walked into her town, into Eliza’s house, and without even knowing it, cracked everything open again.

Agatha closed her eyes for a moment, trying to will the world quiet. But it wasn’t quiet. Not anymore.

The Thread in her was humming.

And Agatha Harkness, for the first time in twenty years, didn’t know what came next.

~*~

The house Agatha lived in sat on the northern edge of Thistle Grove, tucked into a thicket of bare-limbed trees and ivy-covered stone. It wasn’t large, but it was old, older than anyone remembered, and it creaked with history in the same way she did—soft, constant, and just under the surface.

The morning was overcast, the kind of gray that made everything look muted and tired. Agatha didn’t light the lamps. She liked the way the light sifted through the cloudy windows today, low and dim. Quiet.

She stood in the living room, her fingers curled around a chipped ceramic mug. The tea inside had gone cold, untouched.

On the mantle above the old fireplace, tucked between bundles of dried herbs and a few odd talismans, sat a small silver-framed photo. A boy with dark curls and laughing eyes, mid-spin in a blur of motion, his shirt half-untucked like he’d never stayed still long enough for anyone to fix it.

Nicky.

Today would have been his eighteenth birthday.

Agatha sat slowly on the worn velvet settee across from the hearth, knees stiff, breath tight in her chest. She’d woken with the ache already inside her, a hollow, bruised place she tried not to touch most days. But not today. Today it howled. Today it felt like a gaping, bleeding wound.

She let her head fall back against the cushion and closed her eyes.

He would’ve been tall by now. His warm brown eyes would probably be the same, though. Always so full of mischief, so full of love. 

He would’ve had too much magic in his blood, too much curiosity for his own good. She would’ve taught him everything—how to ground himself, how to weave intention into spellwork, how to build protections so strong no one could hurt him.

But that wasn’t the story the world had written for her. How unfair was it that the one person she needed to heal, she couldn’t? What good was it to have a healing power when it wouldn’t work on your own flesh and blood?

She’d buried him on a bitter winter morning, the earth too hard and the sky too quiet. Wanda had held her up when her knees gave out. Lilia had come every night for weeks with broth and silence. And Agatha had decided—right there, right then—that she would never let herself love anything like that again.
