Her mother had vanished when Agatha was just a girl. No body. No answers. Just a trail of ash and whispers. And then Nicky, stolen from her before he’d even had a chance to become the person he was meant to be.

She had lost too much to ever risk that again.

Even now, after all these years, the weight of it made her feel like she couldn’t breathe.

She glanced toward the window.

Somewhere out there, Rio Vidal was walking around with curious eyes and a stubborn streak a mile wide. Drawing in that sketchbook like she could capture the world with charcoal and paper. Stirring things Agatha had worked hard to bury.

No. She couldn’t afford to let anyone in. Especially not someone like her.

Not when the magic was waking up again.

Not when the Threads were calling.

And definitely not when Agatha still hadn’t forgiven the world for taking everything she loved.

~*~

The care package arrived just after noon.

Agatha didn’t hear anyone at the door—Wanda never knocked—but when she wandered back into the front room, there it was on the stoop. A brown paper bag folded neatly at the top, sealed with a red wax stamp. Wanda’s seal. The flaming phoenix.

Agatha sighed, her chest tightening. She picked it up gently, the scent of rosemary and sweet clove already wafting through the paper. Inside, she found a small container of butternut squash soup—Wanda’s best—still warm to the touch, a crusty roll wrapped in a cloth napkin, and a tin of cinnamon shortbread cookies, hand-cut into little stars. Tucked between the napkin and the tin was a note.

Thinking of you. If you want company today, you don’t have to ask. I’ll come running. But if you want quiet, I get it. You know where to find me.

Love always,

W

Agatha’s throat closed up. She set the bag on the counter without opening anything. She wasn’t ready to eat. Wasn’t ready to sit and feel Wanda’s kindness seep into the cracks of her armor.

Not today.

She changed into her thick gray sweater—the one Nicky used to burrow into when he was small—and pulled on a long black coat, a pair of gloves, and a scarf she didn’t remember knitting but had kept anyway. Her boots crunched on the frost-tipped leaves as she stepped out into the stillness.

The walk behind the Vidal cottage was mostly forest, quiet and untouched. A narrow path wound down to the lake, barely wide enough for one person, flanked by ferns and gnarled roots. Agatha had been coming here for decades—since long before Eliza Vidal had passed. Eliza had never minded. In fact, she’d encouraged it.

“You’re the only Harkness who doesn’t scare the trees,” Eliza had said once with a wink, pouring Agatha a cup of something herbal and sharp. “That lake knows you.”

Maybe it did. It was the only place in Thistle Grove that had ever felt like it knew her.

The water shimmered in the low light, dark and glassy. A rim of ice had started forming along the edge. Agatha walked to the old dock, its wooden slats slick with frost, and lowered herself to sit at the end of it, legs crossed beneath her.

No spells today.

No grief rituals or burning herbs.

Just silence.

Just her.

The wind stirred the trees gently, and the lake rippled in response. Somewhere across the water, a bird cried out once and then went quiet again.

Agatha closed her eyes.

“I miss you, baby,” she whispered. Her voice barely broke the air.

It had been twelve years since Nicky died. And yet, on days like this, the pain was fresh and sharp. Like he’d only just left. Like she’d find his toy broomstick leaning against the wall, or his little hand in hers as they walked through town on a cold day like this.

He would’ve been so brilliant. So full of life and questions. A powerful witch, no doubt. A good one, if she’d done her job right.

But she hadn’t been able to protect him. Not from illness. Not from the magic that ran too hot in his blood too young.

Agatha tilted her head back, staring up at the pale, colorless sky. The ache in her chest burned like coals.

She didn’t need comfort. She didn’t need soup or cookies or Wanda’s fierce, smothering love.

She needed this.

Stillness. Solitude. The lake. The memory of a laugh now long gone.

~*~

Agatha felt her before she saw her.

Soft, uncertain footsteps—hesitant on the frost-slick path—and for a moment she thought that maybe it was Wanda after all, deciding solitude be damned. But then a voice, light and a little winded, broke the quiet, confirming what she already knew deep inside.

“Wasn’t sure if I needed to chase someone off,” Rio said. “Saw a lone figure sitting on my dock and figured I should come investigate.”

Agatha stiffened, swiping quickly at her cheeks with the backs of her gloved hands. Too late. She knew the shimmer in her eyes hadn’t gone unnoticed.

She turned her head just enough to see Rio stepping onto the dock, hands in the pockets of a worn coat, cheeks flushed from the cold. There was no mockery in her expression. No smugness in the I-caught-you look Agatha had expected.

Only concern. And softness.

“I’m sorry,” Rio said quickly, stopping a few paces away. “I didn’t mean to intrude.”

Agatha looked back out at the lake, breath misting in front of her. “You’re not. I’m the one trespassing.”

Rio stepped closer, cautiously lowering herself onto the edge of the dock beside her. The wood creaked, but held. “Didn’t realize this place had such a view.”

“It’s always been one of the best parts of the property,” Agatha murmured. Her voice sounded hollow, even to her own ears. “Eliza used to say the lake kept secrets better than anyone else in town.”

Rio was quiet for a moment, just breathing. Then: “You okay?”

Agatha hesitated. Lying was easier. Always had been.

But something about the question—simple, not prying—got under her ribs.

“I’m fine,” she said finally.

Rio tilted her head, studying her profile. “You don’t look fine.”

Agatha huffed a breath. Not quite a laugh, not quite a sigh. “Don’t you have internet now? Things to do, people to text?”

Rio gave her a look. “You’re deflecting.”

“And you’re prying.”

“I’m… observing,” Rio corrected gently, then glanced out at the water. “Didn’t mean to interrupt anything. It just—felt weird seeing someone out here. This still.”

Agatha let the silence stretch between them. The lake lapped quietly against the shore, ice ringing gently against rock.

“My son would’ve been eighteen today,” she said, so softly she almost didn’t hear herself.

Rio’s head turned toward her sharply, her expression folding into something stunned and stricken. “Oh,” she said. Just that one word, small and reverent. “Agatha, I’m—”

“Don’t,” Agatha cut in, voice sharper than intended. But then she sighed and softened. “I don’t talk about him. Hardly ever. No one really asks anymore.”

Rio didn’t press. She just reached into her coat pocket, pulled out a small, crumpled packet of tissues, and set it beside her on the dock.

“I brought these because my nose always runs in the cold,” she said, not looking at her. “Not… because I expected this.”

Agatha stared at the offering for a moment before picking one out and dabbing gently at her eyes. “Thanks.”

They sat quietly again, the winter wind brushing over them, gentle and respectful.

After a while, Rio asked, “Do you want to tell me about him?”

Agatha was surprised to find herself nodding. “His name was Nicky. Nicholas.”

Rio smiled, just a little. “That’s a good name.”

Agatha looked out at the lake again. “Yeah. It really was,” she sighed, and was silent for a few beats. “He was mine,” she said, voice quiet. “My son.”

Rio didn’t speak right away, just shifted to sit beside her, close but not crowding. She waited, the kind of patience that felt like permission.

“He was six when he died.” Agatha’s words were precise, controlled—like saying it without emotion would make it less real. “It was… sudden. An illness that took too much too fast. One day he was running around barefoot in the garden, chasing bees. The next—”

She stopped. A breath caught in her throat. She hated this part. Hated that it still unraveled her no matter how many times she tried to fold it away.

“I’m so sorry,” Rio said, and the warmth in her voice undid something in Agatha. “That’s… there’s nothing I can say that makes that better. But I’m here.”

Agatha swallowed and looked away, but not before she caught something in Rio’s eyes. Not pity. Recognition. Loss, too. Maybe not the same shape, but it echoed.

“Eliza helped me with him, back then,” Agatha said after a beat, her voice softer now. “She was… kind. Stern as hell, but kind. Nicky adored her. She used to bring him jars of wildflower honey and make him little charms to keep nightmares away.”

Rio smiled faintly.  

“She meant a lot to this town, you know?” Agatha said. “And to me.”

A breeze stirred the lake, and with it, the faint scent of jasmine and lavender—impossible, Agatha thought, in November. She turned her head.

The dock was framed by tiny blossoms, peeking up through frost-laced grass. Wildflowers, pale and delicate, blooming as if they’d forgotten the season.

Agatha stilled.

Rio didn’t seem to notice. She was smiling softly, tucking her hair behind her ear as she looked out over the lake.

“Strange,” she murmured. “I don’t remember there being flowers here yesterday.”

Agatha stared at her. The way the light curved toward her. The way the earth responded to her joy.

No. Not strange. Not at all.

“I should go,” Agatha said, carefully neutral, her chest feeling tight all of a sudden. “It’s getting cold.”

Rio blinked, startled. “Right, yeah. Of course.”

But her expression shifted as she studied Agatha’s face. “Hey, thank you. For telling me about Nicky.”

Agatha nodded once. “Thanks for listening.”

Rio stood, and turned toward Agatha with a soft but confident smile. She extended her hand, fingers curling slightly as if offering something delicate, like an invitation to step into something warmer.

“Need a hand?” she asked, her voice quiet, but with an edge of playfulness.

Agatha’s breath caught. For a split second, she considered refusing. She was fine, she didn’t need anyone’s help. But then Rio’s eyes—the ones that had softened in that way she couldn’t quite explain—locked with hers, and something in Agatha relented.

She reached out, just as Rio’s fingers brushed against hers.

The moment their hands met, a strange warmth bloomed inside her, deep and untapped. It was as though something had sparked to life in the air around them. And then, in a surreal cascade, the ground beneath them—frosted and barren just moments ago—suddenly stirred with movement.

In a sudden burst, tiny white flowers bloomed all around them, as if the earth had been waiting for this touch to awaken it. A soft, glowing burst of color stretched over the ground, delicate petals unfurling in the cold air, a brilliant contrast to the otherwise muted winter landscape.

Agatha froze, her breath hitching.

Rio’s eyes widened, watching as the flowers bloomed in a rapid, almost unnatural fashion, their petals white as snow, glistening with the fresh promise of spring.

“What the hell…?” Agatha murmured, staring at the ground as the flowers spread like an enchanted carpet beneath them.

Rio blinked, her hand still resting in Agatha’s. She glanced down at the flowers, then up at Agatha, as if trying to gauge her reaction.

“What the hell just happened?” Rio looked around incredulously.

Agatha was still staring at the flowers, her pulse quickening in her veins. She’d seen magic before, but this… this wasn’t ordinary. The way the world seemed to respond to Rio—this wasn’t normal.

“I, uh,” Agatha started, not quite knowing what to say. “I don’t know.”

Rio seemed to notice the shift in Agatha’s tone, and she glanced at her more closely. “You sure about that?”

Agatha’s thoughts swirled with an unsettling clarity. Could it be…? No. That wasn’t possible. Was it?

She let out a breath, shaking her head. “Never mind,” she muttered. “Coffee?”

Rio smiled, though the curiosity in her eyes lingered, clearly unsettled by the sudden change of subject and how it reflected in Agatha’s demeanor.

“Yeah. Let’s get coffee.”

Agatha followed Rio inside, blinking against the warmth of the little cottage. The scent of cinnamon and cedar clung to the air—homey and unexpectedly comforting. She hadn’t stepped foot inside this house since Eliza’s memorial. It looked different now, of course. Lived in again. With Rio’s own flare to it. But something about it still felt untouched, preserved beneath the surface.

“Go ahead and make yourself at home,” Rio said, shrugging off her coat and hanging it by the door. “I’ll get the coffee going. I finally figured out the ancient machine yesterday.”

Agatha let out a faint breath of amusement and made her way to the small kitchen table by the window. She ran a hand over the back of the wooden chair before settling into it. The cottage was small, yes, but cozy in a way that made her chest ache. It wasn’t just the memories—it was something else, something new.

The kettle began to rumble on the stove as Rio moved around the kitchen with a kind of distracted grace. She poured the hot water into a French press and pressed it down slowly, carefully, before bringing over two mismatched mugs. She set one in front of Agatha, then slid into the seat across from her.

“Careful, it’s hot,” Rio said, wrapping her hands around her own mug. “And probably too strong. I never said I was good at this.”

Agatha curled her hands around the warm ceramic, letting the heat soak into her fingers. “Strong is fine,” she murmured, watching Rio over the rim of her cup.

They sat in silence for a beat. The wind murmured outside, brushing against the windows, and Rio’s coffee cup made a soft tap as she set it down on the table between them.

Then, with quiet steadiness, Rio leaned forward, elbows on her knees, hands wrapped loosely around the warm mug. “So,” she said, her voice low, “are you going to tell me what that was?”

Agatha’s gaze stayed on her coffee. She didn’t answer right away. Just took a sip, as if the heat might settle the sudden churn in her chest. It didn’t.

“I don’t know,” she said finally. Her voice was soft, but careful. “Not everything has an easy explanation.”

Rio raised an eyebrow. “But it has one, though.”

Agatha glanced at her, surprised by the calm in her tone. There was no pressure, no suspicion—just curiosity. Maybe even something like patience.

“It wasn’t supposed to happen,” Agatha said after a long pause. “The flowers.”

“Yeah,” Rio said, glancing out the window. Frost still rimmed the glass, but outside, the garden shimmered faintly with color. “Kinda figured wildflowers don’t usually show up after the first snow.”

Agatha let out a quiet laugh—dry and faintly bitter. “No. They don’t.”

They fell quiet again. The space between them felt charged now. Not tense, exactly—but full. Like something unsaid was hanging just above the table, waiting.

Rio looked back at her. “Look, I know today is a hard day for you. I don’t need you to explain it. Not right now. But I know you know more than you’re letting on and I want to know what the hell that was,” She paused, then added, “But I just… I saw you out there. You looked like you were carrying something heavy. I just… wanted to make sure you were okay.”

Agatha’s fingers tightened around her mug. Her throat felt tight again. “You weren’t intruding,” she said before she could think better of it.

Rio’s smile came slow and lopsided. “Good,” she said. 

Agatha looked up at her—really looked. The tousled curls, the little smudge of ink on her thumb, the quiet steadiness in her eyes. She hadn’t expected any of this. Not the garden. Not the warmth. Not Rio. Not such powerful, ancient magic tangled with hers.

“Thanks for the coffee,” she said. And though it was simple, the words landed heavy. Meant more than they should.

Rio didn’t answer right away. She just reached across the table and set her hand gently over Agatha’s.

It was a small thing. Light pressure. Warm skin.

But the second their palms met, something moved. A soft hum in Agatha’s chest, like a chord being struck. Outside, the frost-bitten garden brightened again—color breaking through the snow-dusted ground. Tiny blossoms unfurled, reaching for the gray light.

And then a beautiful white daisy appeared on the coffee table in front of them.

Agatha froze. Breath caught.

Rio’s hand stayed steady in hers, thumb brushing lightly against her knuckles.

Neither of them said a word. They didn’t need to.

Agatha’s gaze flicked to the window, heart hammering. It was beautiful. Too beautiful. And too familiar in a way that shook her.

She’d buried this. Locked it away.

And now here it was—breaking through the frost, wild and impossible.

Rio’s eyes stayed fixed on the coffee table, on the impossible flower that just appeared there, her lips parted just slightly. “This is…” she whispered, then blinked, stunned. “This is impossible.”

Agatha didn’t move. Her hand still rested under Rio’s, but her body had gone very still.

Tiny blue blossoms trembled in the cold breeze outside, petals delicate and defiant against the frost. And Rio looked at them like they were a miracle.

Agatha couldn’t take it.

She pulled her hand back gently, stood too quickly, the chair scraping against the floor. “I—I need to go.”

Rio turned, startled. “Wait—what?”

Agatha shook her head, already moving toward the door, the walls feeling too close, her skin too tight. Her magic buzzed just beneath the surface, wild and unchecked, like it was trying to claw its way out.

“I just—thank you. For the coffee,” she said again, but this time her voice was strained. Hollow.

Rio stood too, confused and concerned. “Agatha, hey, wait. What’s going on? Is it something I did?”

“No,” Agatha said sharply, too quickly. Then, softer, “No. It’s not you. It’s me. I just… I can’t—”

She stopped herself before she said too much.

Before she said I can’t fall into this again. I can’t feel this much again.

Outside, another blossom pushed through the frost. A dandelion this time. Bold. Unapologetic.

She couldn’t bear it.

Agatha opened the door and stepped into the cold, barely feeling it. The wind cut through her coat, but it didn’t matter. The magic in her veins was louder than her thoughts, running wild under her skin.

She didn’t look back.

But Rio stood at the threshold, heart pounding, watching her go—and wondering what the hell had just bloomed between them.

 

Chapter 5: Five

Chapter Notes

Thank you so much for your lovely comments. I'm so glad to see you guys are excited to read this. I know things are still vague and cryptic, but please bare with me while I build this world. There's a whole lot of mythology to introduce and we'll get to it slowly, I promise.

The door clicked shut behind her, and the warmth she’d brought with her seemed to vanish just as quickly.

Rio stood there for a moment, unmoving, coffee mug in hand and heart thudding a little too hard in her chest. Her eyes drifted to the window, where the faintest outlines of flowers still shimmered in the frost-edged glass, their petals soft and defiantly alive against the November cold.

What the hell had that been?

She’d seen weird things before—living in LA meant running into the occasional eccentric psychic or auras reader who swore her energy was “rare”—but this?

This was something else entirely.

It had started the moment they touched. The warmth, the spark, that strange hum in her bones like the world had briefly gone into tune around her.

Was it Agatha?

No, she didn’t think so. Not just her.

Something had stirred in Rio too. Something deep and strange and inexplicably real.

She ran a hand through her hair and sighed, setting her mug down. “I’ve officially lost it,” she muttered.

But she didn’t feel lost. If anything, she felt sharper than she had in months.

Alive .

~*~

The days bled together in a quiet rhythm—soft light through lace curtains, the scratch of pencil on paper, the slow return of something Rio hadn’t felt in months: inspiration. It crept in gradually, like a shy animal testing the air, but it was there. Real. Tangible.

By Friday, her fingers itched if she went too long without a pencil in hand. Her sketchbook was starting to fill again—not with polished panels or tight layouts, but raw, alive sketches. Character faces. Dreamlike landscapes. A girl standing at the edge of a lake with flowers blooming around her feet.

She didn’t have to ask who the girl was.

~*~

It was early afternoon when she ducked into The Hollow again, drawn by the promise of warmth and the ever-elusive lure of decent Wi-Fi and some hot tea. The little diner was quieter than usual. A few locals lingered over mugs of something strong and sweet, but none paid her any mind.

She slid into her usual corner booth and ordered a cinnamon tea from the disinterested teenage server who’d clearly rather be anywhere else. Then she opened her sketchbook and fell in.

Time passed. She didn’t notice the light starting to fade, didn’t notice the hush deepening in the diner—until the bell above the door rang.

She glanced up automatically.

And there she was again.

Agatha Harkness, wrapped in a deep charcoal coat that suited her perfectly, the wind still clinging to her hair like it didn’t want to let go. Beside her was a tall teen with smudged eyeliner, a punk band tee, and a mouth already mid-rant about something that had probably started ten minutes before they walked in.

Agatha was murmuring something to him—probably a well-placed jab if her expression was anything to go by—but the teen didn’t seem fazed.

He was halfway through another sentence when his eyes landed on Rio.

And he stopped cold.

“Oh my god,” he said, pointing right at her, eyes wide. “No way. You’re her.”

Rio blinked. “Me?”

The kid practically bounded over, starstruck. “You’re Rio Vidal. Like, the Rio Vidal. Creator of Ink & Thorn, Orbit City, and don’t even get me started on Bitter Honey—do you understand what that book did to me in junior high? I cried. Like, actual sobbing. In the library. People thought someone had died.”

Rio laughed, startled and genuinely flattered. “I… wow. Thank you. That’s really sweet.”

“I have all your books. Hardcover. Signed editions when I could find them. My mom thinks I have a shrine, and honestly, she’s not wrong.”

Agatha, already at the counter, let out a low sigh. “Billy, breathe.”

Billy waved her off, still beaming at Rio like she’d descended from the heavens.

“You saved me. No exaggeration. I was, like, thirteen, gay, miserable. And then I found your work and—boom. There was someone out there who got it. Who didn’t make queer stories about suffering or tragedy, but about magic and love and messy, gorgeous life. You gave me something. I don’t know what my teens would’ve been like if I hadn’t found your work.”

Rio flushed, caught between embarrassment and the kind of gratitude that leaves you a little breathless. “Wow,” she said again. “I don’t know what to say. I think that’s one of the nicest things anyone’s ever said to me.”

Agatha returned then, two mugs in hand. “He’s still a teenager, in case you couldn’t tell.”

Billy stuck his tongue out at her but didn’t miss a beat. “He’s a teenager who’s just met his idol. Show some respect, Aunt Agatha.”

Rio bit back a grin and looked up at her. “You two always like this?”

“Unfortunately,” Agatha said, her tone dry but not unkind.

“We live to torment each other,” Billy said proudly. “It’s a sacred pact.”

Rio took a sip of her tea, the warmth blooming through her chest as she looked between them.

For a moment, she felt… anchored. Like she’d stumbled into something real in this strange little town. Something unexpected. Something—she wasn’t sure yet. But it was good.

Her eyes met Agatha’s across the table, and something unspoken passed between them. Not quite a smile, but something warmer. Softer.

Familiar.

Rio leaned back a little, pushing her sketchbook aside just enough that it caught Billy’s eye again. His gasp was so dramatic she nearly dropped her tea.

“Is that your current work? Oh my god—may I?”

Rio chuckled. “Be my guest.” She slid the sketchbook toward him. “It’s mostly messy stuff. Just warming up.”

Billy sat without waiting to be asked, flipping through the pages like they were sacred texts. “Messy? Girl, this is divine . Look at the linework—do you see this? Aunt Agatha, look at this.”

Agatha raised a brow but lowered herself gracefully into the booth beside Billy, her own mug cradled in her hands. “I’ve seen her work,” she said, giving Rio a sly look. “But I suppose I wouldn’t mind another peek.”

Rio felt heat rise in her cheeks, caught somewhere between pleased and self-conscious. It was one thing to have a teenage fanboy over you—it was another to have Agatha Harkness, who carried herself like a secret wrapped in velvet, staring down at her drawings like they held something meaningful. Especially after everything that had happened the last time they’d seen each other.

Billy flipped to the lake sketch. The one with the two figures on the dock—one seated, hunched, a shadow of grief wrapped around her; the other standing just behind her, hand outstretched, offering something unseen.

“Oh,” he said, quieter now. “This one…”

Agatha was quiet too. Rio watched as her eyes settled on the page and didn’t move.

Rio cleared her throat. “That was just… a moment that stuck with me. From earlier this week.”

“You’ve got a good eye for people,” Billy murmured. “The way you catch emotion in stillness… I don’t even have words. It’s like—like your characters breathe.”

Agatha’s gaze slid from the sketchbook to Rio, thoughtful. “Your creative block seems to be well and truly broken.”

Rio shrugged, but there was a smile tugging at the corners of her mouth. “Yeah, I guess so. It’s weird. Ever since I got here, it’s like… something cracked open. Not all at once. But I feel like I’m finally remembering what it feels like to want to create again.”

Agatha tilted her head slightly, her voice softer than Rio had expected. “Maybe this place is exactly where you’re meant to be right now.”

Rio met her gaze and something in her chest pulled tight.

It would’ve been easy to dismiss the words as small talk. Polite encouragement. But there was a weight behind them. Like Agatha had felt it too—that strange, invisible thread weaving between them since the moment they met.

Billy was still flipping through her pages in reverent silence, and for a little while, the three of them just sat there—warm tea, warm light, and the soft scratch of pages turning.

Rio looked around the diner, at the sleepy golden afternoon pouring through the windows. The world beyond the glass was cold and brittle, late November settling deep into the bones of the town. But here, inside this booth, with Billy’s laughter and Agatha’s quiet eyes on her sketchbook, it almost felt like spring again.

~*~

The conversation over tea had drifted from art to books to the quirks of small-town life, and Rio found herself laughing more than she had in weeks. Billy was funny and endearing, and Agatha—well, Agatha didn’t say much, but when she did, her dry wit landed with perfect timing, and her eyes sparkled like she was far more entertained than she let on.

The tea had long gone cold in Rio’s mug by the time she checked her phone and blinked at the hour.

“Shit,” she muttered. “It’s already seven?”

Billy’s eyes widened in mock horror. “Time flies when you’re drinking Lilia’s suspiciously strong black currant brew.”

Rio smiled, standing to gather her sketchbook and coat. “I should head back before it gets any darker. My boots are great for wandering town, not so much for stomping through icy leaves in the dark”

Billy stretched and slung his scarf over one shoulder. “I’m off too. I’ve got a thing.”

Agatha arched an eyebrow. “A ‘thing’?”

He gave her a sly grin. “A date, maybe. Or possibly just someone who appreciates eyeliner and sarcasm. Either way, I’m intrigued.”

Agatha shook her head but said nothing, though there was the faintest tug of a smile on her lips as she watched him go.

When the door jingled closed behind him, the silence between them settled differently—heavier, maybe, but not uncomfortable. Rio hesitated, her coat slung over her arm, unsure if she should bolt or stay.

Agatha broke the quiet first. “He adores you, you know.”

Rio glanced toward the door, bemused. “Billy?”

A soft huff of amusement. “He doesn’t like many people. Especially not enough to sit still and drink tea with them for over an hour.”

“High praise,” Rio murmured, then added, “He’s great. I can see why you keep him around.”

Agatha shrugged. “He keeps showing up. I haven’t found a way to stop him yet.”

That made Rio laugh again, the sound surprising even to her. When she looked back at Agatha, the other woman was already watching her. The firelight made her features softer, gentler, and yet that same quiet gravity lingered in her eyes.

Rio cleared her throat, nerves fluttering in her chest. “Well, I should go. Thanks for the tea and the company.”

Agatha nodded. “Be careful on your drive back. The frost’s already setting in.”

“I’ll manage.” But as she reached for the door, she paused. “Agatha?”

“Hm?”

Rio turned slightly. “It was… really good seeing you again.”

Agatha tilted her head just a bit. “You say that like you weren’t planning on it,” she teased.

Rio’s lips curved. “I wasn’t sure if you were the type who planned things.”

Agatha leaned back in her chair, fingers still curled around her teacup. “I’m not.”

Their eyes met again. Something passed between them—unspoken but not unfelt.

Rio gave a small nod, then pushed out into the chill, the warmth of the diner clinging to her skin like a memory.

Rio paused just outside the door, hand resting on the old wooden frame as she glanced up at the sky. The light had shifted while they’d been inside—what had been soft overcast now darkened to something more ominous. Heavy clouds rolled low across the sky, thick and bruised-looking, like they were debating a downpour or worse.

She turned halfway, looking back into the diner where Agatha still sat, the soft amber glow of the table lamp catching in the sharp lines of her profile. She looked thoughtful—or maybe just tired—and Rio hesitated, her hand tightening around her keys.

“Hey,” she said, stepping back in. “It’s looking like it might storm.”

Agatha glanced out the window, her brow lifting slightly as she noted the sky. “Mm. Wouldn’t be the first time the weather’s turned without warning.”

“I could give you a ride,” Rio offered, trying to make it sound casual. “I mean, if you’re walking.”

There was a flicker of something unreadable across Agatha’s face. Surprise, maybe. Or suspicion softened by something gentler. She studied Rio for a long second, like she was trying to see the motive under the words.

“You don’t have to,” she said quietly.

“I know,” Rio replied, just as soft. “But I want to.”

Another beat of silence stretched between them. Then, finally, Agatha stood, slow and deliberate. She pulled on her coat, buttoning it at the collar, and met Rio’s gaze with a hint of a wry smile.

“Well. If I get struck by lightning, I suppose I’d rather it be in your company.”

Rio grinned. “Wow, high praise again. Twice in one evening.”

Agatha rolled her eyes as she stepped past her, the door swinging open with a soft creak. “You’re starting to sound like Billy.”

“I’ll take that as a compliment.”

Outside, the wind had picked up, sharp and damp with the promise of rain. Rio unlocked her car, and for a fleeting moment as Agatha slid into the passenger seat, it felt oddly like something… normal. Not charged with magic or heavy with grief or mystery.

Just two women, trying to get out of the storm.

And for a little while, that was enough.

~*~

They turned off the main road, the cottage-lined stretch growing narrower and darker as the trees thickened. The storm was creeping in fast now—wind whistling through the leaves, rain beginning to speckle more steadily against the windshield.

Rio stole a glance at Agatha, who hadn’t said much since they’d left the diner. She sat with one hand resting on the window ledge, fingers occasionally twitching, like she was thinking hard but trying not to show it. Her face, usually so composed, looked softer in the quiet—tired maybe, or thoughtful.

“Are you always this broody after tea?” Rio asked lightly.

Agatha let out a small huff of a laugh, the kind that barely escaped her lips. “Only when I’ve had too much honey.”

Rio smiled at that, but didn’t press. There was something fragile about the moment—delicate, like glass that hadn’t shattered yet but could if touched too firmly.

They pulled up to Agatha’s place a minute later: a dark, ivy-covered home set back from the road with a wrought-iron gate and flickering lanterns casting long shadows across the porch. It looked like something out of an old storybook, regal and a little lonely.

Agatha moved to unbuckle her seatbelt but paused, her hand still on the strap.

“You didn’t have to do this,” she said softly. “Drive me, I mean.”

Rio looked at her, something warm stirring behind her ribs. “It was no trouble. And I wanted to. I kinda like you, Harkness.”

Agatha’s lips curved into something almost-smile. She opened the door, rain brushing her face as the wind tugged at her coat.

She hesitated. Just long enough that Rio noticed.

Then, softly—almost too quiet to hear over the rain—Agatha said, “Be careful with this place, Rio.”

Rio frowned, turning in her seat. “You’ve said that before. What does that mean?”

Agatha looked back at her, eyes unreadable in the dark.

“It means Thistle Grove gives you what you need… but it rarely asks if you’re ready for it.”

And with that, she stepped out into the night, pulling the door shut behind her.

Rio sat there a moment longer, watching the curve of Agatha’s back as she walked up the stone path, dark curls whipped by the wind. A light flicked on inside the house just as the rain began to fall harder, steady now, like a quiet percussion against the car roof.

That strange ache behind her ribs tightened, just for a second.

Then she turned the car around, heading back through the winding road toward her own cottage.

She didn’t know what she was getting herself into here.

But she was starting to suspect Agatha Harkness wasn’t just a local with sharp eyes and sharper words.

She might’ve been a storm in disguise.

~*~

Rio replayed Agatha’s words over and over as she drove, windshield wipers working overtime against the curtain of rain. Be careful with this place, Rio.

The hell was that supposed to mean?

She turned the question around in her head, prodding it like a sore tooth. Was it about Thistle Grove in general? The weird vibe, the kooky and weirdly specific shopkeepers, the flowers that bloomed out of season? Or was it something else—something Agatha had seen in her? Or maybe felt?

The storm answered her thoughts with a crack of thunder that rattled her windows. She jumped slightly, her hands tightening around the steering wheel as her little car veered slightly over the slick road.

“Okay,” she muttered, brow furrowing. “That’s enough melodrama for one night.”

But it wasn’t. The storm only seemed to build the more her mind spiraled—gale winds shaking the trees, lightning forking across the sky like Mother nature herself was throwing a temper tantrum.

She finally pulled into the gravel clearing by her cottage, headlights cutting through the mist just enough to see the sagging outline of the front porch. The steps still groaned in protest from when she’d first arrived, and the whole thing looked more waterlogged than ever. She cursed under her breath, cutting the engine.

“Fix the porch,” she told herself aloud. “Seriously. Fix. The damn. Porch.”

She grabbed her bag and bolted for the door, hunched against the downpour. Her boots splashed through puddles, and cold rain instantly soaked through her jeans and jacket. One of the wooden boards on the top step shifted under her weight with a loud crack that made her stomach lurch.

“Shit,” she hissed, fumbling for her keys.

Thunder split the sky again, and she could’ve sworn it sounded like a growl. Not a rumble, but something sharp and angry. It echoed through the woods like it had teeth.

The key jammed in the lock. “Come on, come on, come— yes ,” she muttered in triumph as it finally gave way.

She stumbled inside, dripping wet and shivering, her pulse thudding somewhere behind her ears.

The door slammed shut behind her.

The cottage welcomed her with creaks and a familiar warmth, her now-functional space heater humming in the corner. She kicked off her boots, wiped her face with the sleeve of her coat, and dropped her bag with a thud. Her sketchbook had gotten a little wet despite her best efforts—edges curling like they were protesting the weather too.

She crossed to the window and looked back out.

All she could see was black, the kind that swallowed the trees and erased the hills. The lake was hidden beyond that, but she could almost feel it—still and watchful.

And for a split second… she thought she saw a flicker of movement.

Just there. At the edge of the trees. A figure? No, just a shadow.

Probably.

She drew the curtain shut, wrapping her arms around herself as another roll of thunder trembled through the house.

“Be careful with this place, Rio.”

Yeah. No kidding.

She lit a fire. She made tea. She toweled off and changed into dry clothes. But the storm didn’t let up, and neither did the hum under her skin.

~*~

The storm had passed, but its ghost still lingered in the dew-slick grass and heavy gray sky outside the morning after. The world felt quieter in its aftermath, like it was holding its breath.

Rio sat at her kitchen table, fingers smudged with pencil dust, her second mug of coffee going cold beside her.

The sketch in front of her was almost complete. A cat—not any cat she recognized, but one that had shown up, sharp and vivid in her mind the moment she opened her eyes that morning. Sleek and dark-furred, with intelligent eyes and an alert posture, like it was on the verge of speaking. Or casting a spell.

She furrowed her brow, adding delicate detail to its whiskers. Had she dreamt about it? She couldn’t remember. All she knew was that something—someone—had insisted on being drawn.

She didn’t even question it. Just padded out of bed, tossed on an old sweatshirt, made coffee, and reached for her sketchpad. The most natural thing in the world. Which was strange, considering how unnatural everything else had felt lately.

She blew gently on the page, brushing away a few stray pencil shavings, and looked at the cat again.

It stared back at her.

Not in the literal sense—she wasn’t that far gone—but something about the expression she’d given it unnerved her. There was a knowing there. Like the cat knew secrets it had no business knowing.

She glanced out the window. The lake was still veiled in mist, a pale sheet of it curling over the water. Beyond that, the dock was empty. No dark figures. No visitors. Just silence and fog.

Rio leaned back in her chair, rubbing her neck.

First, flowers blooming at her feet. Then storms that seemed to follow her mood. Now… prophetic cat drawings?

“What is this place,” she muttered.

She didn’t have an answer. But the strange part wasn’t how weird it all felt—it was how right it did.

Like some small, quiet part of her recognized it all. Like she was finally slipping into something that had been waiting for her all along.

She stared at the cat a little longer.

Then she picked up her pen and gave it a name.

Salem .

Because of course.

~*~

Rio pulled on her jacket and boots, sketchbook still open on the table behind her, the freshly drawn cat watching her with those vivid green eyes she couldn’t shake. She shook her head, grabbing her keys and grocery list, already half-lost in thoughts of what the town’s tiny market might or might not have in stock.

She opened the door—and froze.

A cat sat on her porch.

Gray fur. Silky and sleek, despite the cold. Eyes the color of deep moss or spring leaves, sharp and knowing. It tilted its head at her, as if in greeting—or judgment.

Rio blinked. Hard.

“You have got to be kidding me.”

The cat blinked back.

It was the same cat. The exact same cat she’d drawn less than an hour ago.

Before she could say anything else, it rose gracefully to its feet, padded past her ankles like it owned the place, and strolled inside.

“Hey—wait a second,” Rio stammered, turning to watch it as it sauntered toward the table, tail flicking. It jumped onto a chair, curled into a neat ball beside her sketchbook, and rested its chin on one paw.

Rio stepped inside slowly, shutting the door behind her as if she might spook it. Or maybe herself.

“What the hell is going on?” she muttered, staring at the feline intruder.

The cat yawned, eyes half-lidded, completely at home. Like it had been here before. Like it belonged.

“Okay,” Rio said slowly, running a hand through her hair. “Either I’m losing it, or this town is weirder than I thought.”

The cat didn’t argue.

Instead, it purred.

Low. Warm. Like it had been waiting for her.

Rio crouched down slowly, her eyes scanning the cat’s slender frame. He wasn’t alarmingly thin, but there was a certain gauntness to his ribs, a hollowness around his flanks that made her chest tighten with something she didn’t want to name.

She rubbed her palm against her jeans. “You haven’t eaten in a while, huh?”

The cat didn’t answer, of course, but he looked up at her with those vivid green eyes—steady, ancient, almost like he could.

“I don’t even have a can of tuna,” she muttered, standing with a sigh and heading toward the kitchen. “You want eggs? That’s a thing cats eat, right?”

She scrambled a couple in a pan with the kind of care she didn’t usually apply to her own meals. When she set the plate down, the cat sniffed at it, then began to eat delicately, like he’d been raised in a palace, not scavenging behind diners or back alleys.

“Of course,” Rio said, crossing her arms and watching him. “You’re a fancy bastard.”

He flicked his tail once in response.

She let out a breath and leaned back against the counter, still watching him. There was something uncanny about him. Not just his arrival, but how he’d matched exactly the image she’d drawn that morning, right down to the little silver-tipped hairs around his ears.

“Okay, this is weird,” she whispered. “This whole town is weird.”

The cat finished eating and sauntered into the living room like he owned the place, curling up on a blanket like he’d been waiting for her to get his room ready.

She followed him, brows drawn together, arms crossed. “You need a name,” she said slowly. “Unless you already have one.”

He cracked one eye open, his pupils narrowing into slits.

Rio hesitated, the name on the tip of her tongue before she even realized she was thinking it. “Salem.”

His ears perked. He sat up, tail curling around his feet. And then— then —he looked right at her.

She blinked. “No way.”

He meowed.

Rio stared at him. “You like that name?”

A louder meow.

“Okay, Salem it is,” she said, uneasily amused. “But we’re still going to a vet. Just in case you’re not secretly a demon.”

He yawned, long and slow, as if insulted.

Rio threw on a coat, scooped him into an old laundry basket lined with a blanket, and grabbed her keys. “You picked the weirdest girl in town to haunt, you know that?” she muttered, and Salem blinked up at her with what she swore looked like agreement.

~*~

The ride into town was mostly silent, the soft whir of the car tires the only sound. Salem was curled in the passenger seat now, having insisted on climbing out of the basket halfway through the drive. He watched the road like he was navigating.

“Okay, but just so we’re clear,” Rio muttered, “I’m still in charge.”

Salem flicked his tail.

The vet’s office sat in a cozy corner of Thistle Grove’s main strip, wrapped in creeping ivy and painted a calming moss green. The sign above read: “Dr. Jennifer Kale, DVM – Thistle Grove Animal Clinic” in sleek gold lettering.

Inside, the waiting room smelled like cedarwood and something minty. Rio barely had time to sit down with Salem in her lap before the door to the back opened and a tall woman stepped out.

She was striking—tall and commanding, with warm brown skin, a shaved head that caught the light, and eyes sharp enough to catch a lie mid-air. Her white coat was spotless, and her smile was kind but steady.

“Rio Vidal?” she asked, already clocking the cat in her arms. “I’m Dr. Kale. Come on back.”

The exam room was cozy, full of soft lighting and soft-spoken energy.

“He’s gorgeous,” Dr. Kale said, stroking Salem’s head with practiced fingers. “Little underweight. No collar, no chip. But otherwise? Very healthy. Did he find you or…?”

“He found me,” Rio said. “Walked right into my house this morning like he’d been planning it all along.”

Dr. Kale chuckled. “Cats don’t choose lightly. If he walked in, he’s already decided you’re his.”

Rio gave Salem a sidelong glance. “That’s what I’m afraid of.”

Dr. Kale smiled as she entered notes into a tablet. “Some animals… have a way of knowing where they’re needed. He doesn’t seem like your average stray.”

No kidding, Rio thought.

Once Salem was cleared and she paid the bill, Rio asked, “Do you know a place in town where I can grab pet supplies?”

“Familiar Spirits,” Dr. Kale said. “Corner of Maple and Ash. Ask for Maeve. She’ll hook you up.”

~*~

Familiar Spirits was exactly what Rio should’ve expected from a shop in a place like Thistle Grove. Half pet store, half occult emporium. The shelves were stacked with everything from organic kibble to tarot card chew toys and hand-carved pet beds shaped like crescent moons.

Salem trotted beside her on a leash, which he somehow tolerated. She was scanning a wall of plush mice when she heard a familiar voice from the next aisle.

“No, Scratchy, we are not getting the parsley hay. You never finish it, and it makes your breath smell like compost.”

Rio peeked around the corner—and there she was. Agatha Harkness in a long charcoal coat, hair twisted back into a loose knot, holding a small basket of rabbit supplies and arguing quietly with a velvet-lined carrier perched in her cart. Inside it sat the fluffiest white rabbit Rio had ever seen, with what looked like an honest-to-god attitude problem.

Agatha looked up, mid-eye-roll, and froze.

Rio blinked. “Hey.”

Agatha’s brows rose, but she recovered quickly, smoothing her expression into something halfway amused. “Well, if it isn’t the new cat lady of Thistle Grove.”

Rio held up a bag of salmon treats. “Guilty. You know, it’s only been a few hours and I already feel like he owns my house.”

Agatha’s eyes slid down to Salem, who—naturally—had wrapped his leash around Rio’s ankles like he was casting a trap spell. “He looks like the type.”

Rio laughed softly, adjusting the leash. “You here for your—what did you call him? Scratchy?”

The rabbit, as if on cue, gave a loud thump from his carrier.

Agatha sighed. “The demon himself. He’s my companion-slash-overlord. We have an arrangement.”

Rio’s eyes crinkled. “That’s… shockingly relatable.”

For a moment, they just stood there, two grown women surrounded by squeaky toys and pet crystals, their animals clearly running the show.

Agatha tilted her head, a smile tugging at the corner of her mouth. “You know, I don’t usually run into people here. Most locals get their supplies from the feed store in the next town over. This place is a little… odd.”

Rio looked around at the moonstone pet collars and the herbal remedy shelf. “Odd seems par for the course these days.”

There was a beat. Then Agatha stepped closer, her voice a bit softer.

“You settling in okay?”

Rio nodded. “Yeah. I mean… as much as anyone can in a house with creaky floorboards and a cat that may or may not be a figment of my imagination.”

Agatha’s gaze flicked to Salem again, and something thoughtful passed over her face.

“Well,” she said lightly, “if he is imaginary, he’s got excellent taste in canned food.”

Rio chuckled, warm and easy. “Thanks. I think.”

Agatha reached for a bag of alfalfa hay, but her voice stayed casual. “Maybe we’ll bump into each other again soon.”

Rio hesitated, then smiled. “I’d like that.”

As she turned to check out, she couldn’t help but glance over her shoulder one more time. Agatha was still watching her, just briefly, before turning her attention back to her rabbit.

And Salem? He was purring. Again.

“You’re trouble,” Rio whispered to him, but she was smiling as she said it.

 

Chapter 6: Six

Agatha lingered for a beat too long after Rio left.

The chime above the pet store door had barely stopped ringing when she finally moved again, placing the last of Mr. Scratchy’s items on the counter without comment. Her smile toward Maeve was thin, automatic. She paid, scooped up her bunny’s carrier, and headed out into the chilled afternoon air, her boots striking sharply against the pavement.

Once in her car, she buckled Mr. Scratchy in with practiced ease, then sat in the driver’s seat for a moment, staring at her hands on the wheel.

“A figment of her imagination. She must have dreamt about him,” she muttered, shaking her head. “Of course she did.”

The cat wasn’t just a stray. Salem— gods, even the name —was a familiar. And not the kind that wandered into just anyone’s life. Familiars chose witches, and they only did so when the bond was ready. When the power was ready.

And Rio Vidal, like it or not, had been chosen.

~*~

Agatha slammed the car door harder than necessary and stalked up the winding path to Wanda’s house, her boots crunching over the gravel like thunder in the quiet dusk. The storm from the night before had passed, but something inside her hadn’t settled.

Mr. Scratchy, perfectly content in his carrier, gave a disapproving thump of a hind leg as she placed him down on Wanda’s kitchen floor. Agatha barely spared him a glance before unbuttoning her coat with stiff fingers and letting it fall over a chair.

Wanda was already waiting for her—of course she was. Leaning against the kitchen counter, sipping from a chipped mug with a faint smirk tugging at her lips.

“You look like someone who just saw a ghost,” she said lazily, setting the mug down with a soft clink.

Agatha crossed the room in three quick strides. “She has a familiar.”

Wanda’s eyes sparked, and she didn’t even try to hide the satisfaction blooming on her face.

“That was fast ,” she murmured, more to herself than Agatha. Then, louder: “And what kind of familiar are we talking? Crow? Fox? Something dramatic?”

“Cat,” Agatha bit out. “Gray. Green eyes. Looked like it had been through hell, but it marched into her cottage like it belonged there.”

Wanda smiled into her tea. “Because it does belong there.”

Agatha narrowed her eyes. “Don’t start.”

“I’m not starting. I’m continuing,” Wanda said sweetly. “Because I’ve been saying for a while now that she’s waking up. And you—” she pointed a finger at Agatha like a wand— “have been pretending it’s all coincidence.”

“She still doesn’t know anything.”

“She has a familiar, Agatha.”

Agatha didn’t reply.

Wanda set her mug down. “Do you remember how long it took Scratchy to come to you?”

Agatha’s throat tightened.

Mr. Scratchy, now out of his carrier and lounging beneath the kitchen table, let out a soft grunt, as if remembering too. He had come to her when Nicky died—when her magic had been threatening to consume her entirely. She hadn’t known how badly she needed him until he showed up and refused to leave.

“She’s untrained,” Agatha said finally. “She doesn’t know what’s happening to her. Doesn’t even know what a Thread is. And now she’s bonded with a familiar. Her powers might be stronger than we can even imagine at this rate. And she has no idea.”

“Then maybe it’s time you told her.”

“She’s not ready.”

Wanda raised a brow. “Or you’re not.”

Agatha glared, but it lacked heat. The truth was too close.

Wanda’s tone softened slightly, though the smug undercurrent still lingered. “You know what it means, don’t you? For a familiar to appear like that. It’s not random. It’s not an accident. It means the land recognized her. The magic did. And it offered her something sacred in return. It means she’s ready.”

Agatha turned toward the window. Outside, the trees stood dark and still, the clouds slowly parting to let pale light spill through. “She doesn’t even want to be here. She’s just trying to grieve and make sense of her life again.”

“Which is exactly when magic finds us,” Wanda said. “When we’re cracked open. When something inside us is ready, even if we’re not.”

Agatha shut her eyes. “She’s not safe.”

Wanda tilted her head. “No. Not if we leave her like this.”

Silence lingered between them, thick as fog. 

Agatha folded her arms tightly across her chest. “I don’t want her dragged into this. Into us. Into what this whole Threads bullshit expects.”

Wanda crossed the space and touched her arm gently. “You think she hasn’t already been dragged in? The moment the cottage called her back. The moment her fingers made flowers bloom. The moment that cat crossed her threshold. She’s not standing on the edge anymore, Agatha. She’s already in it.”

Agatha shook her head, quiet. “I’ve already lost too much.”

Wanda’s eyes softened. “And maybe she’s not here to take anything else. Maybe she’s here to give something back.”

Agatha didn’t answer.

But in her chest, that old ache stirred again—tangled with a new pull she didn’t want to name. Not yet.

Not when everything was changing again.

~*~

The bar was warm in that hazy, familiar way—dim lighting, a soft buzz of conversation, the smell of old wood and whiskey. She sat in her usual corner booth, fingers curled around a half-empty glass of Laphroaig, letting the smoke bite down her throat just enough to keep her grounded.

She wasn’t sure what she’d come here for. Answers didn’t live in bars. But the truth was, she didn’t want to go home. She didn’t want to sit in silence and think about the cat. The familiar.

Or Rio, for that matter.

Gods.

Agatha had barely made peace with the idea that the woman was one of the Threads—and now a familiar had shown up? That only happened when magic had rooted itself deep, anchored itself in someone’s soul.

And Rio hadn’t even flinched.

No panic. No questions. Just picked the damn thing up and bought it toys.

Agatha took another drink, slow, thoughtful.

She hadn’t told Wanda everything. Not really. She hadn’t said that when she and Rio touched—first by the lake, then again on the way into the cottage—it felt like something woke up. Like a piece of her she’d buried years ago cracked open.

That was the part she didn’t know how to explain. Or maybe didn’t want to.

The door opened behind her. A gust of cold slipped in with the sound of boots on wood.

Agatha didn’t have to look. She felt her before she saw her.

Rio Vidal. Wearing a black leather skirt, a deep green sweater and a black coat over it, which made her look unfairly gorgeous.

She scanned the room, dark eyes skipping across the tables—until they found her.

And then Rio smiled. Just a little. Not smug, not flirty, not trying. Just… warm.

Agatha swallowed her sigh and looked back down at her glass.

A moment later, Rio appeared beside the booth. “Funny running into you.”

Agatha didn’t look up. “It’s the only bar in town.”

“That’s not true. There’s the distillery outside of Thistle Creek. Technically counts.”

Agatha gave her a sidelong glance. “That’s forty-five minutes away.”

Rio shrugged, curls still wild from the wind. “Maybe I was in the mood for local.”

She didn’t ask to sit, just slid into the booth across from her like she belonged there.

And maybe she did.

Agatha tried not to stare. “Rough day?”

“Long one,” Rio said. “But good. Productive.”

That word made Agatha look up. “You’re drawing again?”

Rio grinned, clearly surprised. “You remembered that?”

Agatha didn’t answer.

Rio reached into her coat and pulled out a folded-up paper bag. “Made some poor financial decisions at the pet store.”

Agatha blinked. “Salem.”

“Yeah,” Rio laughed. “He’s already acting like he pays rent. But he let me sleep for eight hours straight last night, so honestly, I might buy him a whole condo.”

Agatha’s brows lifted. “You’re taking this… surprisingly well.”

“What, the cat? I mean, I didn’t expect him, but…” She shrugged. “Some things just fit, I guess.”

Agatha sipped her drink to avoid saying something foolish. Like: That’s not just a cat. Or: You’re more than you think.

Or: I have no idea how to protect you from what’s coming.

Instead, she said, “You named him Salem.”

“Yeah. Classic, right? He just looked like a Salem.”

Agatha nodded slowly. “It suits him.”

Rio leaned back, looking at her curiously. “Why are you here, Agatha?”

“What do you mean?”

“I mean…” She waved a hand between them. “You and me. Bar. Friday night. We’re turning this into a habit. I figured you’d be home with your rabbit and a bottle of something better than this.”

Agatha smirked despite herself. “Mr. Scratchy does prefer quiet nights.”

“So why aren’t you there?”

Agatha stared at her glass for a long moment. “Maybe I needed noise. Or… company.”

“Or both?”

Agatha met her gaze. “Maybe.”

They sat in that quiet space for a while—warm and electric and impossible.

Rio eventually finished her ginger beer and pushed the empty glass aside. “Well, for what it’s worth, I’m glad I ran into you.”

Agatha tried to keep her voice level. “Why?”

Rio smiled. “You have a calming presence.”

Agatha huffed a laugh. “No one’s ever said that to me before.”

“Then they haven’t seen you like this.”

Agatha froze.

Something twisted in her chest. Something dangerous.

The silence stretched between them—comfortable, but laced with something neither of them had quite named yet. Agatha found herself studying the slope of Rio’s jaw, the curl of her dark hair damp at the ends from the walk over, the faint smudge of graphite still on her fingertips.

She’s real. That’s what Agatha kept thinking. Real and stubborn and grounding in a way she hadn’t realized she needed.

Before she could stop herself, she heard her own voice say, “You want something better than bar whiskey?”

Rio looked up, brows lifting. “That sounds dangerously like an offer.”

Agatha smirked. “It’s not dangerous. Just… better scotch. Warmer chairs. Less sticky tables.”

Rio leaned forward, eyes dancing. “And Mr. Scratchy.”

“He’s judgmental, but he keeps to himself.”

Rio considered for a beat, then nodded. “Sure. Let’s go.”

They walked back through the quiet streets of Thistle Grove, wind whistling faintly through bare trees. The last of the leaves clung to branches in deep gold and rusted red, but winter was making its presence known. A sharp chill had settled into the bones of the town.

Agatha’s house wasn’t far—an old two-story just past the apothecary, tucked into the end of a cul-de-sac. It was quiet, the porch light a soft glow in the dark. Rio looked around, taking in the creeping ivy on the front and the worn but well-kept stone steps.

“Didn’t picture you as a ‘homey’ type,” Rio said as they walked up to the door.

“I’m not,” Agatha replied, unlocking it. “But I like my space. And no one bothers me out here.”

She pushed the door open, letting Rio step inside first.

It was warm, cozy even. Not in a traditional way—there were strange herbs hanging by the window, stacks of books along every surface, and a fireplace that crackled with an actual flame—but there was something lived-in about it. Safe.

Rio turned in a slow circle. “Wow. It’s like… a storybook witch’s house, but cooler.”

Agatha raised an eyebrow. “Is that a compliment?”

Rio grinned. “Absolutely.”

Agatha chuckled and moved to the kitchen. “Sit down. I’ll grab the good stuff.”

She poured two fingers of Macallan into heavy glasses and came back to find Rio crouching by a bookshelf, flipping through an old volume of poetry.

~*~

They’d been sitting together for over an hour now, low in their chairs by the fire, the glasses of scotch sitting nearly empty between them on the side table. Outside, the wind had picked up, tapping against the windows like impatient fingers. Inside, though, it was warm. Quiet. A kind of easy that Agatha wasn’t used to feeling—especially not around someone who made her pulse do what Rio Vidal did.

Rio leaned forward to set her glass down, brushing her fingers against the rim. “That’s really good,” she murmured, smiling. “You have taste, Harkness.”

Agatha smirked. “Of course I do.”

She watched Rio move, graceful without trying to be, every shift of her body so natural it made Agatha’s mouth go a little dry. Rio pushed her chair back without thinking, the movement a little too sharp. Her hip clipped the edge of the narrow table between her and Agatha, and with a clatter, the glass tumbler toppled over. It hit the hardwood floor and shattered into jagged shards that skittered outward like tiny knives.

“God, I’m so sorry!” Rio said quickly.

Agatha barely had time to process the sharp clatter of the tumbler hitting the floor before Rio was already moving — too fast, too thoughtless — dropping to her knees among the scattered shards of glass.

“No—” Agatha started, but the word snagged uselessly in her throat.

She saw it happen as if time slowed — the wicked point of a shard waiting, the way Rio’s knee would land. And worse — she felt it.

A pulse of magic tore through her without warning, snapping tight behind her eyes. In a flash, she saw the outcome: the glass driving deep, blood pouring fast, infection setting in, tendons torn — pain that would not stop unless someone intervened.

Her heart lurched into her mouth.

The glass punched through her pantyhose with a sickening crunch. Rio gasped, half-falling as her leg gave out under her. Blood was already blooming, fast and dark, against the sheer fabric.

Agatha moved on instinct, every nerve lit up with urgency. She caught Rio under the arms, dragging her up and away from the broken glass.

“Hold still,” she ordered, her voice tight with a fear she couldn’t afford to show.

Rio sagged against her, breath hissing through her teeth. Agatha pressed her hand over the torn fabric, feeling the blood, the damage — and she knew, with grim certainty, what had to happen next.

There was no time to hesitate. No time to lie.

“I can fix it,” she said quietly.

Rio gave her a look—half skeptical, half confused. “Do you… have a first aid kit or something?”

“No.”

Agatha let her fingers hover just above the gash. The magic thrummed under her skin, eager and warm and terrifying in how instinctive it felt.

“I have this.”

Rio stared at her, dazed and hurting, the confusion in her eyes cutting deeper than any wound.

Agatha didn’t hesitate. She couldn’t.

She pressed her palm carefully over the torn fabric, over the slick, bleeding wound. The moment her skin touched Rio’s, her magic leapt to meet her — not wild, not unruly, but steady and sure, like it had been waiting for this.

A soft golden light unfurled from her hand, spilling through her fingers and sinking into Rio’s skin. The glow warmed the space between them, delicate and bright, as if the magic itself knew to be gentle with her.

Under her touch, the bleeding slowed. Torn flesh knit itself together with quiet, aching precision. The magic hummed low in her bones, wrapping around Rio like a promise.

When the worst of it had passed, Agatha pulled her hand away, her fingers shaking just a little. The blood was already drying. The wound was nothing but a faint, angry mark — nothing that would scar.

Rio was staring at her, wide-eyed, the fading golden light reflected in her gaze.

Agatha swallowed hard. For once, she didn’t have the strength — or the will — to lie.

Rio stared at her. “That’s… what did you just—?”

Agatha didn’t pull back right away. Her hand lingered, brushing lightly against Rio’s calf, grounding them both.

“I healed you.”

Rio blinked. “Yeah, no shit. How ?”

Agatha finally looked up at her, eyes shadowed and serious. “Because I couldn’t not.”

She stood slowly, brushing glass off her palms, but her gaze never left Rio’s.

Rio opened her mouth to say something—maybe a joke, maybe a challenge—but stopped when she saw the look in Agatha’s eyes. The depth of it. The fear. The honesty.

“I saw what would happen if I didn’t act right then,” Agatha went on, her voice steadier now, but still soft. She touched the spot on Rio’s leg again. “I saw too much blood. Panic. You falling. I just… I couldn’t let it happen. Not to you.”

The room went still. The fire crackled behind them. Rain tapped gently against the windows.

For a long moment, neither of them said anything. Agatha’s hand finally slipped away from Rio’s skin, but the space she left behind still hummed with something Rio didn’t have words for. Not yet.

Rio let out a shaky breath and stood, pacing the length of the living room for a moment. Then she stopped and leaned back slightly against the back of the couch, blinking at Agatha like she was trying to make out a shape in the fog.

“You saw what would happen?” she echoed, incredulously. “Like—what, in your head? Some kind of psychic flash? Is that a thing now too?”

Agatha didn’t smile. She just nodded once, slowly.

Rio laughed, but there was no humor in it—just disbelief, nerves crackling like static. “Jesus. Okay. So you can heal people, you can see the future, and you’re—what, casually psychic with a side of storm-summoning?”

Agatha gave her a look—dry, steady. “I didn’t summon any storms.”

“That’s the part you’re taking issue with?”

“I’m serious.”

“So am I!”

The air between them buzzed with tension, almost electric. Rio’s eyes were wide, still a little wild, like she wasn’t sure whether to bolt out the door or sit down and ask a thousand more questions.

Agatha softened just a little. “I know this is a lot. But I didn’t do it to scare you. I didn’t want to do anything. I just… reacted. It was instinct.”

Rio stared at her, and then glanced down at her leg—smooth, clean skin where a deep cut should have been. She looked back up. “That wasn’t instinct, Agatha. That was—impossible.”

A beat. Agatha tilted her head. “You live in a haunted cottage, saw flowers bloom around us, dreamed about a cat that now lives with you, and you think this is where it gets impossible?”

Rio opened her mouth, closed it again. 

“Rio, you’re just like me. A witch,” Agatha continued, her voice soft.

A different kind of silence fell. This one was heavy, contemplative. Something had shifted—between them, around them.

And Rio, despite everything, didn’t run.

Rio moved toward the chair she’d been sitting in before, like her legs weren’t quite steady underneath her. She pulled out a chair and sat down heavily, her eyes still darting to her leg like it might suddenly decide to start bleeding again. It hadn’t. The skin there was clean, smooth. Whole.

Agatha stayed near the counter, giving her space. Her fingers brushed against the rim of her tumbler, but she didn’t lift it. Her eyes stayed on Rio.

“I know it’s a lot,” she said quietly.

Rio snorted softly, though there wasn’t much humor in it. “ You think ?”

Agatha’s mouth quirked into the ghost of a smile. “I do.”

A long beat passed between them. The only sound was the clock on the wall ticking softly behind them and the quiet wind beyond the windows.

Rio ran a hand over her face. “So… okay. Let’s just say I’m… not completely ruling it out. Hypothetically. You’re saying I’m a witch. But I’ve never done anything… witchy. I don’t have any spells. I don’t even own sage.”

Agatha didn’t laugh, but her eyes warmed. “It’s not about spells. Not really. Not at first.”

“Then what is it about?”

Agatha took a moment, choosing her words carefully. “It’s about connection. To the land. To instinct. To power that isn’t always visible, but always present. It starts small—gut feelings that turn out to be right. Dreams that linger too long. The way you dreamed about Salem before he showed up.”

Rio stiffened. “Wait. How do you know about that?”

Agatha blinked. “You told me. At the pet store. You said that you didn’t know if he’d come from your imagination or something like that. Remember?”

“Oh. Right.” She shook her head. “Jesus.”

Agatha stepped a little closer, cautious. “You don’t have to believe me all at once. I know it’s a hard pill to swallow. But something in you already does, or you wouldn’t be asking questions.”

Rio didn’t answer right away. Her eyes were on her hands, which were folded on her lap now. The fingertips of one tapped lightly against the other, as though keeping time with a rhythm she didn’t quite understand yet.

Then, finally: “And this thing with you… and me… when we touch. The flowers. That’s part of it?”

Agatha’s breath caught.

“Yes,” she said, quietly. “Kind of. Though that’s… more complicated.”

“Of course it is.”

“I didn’t mean for any of this to happen,” Agatha added. “I wasn’t trying to drag you into something you weren’t ready for.”

“Then why did you heal me?”

Agatha looked at her. Really looked. And for a moment, something unguarded flickered in her expression. Something real.

“Because I couldn’t not do it.”

Rio’s eyes narrowed slightly. “Why?”

A breath. A heartbeat. And then: “Because I saw what would happen if I didn’t. And I couldn’t let that happen to you.”

Rio stared at her. She didn’t speak. Not for a while.

And Agatha let the silence hang, let Rio sit with the impossible truth of what she’d seen, what she’d done.

Finally, Rio leaned back in her chair and exhaled like she was emptying a whole drawer of thoughts at once. “Okay. Alright. I need… I don’t know. More scotch. A nap. A full psychiatric evaluation. Possibly all three.”

Agatha smirked. “I can do scotch.”

“Figures.”

She didn’t say no when Agatha poured them both another glass, the amber liquid catching the light in the room as it slid into the crystal tumblers.

“Does it always feel like… this?” Rio asked, after a long, contemplative sip. “Like, I’m just… thrown into this whole new world where magic is real and I’m supposed to… do something with it?”

Agatha leaned back against the counter, watching her with an unreadable expression. “I’m not going to say it’s easy. It might not feel natural at first. But eventually, it’s just part of who you are. Like breathing.”

“And Salem. That damn cat–”

“He’s your familiar. The fact that he’s already found you means that your power is definitely active and ingrained in who you are.

“This is messed up,” Rio muttered, taking another drink. 

“Not really,” Agatha said. Her voice softened slightly. “The hardest part is understanding that you’re not alone. You’re connected to something bigger, even when it doesn’t feel that way.”

Rio glanced at her through lowered lashes. “This is what you were talking about, right? Connection. The land. The power.”

“Exactly.”

Rio blew out a breath, looking at her glass before meeting Agatha’s gaze again. “So what now? Do you… teach me to be a witch? Do I need to go to magic school or Hogwarts or whatever? Or is that just not how it works?”

Agatha’s expression was unreadable for a long moment before she chuckled. “Calm down, Potter, you’re not getting a wand just yet,” she offered a playful grin and Rio let out an incredulous laugh. “I can teach you the basics, but I suppose that depends on whether or not you want to learn. This isn’t something that can be forced.”

Rio stared at her. “And you think I want to?”

Agatha gave a half-shrug, her gaze steady. “I think you already do. Whether you’re ready to admit it or not.”

Rio wasn’t sure what to make of that, so she took another sip of her scotch and didn’t say anything. The silence between them stretched a little longer, but this time, it didn’t feel quite so heavy.

Rio swirled the amber liquid in her glass, the ice long since melted. “So what exactly can you do? You know, besides magically healing gaping wounds and brooding dramatically in corners.”

Agatha smirked, but it didn’t quite reach her eyes. “You’ve barely scratched the surface of my dramatic repertoire.”

Rio snorted softly, then sobered. “Seriously, though. Is this your whole life? Potions, spells, secret familiars?”

Agatha leaned against the kitchen counter, crossing her arms. “I’ve kept my power pretty quiet for a long time now. I only started using it again… recently.”

“Why’d you stop?”

A beat.

Agatha looked away, her jaw tightening slightly. “That’s a longer story. One I’m not sure I’m ready to share just yet.”

Rio gave her a look, half amusement, half challenge. “You do realize I just watched you stitch my skin together with glowing light, right? I think I can handle a story.”

Agatha’s lips parted like she might say something, but she hesitated. Instead, she let out a soft breath and tilted her head. “Let’s just say… magic comes with a price. And sometimes it fails you. So I was angry and I decided to leave it all behind me. After what happened with my mother… And—”

Rio’s gaze softened. “She was a witch, too?”

Agatha nodded. “She disappeared over twenty years ago. No note. No explanation. One day she was just… gone. People say she got lost in her magic. That it consumed her. I didn’t want that to happen to me—or to Nicky.”

There was silence for a moment, heavier now but not uncomfortable.

Rio stared at her scotch, then murmured, “Is that what you think will happen to me?”

Agatha looked at her then, really looked. Her voice was low but firm. “No. I think you’re different. You’re grounded, even if you don’t realize it yet. And you have something my mother never had.”

Rio raised an eyebrow. “And what’s that?”

“Someone watching out for you,” Agatha said, then took a slow sip of her drink. “Even if you don’t want me to.”

That took the air out of Rio for a second. Her breath caught, almost imperceptibly, but it was there. She didn’t speak right away, just stared down at her glass like it held answers she couldn’t find anywhere else.

Finally, she asked, softer now, “So how do I even begin with this? I mean, is there a rulebook? A tutorial? A ‘Witchcraft for Dummies’ guide I can borrow?”

Agatha chuckled under her breath. “Not unless Wanda’s finally written one.”

Rio blinked. “Wanda?”

“My best friend. Red hair. Sharp tongue. Absolutely terrifying. You guys met the other day at the shop.”

Rio smiled faintly. “Sounds like someone I’d get along with.”

Agatha tilted her head. “You just might.”

Another quiet moment stretched out between them, the kind that didn’t need to be filled. The scotch, the flickering candlelight from the kitchen, the distant rumble of the storm outside—everything felt muted, suspended.

Rio glanced at the clock. “It’s late.”

“Yeah,” Agatha said softly, but didn’t move.

Rio stood, glass in hand, and leaned back against the table. “So, you’re saying I’m a witch.”

Agatha nodded.

“And I’ve got a familiar.”

“Definitely.”

“And possibly very dangerous powers I don’t know how to use.”

Agatha gave a dry smile. “Welcome to Thistle Grove.”

Rio shook her head slowly, then drained the rest of her scotch. “This is officially the weirdest week of my life.”

Agatha chuckled. “It only gets weirder from here.”

“I was afraid you’d say that.”

Rio ran a hand through her curls and set the empty glass down with a little clink. Her eyes swept the living room, then landed on the front door. “I should probably head back.”

Agatha, who’d just stood to grab them more water, shot her a look over her shoulder. “You’re kidding. It’s raining like crazy out there.”

“I’ve driven in worse,” Rio offered, though even she didn’t sound convinced.

“You’ve also been drinking. I’m not healing you again because you decided to be stubborn.”

Rio blinked. “You say that like it’s a real possibility.”

Agatha crossed her arms. “You think I’m exaggerating?”

“…No,” Rio muttered, glancing back at her leg. “Fine. I’ll crash on your couch or something.”

“Guest room,” Agatha said, already turning to grab some water glasses. “I’m not a total monster, you know.”

As she passed one to Rio, their fingers brushed—just a brief, incidental graze, but the kitchen light flickered overhead. Rio’s head jerked up, eyes wide.

“Was that—?”

“Probably,” Agatha said calmly, though her fingers lingered a second too long on the glass before she stepped back.

Rio swallowed and rubbed her palm. “That… doesn’t happen with anyone else.”

“I should hope not,” Agatha said with a ghost of a smirk.

The mood wasn’t heavy now, not exactly, but there was a hush to it—an awareness. Like the air was thick with something unsaid.

Agatha moved to sit at the edge of the low windowsill, watching the rain trickle down the glass. “When two witches’ magic aligns, it can do strange things,” she said quietly. “Especially when they’re… connected.”

Rio leaned against the counter, her brow furrowed. “Connected how?”

Agatha shrugged one shoulder, fingers absently trailing the wooden sill. “Sometimes magic just recognizes something before we do.”

Rio stared at her hand, remembering the golden light that had spilled from Agatha’s palm and stitched her skin whole. She stepped closer, setting her glass beside the sink.

“Can I try something?”

Agatha tilted her head, intrigued. “Try what?”

Rio held out her hand. “Touch. On purpose this time.”

Agatha looked at it for a beat. Then, with deliberate slowness, she lifted her own and pressed it to Rio’s.

The effect was instant.

A low hum passed between their palms, like a current of warmth. Gold light shimmered at the center of their contact point, spiraling outward in thin, glowing tendrils. A nearby candle flared brighter. From a nearby potted plant, a single flower bloomed, soft and violet, trembling like it had just breathed for the first time.

Rio’s mouth parted slightly. “Holy—”

Agatha’s voice was low. “Yeah.”

The light faded slowly, leaving only the heat of their skin. But Rio didn’t pull away. Her gaze drifted to Agatha’s face, searching.

“Does this happen with other witches?”

“Some,” Agatha admitted. “But not like this.”

Rio glanced at the flower, then their hands. “This is insane.”

“It’s magic,” Agatha murmured. “It’s rarely sane.”

They stayed like that for a moment longer—hands joined, pulses steady but elevated. Then Rio gently withdrew, fingertips brushing along Agatha’s as she did. Lights above them dimmed, like the house was reluctantly letting them go.

Rio licked her lips. “So… guest room, huh?”

Agatha blinked, then smirked. “Unless you’re brave enough to try sleeping in the kitchen with Mr. Scratchy.”

“I think I’ll take my chances in the guest room, then.”

As Agatha led her down the hall, their shoulders brushed in the narrow corridor—and a soft flicker of light rippled down the walls, chasing them like fireflies.

 

Chapter 7: Seven

The guest room was cozy and a little mismatched, like the rest of Agatha’s house—warm-toned quilts, old wood furniture with ornate carvings, and soft lamplight that pooled golden over everything. It smelled faintly like lavender and something else—moss and rosemary, maybe. Something wild.

Rio stood awkwardly by the bed, arms loosely folded across her chest as Agatha flitted around her, smoothing the blanket, lighting another small candle on the bedside table.

“You really don’t have to fuss,” Rio said, a little overwhelmed by the domesticity of it all.

Agatha only arched an eyebrow. “It’s basic hospitality,” she replied, leaving a pair of flannel pajama pants and a shirt on top of the blanket.

“I didn’t know you did hospitality.”

“I’m full of surprises.”
