Rio watched her in the soft light, the way her hands moved with quiet efficiency. There was something careful in it, like she didn’t want Rio to feel like a burden. It worked. It made something sharp in her chest soften.

Agatha turned to her, brushing her hands together. “There are fresh towels in the bathroom if you want a shower, and I put a glass of water on the nightstand.”

Rio nodded with a small smile. “Thanks. For all of this.”

Agatha shrugged, but there was warmth in her eyes. “You’re welcome.”

Their gazes held a second longer than necessary. Not quite a charged moment—but close. Familiar. Magnetic.

Then Agatha stepped back toward the doorway. “Get some rest, Rio.”

Rio nodded, watching her go.

But just before Agatha could fully disappear down the hallway, Rio called softly, “Hey.”

Agatha paused and looked back, a flicker of something unreadable in her expression.

Rio gestured to the space around her. “All of this… it’s nice.”

A ghost of a smile curved Agatha’s lips. “Don’t get used to it.”

And with that, she vanished into the hall, the door clicking shut behind her.

Rio stood in place for a moment, listening to the sound of her own breath, to the rain falling softly outside. Then she sat down on the edge of the bed and toed off her boots, running a hand through her curls as she looked around the room once more.

The flower Agatha had placed in a little glass dish on the windowsill—pale and violet, the one that bloomed when they touched—was still there, alive and open, its petals stretching toward the dark.

Rio stared at it, heart beating a little too fast.

“Okay,” she whispered to the quiet. “This is happening.”

After changing into the clothes Agatha had left for her, she lay back on the bed, eyes wide in the dark, and let that truth settle into her bones.

~*~

She lay there for a while, staring up at the ceiling, but rest didn’t come.

The more she thought about it, the more it felt like her thoughts were starting to spin out of control, cycling through the same questions she didn’t have answers to.

You’re a witch, Rio.

Agatha hadn’t said the words exactly like that. Not directly. But she might as well have.

And then there was the glass. The magic. The healing. The flower. The way her body had pulsed like something inside her had been awakened. And the cat. Salem.

God—the cat.

She sat up sharply, heart pounding. She hadn’t told Agatha about drawing him. Or dreaming about him. Or how he looked at her like he knew her the moment she said his name. None of this made sense. None of it could be real.

Could it?

As her breathing quickened, the storm outside began to rise. Wind slammed against the windows. Rain battered the roof in heavy, unrelenting waves. The trees beyond the glass groaned under the weight of the wind. A sharp crack of thunder split the sky, and the lights flickered—just once—but it was enough to make Rio jump.

“Okay,” she whispered, standing up. Her voice shook. “Okay, just breathe. You’re not losing it.”

She paced the room in slow circles, rubbing her arms. Her skin felt like it didn’t belong to her. Her fingertips tingled.

Lightning struck again—closer this time, illuminating the room in a brilliant, white-hot flash that made the flower on the windowsill almost glow.

Rio turned toward it. The petals shimmered faintly. Undeniably alive.

Her chest tightened.

“Shit.”

She stumbled toward the window, trying to steady her breathing, but the storm outside was matching her panic beat for beat, like it was wired to her heartbeat. 

Was it?

Another rumble of thunder vibrated through the glass.

She grabbed the edges of the windowsill and bowed her head. Her curls fell forward, damp with sweat.

“What is happening to me?” she whispered.

No one answered.

But deep down, something inside her stirred.

Not in fear.

In recognition.

The storm crashed again, louder this time, shaking the walls like some great beast had slammed against the house. Thunder boomed so close, so sharp, Rio swore she felt it in her bones. Rain hammered the windows like fists, and the wind howled, tearing through the trees outside with a ferocity that made her stomach twist.

She pressed her palm against the cold glass of the window, heart hammering in her chest. Her breaths came too fast, too shallow, and she couldn’t stop the spinning thoughts: What is happening to me? What am I becoming?

The lights flickered again.

Her hands trembled.

She didn’t know whether she wanted to scream or cry or run, but her body had gone cold with panic. Magic? That word still felt too big for her. Too far-fetched. She was just a girl who liked to draw, who’d been cheated on, who’d inherited a crumbling cottage and a storm she couldn’t control.

Then—soft footsteps.

A gentle knock at the door, followed by a low voice that reached her like an anchor in a hurricane.

“Rio?” Agatha’s voice. Steady. Warm. Grounding.

She turned sharply, breath catching, eyes wide like a deer caught in headlights.

The door creaked open.

Agatha stepped inside, wrapped in a deep burgundy robe, her long dark hair loose and slightly tousled from sleep. She took in the scene without speaking: Rio standing pale and shaken by the window, the way her shoulders rose and fell with ragged breaths, the panic in her eyes.

Agatha’s expression softened instantly, her voice dropping to something that felt like a balm. “You’re causing the storm.”

Rio blinked, her mouth opening but no sound coming out at first. “What?”

“The wind, the thunder, the lightning—it’s you,” Agatha said gently. “Your panic. Your fear. It’s bleeding into the world.”

“That’s… that’s not possible.” Rio shook her head, voice hoarse. “How could I possibly be doing that?”

Agatha moved a little closer, slow and deliberate, like she might scare her off otherwise. “Magic doesn’t always ask your permission. Especially not when it’s this new. Especially not when you don’t know how to control it.”

Rio backed away a step, bumping into the small side table near the bed. Her hands gripped the edge of it. “I…I didn’t mean to do anything. I didn’t try—”

“I know,” Agatha said, voice low and kind. “But you don’t have to try. That’s why I didn’t want you going through this alone. Just breathe with me for a moment.”

Thunder rolled again—but this time, quieter. Less violent. As if the storm itself were listening now.

Rio stared at Agatha, breathing hard, heart still racing. “This is crazy,” she said. “I’m not—I’m not like you. I’m just… me.”

Agatha’s expression didn’t change. She simply lifted her hand, palm open, not forcing anything. Just offering. “You’re you. And that is enough.”

Rio’s eyes flicked to her hand. Hesitation warred with need inside her chest.

“I’m scared,” she whispered.

“I know.” Agatha’s voice was velvet over stone—gentle but unwavering. “But you don’t have to be alone in it.”

Rio stared a moment longer. Then—fingers trembling—she reached for Agatha’s hand.

The moment their skin met, something shifted.

A soft pulse of golden light bloomed from the point of contact, not blinding or loud, but warm. Safe. The light spiraled between their fingers like threads being woven. Rio sucked in a sharp breath, but this time it wasn’t from fear.

It was wonder.

Outside, the wind softened. The rain tapered. The angry rattle of thunder faded into distant, harmless rumbling.

The house stilled.

Agatha stepped in closer, hand still holding Rio’s, the other reaching to gently brush a strand of hair from her face.

“You’re okay,” she murmured. “Just breathe. That’s all you need to do right now.”

And Rio did.

Slowly. One breath. Then another. She didn’t even realize she was crying until Agatha reached up to wipe away the tear that slipped down her cheek.

“I don’t understand any of this,” Rio whispered. “It doesn’t make sense.”

“It doesn’t have to. Not yet,” Agatha said. “It's okay. You’re not alone.”

Rio blinked at her, eyes wide, the storm finally ebbing inside her chest. “How do you do that?”

Agatha tilted her head. “Do what?”

“Make everything stop feeling so impossible.”

Agatha let out a soft, tired smile. “Magic, maybe.”

Rio laughed weakly, barely a breath. Then she squeezed Agatha’s hand, just a little, just enough.

And somewhere in the corner of the room, a tiny wildflower bloomed right through the cracks of the old wooden floor.

Rio stared at the wildflower breaking through the floorboards—small, delicate, pale blue with petals like watercolor strokes. It shouldn’t have been there. Not in this room, not this time of year, not after a storm.

She looked back at Agatha, voice barely above a whisper. “Did I do that?”

Agatha followed her gaze, and her expression shifted into something reverent. Not surprise, not fear—but awe. She gave a single nod. “Yes.”

Rio swayed slightly where she stood, and Agatha tightened her grip on her hand, steadying her.

“I didn’t mean to.”

“You didn’t need to,” Agatha said softly. “Magic responds to emotion. To connection. You’re just… beginning to see what you’ve always carried.”

Rio let out a shaky breath and dropped her gaze, as if embarrassed. “What if I don’t want it? Any of this?”

Agatha didn’t let go. “That’s your choice. But it’s still part of you. Denying it won’t erase it. It’ll only make it harder to control.”

A pause lingered between them. The room had gone quiet now, the storm reduced to a soft patter of rain on the roof, like the sky had finally taken a breath too.

Rio looked down at their still-joined hands, then finally pulled hers back—not sharply, but gently, as if she were afraid she’d burn from too much contact. “You said I wasn’t alone,” she said, still not looking up. “Does that mean you’ll help me? Even if I’m bad at this? Even if I never get the hang of it?”

Agatha’s eyes searched her face for a long beat. Then, quiet but sure: “Yes.”

Rio blinked. “Even if I don’t want to be part of whatever magical society or weird sacred order you belong to?”

Agatha smirked, a hint of that usual fire back in her eyes. “Especially then.”

That earned a soft laugh from Rio—short, surprised. “You really think this is funny?”

“No,” Agatha said, but her voice was warm. “I think it’s beautiful. And terrifying. But you’re still standing. That counts for something.”

Rio looked away, toward the little bloom still growing between the floorboards. “I feel like I should be more freaked out.”

“You were. About five minutes ago.”

Rio turned back to her, lips quirking. “Right. I forgot—you’re keeping score.”

“I’m very good at scorekeeping.”

There was something in her tone that made Rio look a little longer than she meant to. Something fond and unexpected blooming just behind the sarcasm. She folded her arms, suddenly aware of how close they were standing, how quiet the house had gotten.

“I should probably… go back to bed,” she said, almost reluctantly.

Agatha nodded, just once, and gestured toward the bed. “Okay. I'm right next door if you need anything. Try not to make any more flowers bloom in the hallway,” Agatha grinned, winking at her teasingly.

“No promises,” Rio grinned weakly, following her to the door.

Agatha chuckled under her breath. 

“Hey, Agatha?”

Agatha raised an eyebrow. “Yes?”

“Thanks. For stopping the storm. And for… not running away screaming.”

Agatha looked at her for a long, unreadable moment. “I didn’t stop the storm. You did.” Then she added softly, “I’d never run from you, Rio.”

That left Rio standing stunned for a second too long, heart pounding in a whole new rhythm now. She nodded mutely, slipping into the room.

Agatha didn’t say anything else—just reached out, gently touched the frame of the door, and the lights dimmed as if cued by her presence alone.

Then she was gone, leaving Rio with her thoughts, her barely-stitched-together sense of reality, and a storm that had finally gone still inside her.

~*~

The scent of coffee hit Rio before she opened her eyes.

Not the harsh, burnt kind from the ancient machine she’d salvaged for her own kitchen. This was smoother, richer—something that made her think of dark beans freshly ground, of hands that knew how to coax the best out of everything they touched.

She blinked awake slowly, the light already spilling through the window in thin golden lines across the guest room. Everything was warm. Soft. Safe.

And then she remembered.

The storm. The panic. The flower. Magic.

Agatha .

Rio sat up abruptly, the throw blanket sliding off her shoulders. The memory of Agatha’s hands on hers, of the way her voice had cut through the chaos like a lighthouse beam, returned like a wave—followed by the one that mattered most:

I’d never run from you.

“Jesus,” she muttered under her breath.

She swung her legs off the side of the bed, and made her way toward the bathroom. After splashing her face with cold water and stealing one of the soft towels hanging by the sink, she took a breath and looked at herself in the mirror.

Hair a mess. Eyes a little wide. But she didn’t look broken. Just… different.

Awake.

The scent of coffee hadn’t faded. So she followed it.

The house was quiet save for the low hum of music coming from a speaker somewhere—a piano instrumental, mellow and slow, curling through the air like smoke. Agatha’s house felt lived-in in a way Rio hadn’t noticed the night before. There were books stacked on every surface, half-used candles, a coat draped over the back of a chair like it hadn’t quite made it to the hook.

And in the kitchen, there she was.

Agatha Harkness. Dressed in a loose black sweater and soft pants, hair half-pinned up, a mug in one hand and a spatula in the other as she flipped something on a skillet. She glanced over her shoulder when Rio walked in, raising one brow with the casual ease of someone who made breakfast after mystical breakdowns all the time.

“Morning, sleeping beauty,” she said, her voice light but kind.

Rio rubbed the back of her neck. “I… didn’t mean to sleep in so late.”

“You nearly conjured a hurricane last night. I think you earned a few extra hours of sleep.”

Rio let out a soft laugh and leaned against the doorframe. “You make that sound normal.”

Agatha turned back to the pan. “Normal’s a moving target around here.”

There was a pause. Then Rio asked, a little quieter, “You always this calm the morning after a storm?”

Agatha didn’t turn around, but there was a smile in her voice. “Only when I know it’s passed.”

She slid something onto a plate and set it on the kitchen island. Toast. Eggs. Something that looked like sautéed mushrooms.

“For you. You need something in your system.”

Rio moved forward slowly, looking at the plate like it might disappear. “Didn’t know you were the nurturing type.”

Agatha poured her a cup of coffee. “Don’t spread it around. I do have a reputation to uphold.”

Rio took the mug with a grin, their fingers brushing again—just lightly this time. Enough to make the light above them flicker, like it was holding its breath.

She looked up at Agatha. “So that’s going to keep happening?”

Agatha finally met her gaze. “For now. Until you learn how to control it. Or direct it.”

“And you’re gonna teach me how?”

“I’ll try,” Agatha said. “If you let me.”

Rio stared at her for a long moment, heart unspooling a little more with every second. Then she nodded once and sat down with her breakfast, steam rising in lazy spirals from her plate.

Agatha poured herself another mug of coffee and sat across from her.

For now, that was enough.

~*~

The quiet between them had just started to settle into something comfortable again when the front door flung open with a gust of cold wind, and Wanda Maximoff stormed in like she was the weather.

Agatha stood so fast her chair scraped back across the floor.

“Wanda?” she snapped, eyes narrowing. “What the hell—?”

“Did you feel that storm last night?” Wanda didn’t wait to be invited in. She shed her red coat in a huff and tossed it over the back of a nearby chair like she owned the place. Her hair was still damp, her boots tracked in mud, and she looked frazzled in a way Agatha hadn’t seen since the last time a hex went sideways.

“Yes, I felt it,” Agatha said, folding her arms. “I was in it.”

“I thought something was wrong, I almost called—”

She stopped short, mid-sentence. Her eyes had just landed on Rio.

Rio, still sitting at the kitchen table in one of Agatha’s oversized flannel shirts, a half-drunk mug of coffee in front of her.

Wanda blinked. Once. Then her eyes slowly cut back to Agatha. “ Seriously ?”

Agatha cleared her throat. “Wanda, you remember Rio.”

“We met. Briefly.” Wanda gave Rio a once-over—less judgmental, more curious. She stepped closer. “You were at the apothecary. Bought lavender, right?”

“Uh,” Rio said, caught between defensive and awkward. “Yeah. That was me.”

Wanda turned back to Agatha, voice low but still sharp. “You didn’t think to mention that she was here when I showed up?”

“You didn’t give me a chance, did you? You just barged in.”

“She’s literally the eye of the storm.”

Agatha winced. Rio straightened in her chair. “Excuse me?”

Wanda looked back at her, then sighed and dropped the edge. “Sorry. That was rude. It’s just…” She looked at Agatha again. “You tell her yet?”

“Part of it,” Agatha said. “That’s how the storm happened.”

Rio glanced between them, brow furrowed. “Okay. Someone needs to start explaining things to me like I’m five. Because I’m clearly missing chapters in this magic manual.”

Wanda studied her for a moment.

“She knows she’s a witch,” Agatha said quickly. “She doesn’t know… all the rest.”

Wanda’s eyes gleamed. “Oh. Oh . This is  gonna be fun .”

Agatha shot her a look.

Wanda just smiled at Rio, more kindly this time. “Welcome to the madness, sweetheart. Oh, you have no idea what you got yourself into.”

Agatha stood abruptly and muttered something about needing a word with Wanda before grabbing her friend by the elbow and steering her out of the kitchen and into the living room. Rio stayed frozen in her chair, only half-listening as Mr. Scratchy, Agatha’s pet rabbit, hopped onto her lap and settled in comfortably. She didn’t mean to eavesdrop. Not really. But the cottage was old, the walls thin, and neither of them had closed the door behind them.

“You didn’t,” Wanda was whispering, delighted. “You didn’t.”

“Wanda—”

“Oh my God, you did! Didn’t I tell you it would happen? I told you!”

“We didn’t,” Agatha hissed, her tone low and firm. “Nothing happened. She stayed the night because it was starting to storm, and I wasn’t about to let her drive home drunk and emotionally unstable.”

“You didn’t even kiss her?”

“No!”

“Shame,” Wanda muttered. “You’ve been out of the game so long I thought you were going to combust. I figured if anything could break the spell, it would be a beautiful woman with enormous eyes and the temperament of a bristling cat.”

“Wanda.”

“Okay, okay.” A beat. “But you want to.”

Agatha didn’t answer right away.

Rio swallowed hard and quickly looked away, heart thudding so loudly she was sure Mr. Scratchy could hear it.

She wasn’t trying to listen. Not trying. But… well. Now that she had.

Her fingers tightened around her mug as her mind spun in a dozen directions at once. She wasn’t blind. Of course she’d noticed Agatha. How could she not? That woman walked into a room like she owned it, all quiet confidence and glacial elegance. The blue of her eyes was criminal—like summer skies after a storm, and the depths of an ocean you could drown in if you weren’t careful. And don’t even get her started on that long, dark hair or the way her voice had a way of curling around words like secrets she wasn’t ready to share.

And then there was the other stuff. The little things. The dry humor. The sharpness. The way she’d looked at Rio like she saw her—even when Rio had barely seen herself these last few months.

Yeah, she had definitely noticed Agatha Harkness.

And now she couldn’t stop wondering what it would feel like if something had happened.

She ran a hand through her hair and muttered under her breath. “Jesus Christ, Vidal. Get it together.”

Mr. Scratchy twitched his nose at her, unbothered.

~*~

Agatha returned to the kitchen first, followed closely by Wanda, whose grin could only be described as… unholy.

Rio tried to pretend she hadn’t heard a word. That she had to stop herself from pressing her ear to the wall like some lovesick idiot, heart hammering in her throat. That her ears weren’t still burning, or that she wasn’t gripping her coffee mug like it might save her from spontaneously combusting.

She kept her gaze fixed on the table, on the faded rings of old water damage in the wood grain. If she looked up—if she looked up—she’d have to see Agatha, and she wasn’t sure she was ready for that yet.

“Oh,” Wanda said, a little too brightly, “this is cozy. Morning coffee with a bunny and a—”

She didn’t finish.

Rio didn’t have to look to know Agatha had shot her one of those looks. Whatever it was, it worked like a spell, because Wanda immediately went silent.

“Okay, okay,” Wanda said, chuckling as she backed off, hands raised. “I should go. Leave you two to whatever it is you were doing before I rudely interrupted,” She winked— winked —at Rio, and then padded off down the hall, still humming under her breath like this was all the most delightful thing she’d ever seen.

The second the door clicked shut behind her, Rio let out a breath she didn’t know she’d been holding.

Agatha moved around the kitchen like nothing was out of place, graceful and calm, pouring herself a fresh cup of coffee. She leaned back against the counter, one hand tucked in her pocket, the other lifting the mug to her lips.

Like none of it had happened.

Like she hadn’t just healed Rio the night before with magic—actual magic—and like Rio hadn’t just overheard Wanda gleefully assuming they’d slept together.

“So,” Agatha said at last, in that maddeningly even tone, “you and Mr. Scratchy seem to have bonded.”

Rio glanced down at the rabbit still lounging in her lap like he paid rent. His eyes were half-lidded with contentment, and she swore he smirked when she looked at him.

“Yeah, I guess I’ve been chosen,” she muttered.

Agatha took a sip of her coffee. “He’s not usually that affectionate. Must be something about you.”

Rio’s pulse stuttered. She looked up, slowly.

Agatha’s face gave away nothing. But there was something in her eyes—just a flicker—like she knew. Like she definitely knew Rio had heard every word of that conversation and was now doing mental gymnastics just to hold eye contact.

“Maybe it’s the storm residue,” Rio offered weakly.

“Maybe,” Agatha echoed.

A silence stretched between them, heavier than before. But it wasn’t uncomfortable. Not exactly. Just… full.

Rio didn’t know what she’d expected, waking up in Agatha’s house after the night they’d had. But she hadn’t expected this: sitting here in her oversized borrowed t-shirt, with Agatha across the room, looking like the storm hadn’t touched her at all.

She still looked like an enchantress pulled straight from a dream. Or a fever. Or both.

Rio swallowed.

She didn’t know what was happening. Not really. But she knew this—whatever this was—wasn’t over.

Not yet.

And maybe… maybe she didn’t want it to be.

~*~

Agatha insisted on walking with her. It wasn’t a long way—just down the winding street to where Rio had left her car near the bar they’d met in the night before—but something about the gesture made Rio’s chest feel tight in a strange, quiet way.

They didn’t talk much. The sun was just beginning to peek through the thick cloud cover, everything smelling of damp earth and fresh rain. The town was still, as if holding its breath after the storm.

Their footsteps crunched over the gravel, and every so often, Agatha’s arm would brush against Rio’s.

Rio tried not to think about the night before. The lightning. The warmth of Agatha’s hand pressed to her skin. The way she’d said “I couldn’t not.”

But the memory was persistent, curling around the edges of her thoughts like smoke.

When they reached the car, Rio turned to face her, one hand on the door handle. “Thanks for… you know, everything,” she said, not sure how else to put it.

Agatha nodded, that unreadable expression back on her face. “You’re welcome.” Then, after a pause: “Take it easy today.”

Rio gave a small smile, not quite meeting her eyes. “I’ll try.”

Neither of them moved for a beat too long. Then Agatha stepped back, and Rio slid into the driver’s seat. As she pulled away, she glanced into the rearview mirror.

Agatha was still there, standing in the gray morning light, arms folded loosely across her chest, watching her go.

~*~

The drive back was quiet. The trees lining the road swayed gently in the post-storm breeze, and the ground sparkled with dew and fallen petals. Rio tapped her fingers against the steering wheel, the silence in the car louder than any thunder had been the night before.

When she turned the last corner toward the cottage, she felt it before she saw him.

Salem was waiting on the porch.

The gray cat sat with the same regal indifference as ever, his tail curled neatly around his paws, his green eyes fixed on the drive like he’d known exactly when she’d pull in.

Rio blinked and let out a breath of something between a laugh and a sigh. “Of course you’re there,” she murmured.

She parked, grabbed her keys, and jogged up the path—only to curse under her breath when the porch creaked under her weight, a few warped boards visibly splintering under her feet. The storm had done a number on it, and her body weight alone made it feel like one strong wind might finish the job.

Salem trotted toward the door, unbothered by the damage. Rio followed more cautiously, her eyes scanning the loose nails and bowed planks.

“Okay,” she muttered. “You win. I’ll find someone in town to fix it. This thing’s going to collapse and take me with it if I don’t.”

Salem meowed once in response and darted inside the moment she opened the door, like he owned the place.

Maybe he did.

Rio lingered in the doorway for a second, hand resting on the frame as she looked out toward the trees, still shivering in the breeze.

Something had changed.

Not just the porch. Not just the storm.

Something in her.

And she didn’t know what it meant yet.

But she knew she wasn’t alone in it anymore.

~*~

Inside, the cottage felt warm and welcoming in a way Rio hadn’t fully appreciated before. Maybe it was just the contrast from the charged air of Agatha’s house, or maybe it was the presence of a gray cat now weaving around her legs like he belonged there.

She kicked off her boots and let her bag fall with a soft thud by the door, stretching her arms over her head as she yawned. Her body ached in all the places leftover adrenaline always left behind—shoulders tight, neck sore, something deeper in her chest still humming.

Salem jumped up onto the kitchen chair and meowed once, expectantly.

“Okay, okay,” she said, padding barefoot across the wood floor. “Breakfast coming right up.”

She found the bag of food she’d picked up yesterday, opened it, and poured a generous helping into a ceramic dish she’d bought on impulse. The cat sniffed it once before digging in like he hadn’t eaten in days.

Rio leaned against the counter, arms crossed, watching him. “You really are something else,” she murmured.

He flicked an ear but didn’t stop eating.

She made herself some coffee, the familiar routine of it grounding her, and then walked back toward the kitchen table, mug in hand.

That’s when she saw it.

There was a book sitting in the center of the table.

She froze.

It hadn’t been there when she left.

She would’ve noticed—it was thick and old-looking, with a cracked leather cover and yellowed pages that looked like they’d lived through decades. Maybe longer. The front was embossed with delicate symbols that shimmered faintly when the sunlight touched them just right.

And there, scrawled on the inside of the cover in looping, old-fashioned script:

Eliza Vidal.

Rio’s breath caught in her throat.

She hadn’t seen this before. Not once, in all the hours spent exploring the cottage, in all the times she’d looked through drawers and cabinets for any trace of her family. It had never turned up. Not even once.

She set the mug down slowly and reached out, fingertips brushing the worn cover.

Salem hopped up onto the table beside it, tail curling around his paws again. His green eyes locked with hers like he knew exactly what this was.

And maybe he did.

Rio opened the book.

The pages smelled like herbs and smoke and old paper. Inside, the words were handwritten in fading ink, the margins filled with doodles of plants and stars, symbols and notes in a tidy, elegant script. It wasn’t just a book.

It was a grimoire.

Her chest tightened again—but this time, not with fear. With something more complex. Wonder. Curiosity. A sliver of longing.

Eliza’s grimoire.

Her family.

Rio sat down slowly, flipping the first page with care. Salem lay beside the book, his body warm and steady against her arm.

Outside, the wind rustled the trees softly.

Inside, Rio began to read.

 

Chapter 8: Eight

Saturday afternoon at the apothecary was usually quiet, and Agatha liked it that way. She had come in, tended to a few customers and closed around 4pm. A nice, easy day in her book.

Agatha had just locked up the apothecary for the afternoon and was tucking her keys into her coat pocket when she heard it—the high, chirpy laugh that made her shoulders tense without fail.

“Agatha Harkness!”

She turned, just in time to see Mayor Sharon Davis bustling across the street in a tailored pastel coat and kitten heels that somehow managed not to trip her on the cobblestones. Her hair was its usual fluffy halo of short, butter-blonde curls, and a silk scarf fluttered dramatically around her neck like she was expecting to be painted mid-step.

“Mayor Davis,” Agatha said, forcing a smile as Sharon approached like a runaway shopping cart.

“Out and about, I see,” Sharon chirped, looping an arm through Agatha’s like they were old friends. “It’s not often I catch you during business hours.”

“I was just closing up,” Agatha said, slipping her arm free as gently as she could. “Busy day.”

“Oh, I don’t doubt it. All that tincture brewing and potion-making—I imagine you’re practically a cauldron yourself by the end of it.”

Agatha had to control the urge to roll her eyes. “Something like that.”

Sharon gave another one of her trademark staccato laughs and leaned in conspiratorially, lowering her voice but not enough to be subtle. “I hear you and the new girl in town are spending an awful lot of time together these days.”

Agatha’s posture stiffened slightly. “Word travels fast.”

“Of course it does.” Sharon beamed. “Small town. You know how it is. People notice things. Like the fact that she left your house early this morning?”

She gave a knowing wink that made Agatha want to hex something. But instead, she smoothed her face into something neutral and shrugged.

“She needed a place to wait out the storm.”

“Mmm,” Sharon hummed. “I’m sure it was all very cozy. Well. I do hope she’s settling in. We’ve all got high hopes for Ms. Vidal.”

Agatha narrowed her eyes, just slightly. “Have we?”

Sharon’s smile didn’t flicker, but there was something in her gaze—something just slightly too sharp. “Well, you know me. I like to see newcomers welcomed properly. Integration is important.”

Agatha didn’t respond, just watched her, silent and still.

“Anyway,” Sharon said brightly, breaking the moment with another clap of her hands. “I won’t keep you. Just wanted to say hello. You know where to find me if you need anything.”

“I’m sure I’ll manage,” Agatha replied.

Sharon’s laughter followed her down the street like the tail end of a bad spell, lingering longer than it had any right to.

Agatha watched her go, brow furrowing. Sharon was always a bit much, but something about the whole interaction felt… off. Like the air before a lightning strike.

She slipped her hands into her pockets and started toward her car, muttering under her breath.

“Gods, that woman gives me hives.”

~*~

It was just past seven when Agatha found herself standing on Rio Vidal’s battered porch, a paper bag balanced in one arm, the other hand raised to knock—but hesitating. The porch creaked beneath her boots, groaning like it resented the company. The storm had definitely made things worse; one of the railings hung askew, and a board by the step looked ready to give at the slightest weight. She made a mental note to come back and fix it herself if Rio didn’t call someone soon.

Salem’s eyes appeared in the window, glowing faintly in the dim light, as if he’d known she was coming.

She knocked.

A moment passed, then the sound of footsteps on hardwood. The door opened, and there stood Rio, barefoot in a pair of loose plaid pajama pants and an oversized tee, hair pulled up in a messy bun that still managed to look infuriatingly good on her. Her eyes widened slightly in surprise.

“Agatha?”

“I come bearing gifts,” Agatha said dryly, lifting the bag. “Don’t read into it.”

Rio blinked, then stepped back, opening the door wider. “Are you here to tell me I’ve developed a tail or started speaking in tongues or something?”

Agatha gave a short huff of a laugh as she walked in. “No. Just… checking in. It was a lot to dump on someone in one night.”

Rio shut the door and turned to her, brows raised. “So you brought…?”

“A care package.” Agatha set the bag on the kitchen table and began unpacking it with casual precision. “Lavender tea. A little protective charm to keep your dreams calm. And chocolate. Obviously.”

Rio blinked, visibly caught off guard. “You brought me chocolate?”

“I’m not a monster.” Agatha shrugged. “Plus, I had an extra bar in the cupboard.”

“That’s…” Rio trailed off, then offered a tentative smile. “That’s actually really thoughtful. Thank you.”

Agatha waved it off, already regretting coming. “Just figured you might be spiraling.”

Rio leaned back against the counter, crossing her arms. “You wouldn’t be wrong. I tried to write or draw today and all I could think about was… glowing hands. And thunderstorms. And my leg healing in front of my eyes like I was in an episode of The X-Files.”

Agatha tilted her head. “Were you Agent Scully or the monster of the week?”

Rio gave a tired laugh. “Jury’s still out.”

They stood in silence for a beat. Salem brushed up against Agatha’s leg and she glanced down, arching a brow.

“He likes you,” Rio said, watching them. 

“He has good taste,” Agatha murmured, scratching behind his ears.

Another pause.

“Do you want some tea?” Rio asked. “I feel like you don’t do herbal, but I have black.”

“I’ll take whatever you’re having,” Agatha replied. 

Rio nodded and went to put the kettle on, her movements a little more relaxed now.

Agatha watched her for a long moment, then said, almost too casually, “You’re handling this better than most people would.”

“I’m not sure I am,” Rio said softly, not looking up. “But you showing up like this? It definitely helps.”

Agatha looked away, not used to being the one that helped. “Well. Good.”

~*~

The tea had steeped just long enough to fill the cottage with a soft, herbal warmth. Rio handed Agatha a mug, their fingers brushing, and the overhead light gave a tiny flicker. Neither of them mentioned it. It was starting to feel… normal. Or, at least, normal for them.

They settled into the living room, Rio curling up on the couch with one leg tucked under her, Agatha taking the armchair across from her, a blanket tossed casually over her lap. Salem had claimed the rug in front of the fireplace, paws tucked beneath him like a little gray loaf. Occasionally, he’d flick an ear or glance at Agatha with his gleaming green eyes, but he seemed more content than curious.

The quiet was a companion for a while—comfortable, easy. The storm had passed. The air felt clearer, even with the weight of the day still hovering.

Rio took a sip of her tea, then set the mug down. “So, something kind of weird happened this morning.”

Agatha lifted a brow, her lips curving into something between a smirk and genuine interest. “Weirder than the part where I casually told you you’re a witch?”

“Comparable,” Rio said dryly, then hesitated. “I found a book. On my kitchen table. Just… sitting there.”

Agatha straightened, setting her own tea aside. “What kind of book?”

“Old. Leatherbound. Beautiful, honestly. And it had a name inside—Eliza Vidal.”

Agatha blinked. “You’re sure?”

“I don’t exactly know a lot of people named Eliza Vidal, so yeah. Pretty sure.”

The shift in Agatha’s expression was subtle, but Rio noticed it. She was getting good at noticing her now—the quiet way her emotions moved behind her eyes, the slight tensing of her shoulders.

“That was her grimoire,” Agatha said, voice low. “Her personal book of magic. Her spells. Her thoughts. Her legacy.”

“I’d never seen it before,” Rio said, a little too quickly. “It was just there. I swear it wasn’t there before. And I don’t exactly have a shelf labeled ‘Witchcraft, Probably’.”

“I believe you.” Agatha leaned forward, elbows on her knees. “That book finding you? That’s not an accident.”

Rio stared at her, unsettled. “Books don’t find people, Agatha.”

“They do,” Agatha said, “when they’re tied to blood and power. When they’re waiting.”

Rio’s voice dropped to a whisper. “You think it was waiting for me?”

Agatha nodded slowly. “I think it was waiting for you to be ready.”

There was a beat of silence, save for the soft crackle of the fireplace and Salem’s barely audible purr.

“When I opened it,” Rio said, “it felt like something recognized me. Like the book breathed when I touched it.”

Agatha didn’t speak for a long moment. Then, softly: “It did.”

Rio glanced down at her hands, then back up. “What does that mean?”

Agatha met her gaze, steady and warm. “It means you’re not just someone who stumbled into a storm or dreamed a cat into existence. You’re part of this. And that book… it’s only the beginning.”

Rio leaned back against the cushions, exhaling slowly. Salem hopped up beside her, curling into her side as if he’d done it a thousand times before.

She rested a hand on his back, fingers brushing his fur. “I thought I was going crazy.”

“You’re not,” Agatha said gently. “But your world is about to get a whole lot stranger.”

~*~

The fire cracked softly in the hearth, casting golden shadows across the living room. Agatha sat curled at one end of the couch, legs tucked beneath her, her gaze steady but unreadable. Salem had claimed the other corner, his dark body a quiet, watchful shape against the cushions.

Rio suddenly straightened. “Wait—I left it in the kitchen.”

Agatha blinked. “The grimoire?”

Rio nodded, pushing the blanket off her legs. She stood and padded barefoot toward the kitchen.

Agatha watched Rio disappear into the kitchen with the quiet grace of someone still unsure of their footing. The storm from the night before had passed, but there was a different kind of electricity now—settled just under Rio’s skin. Agatha could feel it humming between them, low and insistent.

She exhaled and leaned back slightly on the couch, her gaze drifting to the fire. Salem remained curled up at the other end, watching her with those unnervingly perceptive green eyes. “Don’t look at me like that,” she murmured, just as Rio returned.

And gods help her—there it was.

The grimoire.

Agatha sat forward instinctively as Rio approached, the weight of the book heavy in her arms. Even from where she sat, Agatha could feel the pulse of old magic emanating from it—familiar, wild, unmistakable.

Eliza’s.

Rio sat down beside her, close enough that their knees almost touched, and laid the grimoire between them like it was something sacred. And in a way, it was.

“I barely opened it,” Rio said quietly, her voice edged with hesitation and wonder. 

Agatha set her mug down on the little wooden side table and glanced toward the grimoire resting on the couch beside Rio, its leather cover now dulled with age but still pulsing faintly beneath the candlelight. The thing had weight—not just physical, but emotional, magical. An echo of someone long gone and a future not yet shaped.

Rio reached for it, fingers brushing the edge. “I figured we could go through some of it, maybe. Together?”

There was something hesitant in her voice, something almost hopeful, and Agatha felt it like a pressure behind her ribs. She hesitated, then sat forward slightly, hands clasped between her knees.

“I want to,” she said gently. “And I will. But Rio… a grimoire is deeply personal. It’s not just a spellbook or some kind of manual. It’s your bloodline speaking to you. Your family’s magic, shaped through their eyes. It was meant for you. ”

Rio looked down at it, brows furrowed. “But I don’t even know what I’m supposed to do with it.”

“I know.” Agatha softened. “Which is why I think you need a moment with it. Alone. To see what it wants to show you first. Once you’ve had that, once it’s opened to you in the way only it can… then I can help you make sense of it. I’ll answer whatever you want to ask. I promise.”

There was silence, but not the uncomfortable kind. Rio nodded slowly, dragging her fingers along the spine of the book before carefully setting it down again, like it might shatter if handled wrong.

“That makes sense,” she said at last. “It feels like it’s waiting for me to do something anyway.”

Agatha stood, stretching a little. “Then let it. When you’re ready, I’ll be here.”

Their eyes met across the space, something unspoken hanging in the air between them again. Not quite longing. Not quite fear. Something more fragile, and infinitely more dangerous.

~*~

The wind had quieted, replaced by the delicate hush of snow brushing against the windows, slow and lazy. It fell in thick flakes, blanketing the trees and roof in a soft white hush. Inside the cottage, the fire crackled in its stone hearth, the warmth licking at the edges of the room but not quite enough to chase the chill.

Rio stood, padding barefoot across the rug. She caught Agatha’s eyes as she reached for a second wineglass from the open cabinet.

“Want some?” she asked, lifting the wine bottle from the counter.

Agatha, curled into the corner of the couch with her legs tucked beneath her, smiled in that calm, quiet way of hers—like she knew how the story would end, and she was simply watching it unfold. “Only if you’re having one.”

Rio poured. Handed her the glass. Then paused in front of the fireplace, arms folded. “We might need more logs.”

Agatha tilted her head slightly, then, without moving from her spot, lifted her hand. Just a slow wave through the air, fingers curling at the end like a conductor finishing a note.

The fire responded instantly.

It flared—not wildly, not dangerous, just brighter, fuller, the flames licking higher like they’d been waiting for her touch. The warmth rolled out toward them in a rush, wrapping the room in a golden glow.

Rio blinked.

Her mouth opened, then closed. Then opened again. “You just…”

Agatha arched one perfectly sculpted brow, the faintest hint of a smirk at the corner of her lips. “Added a log. Sort of.”

Rio stared at her for another beat, then dragged a hand through her hair and muttered, “Okay. Cool. No big deal. Just… casual firebending in my living room.”

“Technically, it’s not firebending,” Agatha said, taking a sip of wine. “But I’m flattered you think it’s that cool.”

“It is that cool,” Rio said, still stunned, her gaze flicking from Agatha’s fingers to the fireplace and back again. “God. You’re like… you’re like straight out of a fantasy movie or something.”

Agatha laughed at that—an actual laugh, low and warm and soft. “That might be the best compliment I’ve gotten in years.”

Rio sat down next to her, the heat from the flames now a steady hum against her skin. “This is going to take some getting used to.”

Agatha tilted her head toward her, blue eyes bright and steady. “I’ll help you. If you want me to.”

Rio met her gaze and held it. “Yeah. I think I do.”

~*~

Agatha sank deeper into the couch, her fingers curled around a glass of red wine as the snow outside thickened, soft and steady, turning the world beyond the windows into a blur of white. The fire crackled steadily in the hearth, painting everything in amber and gold. Across from her, Rio sat cross-legged on the rug, legs tucked under her, nursing her own glass with both hands like it was something sacred. She looked cozy, but alert—like a cat half-asleep and ready to bolt at the first sign of danger.

They were still circling each other in some way. Friends, yes—but edging toward something else. Agatha felt it like a second heartbeat.

“So,” she said lightly, swirling her wine, “Los Angeles. What was it like living in the land of sunshine and spiritually bankrupt smoothies?”

Rio laughed under her breath. “Honestly? Exhausting. Too much noise. Too many people pretending everything’s fine while they’re crumbling inside.”

Agatha tilted her head, intrigued. “Ah, a city full of liars.”

“And actors,” Rio added, smirking faintly. “Which is kind of the same thing.”

Agatha raised her glass in a mock toast. “To brutal honesty.”

Rio tapped her glass against Agatha’s and took a slow sip. “It wasn’t all bad. I had my studio. Friends. Alice. I just… lost my way, I guess. The breakup, the creative block. I kept waiting to feel like myself again, and then one day I realized I didn’t even know what that meant anymore.”

There was a vulnerability in her voice that tugged at Agatha unexpectedly, like a soft pull behind the ribs. She said nothing at first, just watched Rio’s face in the firelight. The shadows clung to her jawline, danced over her lips. She looked older than her years in that moment. Wiser, worn.

“Maybe you weren’t supposed to go back to the old version of yourself,” Agatha said quietly. “Maybe the old you was never the full story.”

Rio blinked at her, a flicker of something unreadable in her expression—but she didn’t have time to respond.

Rio’s phone was suddenly buzzing on the coffee table. Agatha glanced at the name: Alice Wu.

Rio let out a panicked, exasperated sound. “God, of course.”

Agatha arched a brow, sipping her wine. “Who’s that?”

Rio sighed, a short, exasperated laugh escaping her. “My best friend.”

“And you’re not going to accept her call?” Agatha asked curiously. 

“Trust me, she’s a lot. You don’t want to meet her like this.”

Agatha grinned. “ Oh, I beg to differ.”

Rio gave her a pleading look, but answered the FaceTime call anyway. Agatha caught the flash of the screen just as it connected—Alice’s face appeared in motion-blur, her voice already halfway into a sentence.

“—seriously, are you alive or did you get abducted by woodland cryptids? I haven’t heard from you in a couple of days and—wait. Wait .”

Alice’s face froze. Then her grin widened, teeth bared like a fox with a secret.

“Rio Marie Vidal. Who. Is. That.”

Rio muttered, “Alice, I swear to—”

“Is that the mysterious woman from the apothecary?” Alice’s eyes lit up. Rio blushed. “That is her, isn’t it? You did not tell me she was this hot.”

Agatha lifted a hand in a casual wave, amused. “Good evening.”

Alice blinked, then let out a delighted laugh. “She has a voice, too. Are you kidding me, Rio?”

Rio looked like she wanted the floorboards to crack open and swallow her whole. “This is Agatha. We’re just hanging out.”

“Uh-huh,” Alice said, narrowing her eyes at the background. “With wine. And a fire. On a Saturday night. In your little haunted cottage in the woods.”

“It’s not haunted.”

“That’s not what you said when the pipes made those death-rattle noises.”

Agatha’s lips twitched. “The ambiance is charming.”

Alice leaned even closer to her camera. “Okay, but for real—Rio, you have your soft voice on. The one you only use when you like someone.”

“I do not.”

“You do,” Alice said. “It’s the same one you used when you had that three-month crush on your barista and couldn’t order coffee without blushing.”

Agatha turned, full of interest now. “ Oh ? Do tell.”

“No, don’t,” Rio said, mortified, but her voice cracked with laughter. “Please ignore her. She’s impossible.”

Agatha found herself smiling. “I think I like her.”

“She’s dangerous.”

“Dangerous how?” Alice cut in. “Do you mean charismatic, funny, and deeply insightful?”

Rio exhaled hard, clearly resigning herself to the chaos. “Can we not do this right now?”

Agatha, of course, was already filing away every detail. Rio’s flushed cheeks. The sleeve-fiddling. The fact that she looked absolutely helpless when flustered—endearing and sharp and soft in all the ways Agatha didn’t expect.

Alice sighed dramatically, clearly enjoying herself. “Fine, fine. I’ll let you get back to your very cozy, very suspiciously romantic-sounding night with Miss Gorgeous-Dark-and-Witchy.”

Agatha blinked. “Witchy?”

“Vibe,” Alice said quickly. “Witchy vibe. You know. Mysterious. Smarter than everyone. It’s a thing. I mean it as a compliment.”

Agatha smirked. “I’ll take it.”

Alice winked. “You should. It was really nice to meet you, Agatha.”

“You too, Alice. I’d love to hear more about Rio’s tells some other time,” Agatha teased and Rio groaned.

“Bye, Alice!” Rio finally ended the call with a sigh, setting the phone face-down on the table.

Agatha leaned in slightly, one brow raised. “You’re very cute when you panic.”

“Don’t.”

“Mm.” She sipped her wine. “I’m serious.”

Rio groaned and flopped back on the rug, staring up at the ceiling. “She’s never going to let this go.”

“I certainly hope not,” Agatha said, voice smooth. “She’s a goldmine of information.”

Rio shot her a look. “You’re enjoying this.”

“Immensely.”

For a moment, silence settled between them again—but it wasn’t uncomfortable. If anything, it was warmer now, laced with a new current of awareness. Agatha watched the way Rio’s eyes flicked toward the fire, then back to her wineglass, then—briefly—to Agatha herself, before darting away again.

Definitely a sleeve-tugger.

She tucked that knowledge away like a secret, smiling softly into her glass.

~*~

The phone was facedown now, the chaotic energy of Alice Wu gone with it, but the echo of her mischief lingered in the room like the remnants of a spell. Agatha watched Rio carefully as she took another sip of wine, her expression still tinged with secondhand embarrassment, but her body had relaxed into something quieter. Something open.

Agatha waited a moment before speaking, letting the fire crackle between them.

“So,” she said softly, “Los Angeles.”

Rio blinked, then gave a small nod, curling her legs beneath her as she joined Agatha on the couch, her body turned to her. “What about it?”

“You mentioned you hated it,” Agatha said. “I’m curious how you ended up there in the first place.”

There was a flicker in Rio’s eyes—guarded, thoughtful. She looked into her glass like it might hold the answer. Then, after a beat, she exhaled a slow breath and glanced over at Agatha.

“You ever have one of those moments where you just… go? Without thinking too hard about what it means?” she asked. “I was twenty-two. Had some money saved. I’d just gotten my degree and the city felt like this—this vortex of possibility. Like if I stayed still, I’d drown.”

Agatha listened, the softness in Rio’s voice coaxing her to lean just slightly closer. Their knees brushed when Rio shifted, and neither of them moved away.

“Someone I knew back then said LA was the kind of place that either eats you alive or forces you to evolve. I thought I could evolve,” Rio said, her voice wry.

“Did you?”

A small smile played on Rio’s lips. “I think I did. Just not in the way I expected.”

There was a long pause. The fire cracked again, and this time, Rio leaned back against the couch cushions—closer, without meaning to, or maybe meaning to just enough. Her arm stretched out over the back of the couch and pressed lightly against Agatha’s, just a whisper of contact, but it sent a slow spark across Agatha’s skin. She didn’t pull away. In fact, she angled slightly toward it, her fingers brushing Rio’s wrist as she set her wineglass down on the side table.

Rio didn’t move.

Agatha kept her voice casual, but there was something more beneath it. “So you ran to LA to escape being stuck.”

Rio nodded and glanced over at her. “And you? Did you run here to disappear?”

Agatha arched a brow, amused by the reversal. “Would you believe me if I said no?”

“No,” Rio said, a smile tugging at her lips. “Not at all.”

Agatha chuckled, eyes drifting to the firelight dancing along the lines of Rio’s profile. “Fair enough.”

Their arms were still pressed together. A small, accidental touch—but not an unwelcome one.

Rio’s fingers tapped absently against her thigh, and Agatha noticed the way her gaze had gone slightly distant. Contemplative. Her voice, when it came again, was softer.

“I was tired of being invisible,” Rio said. “In foster care, you learn early how to make yourself smaller. Easier to ignore. It was survival. But LA… I thought maybe I could be seen there.”

Agatha’s chest tightened, her instinct to reach for her gently warring with the knowledge that now wasn’t the time to push. So instead, she simply said, “And were you?”

Rio looked at her. Their eyes met. A beat passed.

“I think I’m just now figuring out what that even means.”

Something in Agatha’s throat caught, but she masked it with a slow nod and a quiet murmur of agreement.

Rio’s voice was soft, careful. “What about you? You ever leave Thistle Grove?”

Agatha’s gaze drifted toward the fire, wine glass cradled loosely in her fingers. The snow outside painted soft light across the room, but it was the question that made the air shift.

“I did,” she said finally. “College in New York. Then… Med school. Columbia.”

Rio blinked, visibly surprised. “Med school?”

Agatha huffed a wry laugh. “Don’t sound so shocked. I used to be very impressive.”

“You are very impressive,” Rio said, without thinking. Her cheeks flushed almost immediately.

Agatha didn’t comment on that, but the corner of her mouth quirked. “I thought—if I already knew how to heal with magic, why not do it the regular way too? Help people beyond this town. Use everything I was born with, and everything I could learn.”

She paused, the smile fading. Her eyes darkened, but not in anger. Just memory.

“It didn’t go how I thought it would.”

Rio turned slightly, drawing her knees up beneath her on the couch. Her hand brushed against Agatha’s knee again, this time intentional. “What happened?”

Agatha’s voice dropped. “I couldn’t turn it off. The knowing. The way my magic works—it’s not just touching wounds and knitting them back together. It’s seeing. Outcomes. Possibilities. I’d touch a patient and know if they were going to die. Or suffer. Or recover, but never be whole.”

She picked up her glass and took a sip of her wine.

“And the worst part was—I couldn’t always do anything about it. Not without revealing what I was. Not without paying the price. Magic doesn’t just take from you—it takes from somewhere.”

Rio’s expression softened with something like awe. “That must’ve been… unbearable.”

Agatha’s gaze met hers, steady now. “It broke me, a little. To have the power and still feel helpless.”

Silence settled between them like snowfall—gentle, quiet, full of weight.

“I dropped out after my third year,” Agatha added after a moment. “Came back to Thistle Grove. Back to potions and insomnia and migraines and stubborn townies who don’t want to see a regular doctor. And that was… enough. Mostly.”

“Mostly?” Rio echoed.

Agatha’s eyes traced her face slowly. “There are still days I wonder if I could’ve done more. Been more. But I think I was trying to fix something in myself, more than anyone else.”

Rio didn’t say anything right away. She just shifted closer, her body angled toward Agatha’s, her fingers gently touching her hand. The contact was casual—but deliberate. Grounding.

“You’re not broken,” Rio said, and it wasn’t soft, or tentative. It was certain. “And maybe you didn’t become some fancy New York doctor, but you’re helping people here in ways no one else could. That’s not less. That’s… brave.”

Agatha let the words land, and for a moment, she didn’t feel like brushing them off. For a moment, she let them in.

The fire crackled again. The warmth spread.

Agatha didn’t move, but she felt the weight of the moment settle in her chest like a second heartbeat. Rio’s words had struck something inside her, something soft and unfamiliar. Not the praise—she was used to admiration, used to people thinking she was sharp or capable or intimidating.

But this—being seen as brave? It caught her off guard.

She could feel the warmth of Rio beside her, their arms brushing, her fingers slowly moving on her wrist. The fire threw flickering gold across Rio’s features, illuminating the quiet in her eyes, the way her lips parted slightly like she might say something else but thought better of it. Agatha’s gaze dropped for just a second to that mouth.

Gods, she was beautiful. Not in the sculpted, airbrushed way of magazines or screenplays, but in a way that felt real. Lived-in. Honest. There was something magnetic about her, something that pulled at Agatha in a way she hadn’t felt in years.

She could have leaned in. Just a little.

She didn’t.

Instead, she let her fingers graze Rio’s where they rested on the back of the couch. A light touch—feather soft. Intentional.

And like every time before, that touch bloomed magic.

The fire flared in the hearth, casting tall shadows that danced across the walls. A faint shimmer traced the space between their hands, golden light that pulsed once—like a heartbeat—and then faded.

Rio’s breath hitched, barely audible, and Agatha felt her own pulse respond. Neither of them pulled away.

But neither of them pushed forward, either.

She turned her head just enough to look at Rio again. “You have no idea how powerful you are,” she said, quietly.

Rio’s throat bobbed. Her voice was barely more than a whisper. “That’s the scary part.”

Agatha’s smile was small, a curve of understanding. “The most powerful things usually are.”

They sat like that for a long while. fingers touching. Hands nearly clasped. The fire crackled, and the snow whispered against the windows like a lullaby.

Eventually, Agatha stood, her joints aching slightly from the stillness. “I should get going,” she said softly. “You’ve had enough excitement for one night.”

Rio looked up at her, eyes darker now, like the storm outside had settled behind them. “You don’t have to rush off.”

Agatha hesitated. Then: “If I don’t, I might stay too long.”

Rio didn’t answer that.

Agatha reached for her coat. “Read the grimoire,” she said, her voice back to that dry murmur. “Let it speak to you. Then we’ll talk.”

“Okay.”

Agatha leaned down and pressed a gentle kiss to her cheek before making her way to the door. Turning back one last time. Rio was still sitting on the couch, fingers touching her cheek.

Agatha smiled faintly. “Goodnight, Rio.”

The door closed behind her.

And the spark still hung in the air.

 

Chapter 9: Nine

Chapter Notes

I just wanted to take the time to thank you for all your sweet, thoughtful comments. I really, really appreciate you taking the time to let me know what you think. I hope you enjoy this one.

Tell me all about it!

The cottage was quiet when Rio woke up the next morning. Still. A soft kind of quiet that felt layered and thoughtful, like the house itself was giving her space to think.

Outside, snow had dusted the trees in thin white veils, branches weighed down just enough to droop gently. She pulled on thick socks and made herself tea instead of coffee—her usual routine already felt too harsh, too grounded in a life that had no place for the things she now knew.

And the Grimoire waited.

It was still on the kitchen table where she’d left it last night, as if it had always belonged there. The air around it felt charged. Or maybe that was her imagination. Still, when she reached out to touch it, her fingers hovered for just a moment.

Then she opened it.

The handwriting was elegant and sharp, like the idea she had of the woman herself. Eliza Vidal. Her name felt heavier now. Not just a stranger’s anymore. A connection, however distant, to this strange new reality.

The first few entries were practical. Eliza’s handwriting was firm, unadorned. Notes about tinctures, lunar cycles, healing salves. There were sketches in the margins—leaves, roots, strange symbols. Some pages had been revisited and revised. Others were dated decades apart. Rio found herself lingering over the entries from the early 90s, especially the ones that mentioned her name. Not directly—but ‘my granddaughter’, or ‘D’s girl’, scrawled next to certain protective spells.

And then came the story.

It wasn’t titled. It was just… there, nestled between recipes and diagrams, like it had always belonged. A piece of something deeper.

“Long before the town, before the roads, before the sea was named, there were three sisters.

They were not born of flesh, but of storm and soil and flame.

They walked barefoot through the woods, braided rivers into rope, and taught the moon to rise.

The oldest sister healed with her hands. The second sang to the trees and they listened. The third burned down the night so the morning could be born.

They loved each other fiercely. But the world feared what they could do.

So one by one, the sisters were hunted, betrayed, broken. Until only one remained.

She split her soul into three Threads and wove them into the land.

And she whispered:

“Let those who love fiercely, feel deeply, and burn bright find me again.”

Rio read it slowly. Not skimming, not scanning—reading. Letting it settle.

As she read, a heat bloomed in her chest, slow and heavy. Not fire—something older. Something that moved through her like recognition. The language was simple, but the meaning felt huge. Impossible.

Her fingers trembled on the page. She pressed them flat.

Wild Thread.

Body Thread.

Flame Thread.

The illustrations that followed were strange and beautiful. Spirals, circles, tangled lines that seemed to vibrate on the page. One symbol appeared again and again—a simple triple spiral, vines curling around the outer rings. It pulsed behind her eyes like something half-remembered.

And then Eliza’s voice, raw and certain, filled the margins.

“This isn’t magic. It’s memory. It’s legacy. It’s blood.”

“When the Threads call, they do not ask. They take root. And they bloom where you break.”

Rio closed the book slowly, breath catching.

She looked at her hands. Nothing had changed—but something had shifted. The air around her was charged. The kind of quiet before a thunderstorm.

Outside, the snow kept falling.

She stood, pacing once, twice, then sank onto the couch beside the fire. Salem stretched but didn’t move. She tucked her legs beneath her and stared into the flames.

”Well, shit.”

~*~

Later that night, the fire was down to embers, casting slow-moving shadows across the floor. The grimoire lay on the table, closed but not silent. Rio could still feel it humming. Like a heart. Like a question she hadn’t yet dared to ask.

She’d spent the whole day thinking about the story. About the sisters born of storm and soil and flame. About the Thread of the Wild.

The second sister sang to the trees and they listened.

And Rio… she’d always drawn trees first. Always felt safest beneath them, felt a connection to them, to the earth beneath her feet, sketching bark and branch like it mattered more than people. Even now, if she closed her eyes, she could feel the roots of the sycamore outside the cottage. Not literally—but almost. Like it was a rhythm she could tap into.

She sat back against the couch and stared at the ceiling.

“This is ridiculous,” she whispered. “This is ridiculous.”

But her voice didn’t sound sure. And the house didn’t argue.

~*~

It began with the sound of waves, though she wasn’t near the ocean.

Rio stood at the edge of a forest, the sky above her a pale violet streaked with silver. The trees were impossibly tall, their branches swaying in a wind that didn’t touch her skin. Snowflakes drifted around her in slow, spiraling motion—but when they touched the ground, they bloomed into tiny wildflowers. Purple, blue, white.

She stepped forward.

The air felt dense. Not heavy—meaningful. Like walking into a room where someone had just whispered a secret.

And there, by the roots of a massive sycamore tree, sat a woman.

Older than Rio, with streaks of gray in her dark hair and a softness around her eyes that felt achingly familiar. She wore a coat the color of dusted pine and boots caked in mud. Her hands were rough and ink-stained.

“Rio,” the woman said, and just like that—Rio knew her.

“Eliza?”

Eliza nodded. “It’s good to see you again, my darling child, even if you don’t remember.”

Rio’s mouth felt dry. “I—I don’t understand. Am I dreaming?”

“Yes. But that doesn’t mean it’s not real.”

A breeze moved between them, ruffling the leaves, carrying the scent of lavender and smoke. Somewhere, distant and soft, came a familiar sound: Salem’s purr.

“I’ve been waiting for you to find the book,” Eliza said, voice quiet. “It knew when the time was right.”

Rio hesitated, her fingers curling into her palms. “You’re my grandmother.”

Eliza smiled, warm but sad. “And your mother was Daisy. Wild-hearted, stubborn, too smart for her own good.”

Rio’s chest ached at the name. “What happened to her?”

Eliza looked away for a long beat. “She tried to fight what she was. She thought she could outrun it. The Wild Thread doesn’t let go, Rio. It winds itself around your bones. If you don’t walk with it, it pulls you under. And if you can’t control it, others might try to take it from you.”

“I don’t know what I’m doing,” Rio whispered. “I didn’t even believe in magic until a couple of days ago.”

Eliza looked back at her, eyes sharp now—not unkind, but piercing. “You don’t have to know. You just have to listen. To the land. To yourself. To the places where your art becomes more than lines and color. That’s the Thread speaking.”

Rio’s heart thudded in her chest.

“There are others,” Eliza said. “Two more. The Flame and the Body. You’ll know them when the time comes. One’s already close.”

Agatha.

“Eliza… why now?”

The older woman stepped closer, lifting a hand to brush gently over Rio’s cheek. Her touch was warm. Steady.

“Because you were ready. Because the sisters are stirring again. And because you have the chance to do what Daisy couldn’t—stay. Fight.”

Rio blinked, and Eliza was fading. Her figure growing lighter, like the last burn of a match.

“Eliza, wait—”

“Don’t be afraid to feel everything,” came the reply, soft and echoing. “It’s how the Wild survives.”

Then the forest fell away, and Rio woke up with a start.

The fire was still crackling low in the hearth. Salem was curled on the couch beside her. The grimoire lay on the table—closed, but not quiet.

Rio reached for it with trembling fingers.

She needed answers.

And she knew exactly who might be able to give them.

~*~

The snow had stopped sometime before dawn, leaving the town brushed in soft white, like the dusting of powdered sugar on a cake. The sky was a clear, pale blue—the kind that made the cold air feel clean rather than biting.

Rio tucked her scarf tighter around her neck and stepped into The Hollow.

The bell above the door jingled, and the warmth and scent hit her all at once—freshly brewed coffee, warm sugar, cardamom, and something herbal she couldn’t quite place. Lilia Calderu was behind the counter, wearing a sweater the color of plum jam and a smirk that said she knew far too much.

“Well, well,” Lilia said, tapping the espresso machine with one long, ring-heavy finger. “You’re out awfully early for someone who usually only comes for lunch. Did the world end, or are you finally acclimating?”

Rio smiled, trying to play it cool. “Just grabbing coffee.”

“And pastries, huh?” Lilia said, already packing a box. “You’ve got that ‘bringing-offerings-to-a-witch’ look about you.”

Rio flushed. “She’s not a—okay. Yeah.”

“Of course she’s not,” Lilia said, and winked. “Tell Agatha I said she owes me a tea blend. And no more pretending she’s too busy.”

Rio paid and left with two coffees—one black, one with a dash of oat milk—and a box of pastries that smelled like cinnamon. Her boots crunched softly against the snow as she made her way to the apothecary.

The sign above the door still creaked when the wind touched it. Inside, it was quiet, warm, and filled with the scent of dried lavender and lemon balm. Sunlight streamed through the front windows, catching in glass jars and bunches of dried herbs strung from the ceiling.

Agatha was behind the counter, scribbling in a thick ledger, her reading glasses low on her nose and her dark hair pinned back in a messy twist. She looked up—and smiled, slow and crooked.

“Good morning,” she said. “You’re up early. Did something explode?”

Rio held up the coffees. “Peace offering. Or bribe. Depends on how you look at it.”

Agatha came around the counter, peeling off her glasses. “Is that cinnamon I smell?”

“I figured if I was about to grill you with magical questions, I should bring reinforcements.”

Agatha took the coffee, fingers brushing against Rio’s, warm and easy. “You learn fast.”

Rio followed her to the back, where the little sitting area was half-shadowed and sunlit at once, books stacked on every surface, and a long window seat that begged to be claimed. They settled in, coffees steaming between them.

For a moment, Rio just watched her.

The way Agatha moved, always precise, always thoughtful. The way her smile tilted before it bloomed. She looked more relaxed than Rio had ever seen her—still sharp, still poised, but less like she was bracing for something.

“Okay,” Rio said, tearing into a flaky pastry. “First things first. It’s completely ridiculous that we still don’t have each other’s numbers.”

Agatha blinked at her. “We don’t?”

“No. We’ve been doing this whole low-key friendship thing, and we’re still operating on walk-ins and psychic timing.”

Agatha snorted. “I hate that you described it so accurately.”

Rio handed over her phone. “Here. Fix it.”

Their fingers brushed again as Agatha took the phone, and something warm flickered in Rio’s chest. Not magic. Or maybe just a different kind of it.

“Wild Thread,” she thought, and her gaze softened.

“Okay,” Agatha said after typing, handing it back. “Now you can text me passive-aggressive thoughts about the weather.”

“Or memes.”

“You do seem like a meme sender.”

Rio grinned, but then sobered slightly. “I read more of the grimoire yesterday.”

Agatha’s brow arched. “And?”

“There’s a lot I don’t understand,” Rio said. “But there was something about the Threads. Three of them. Sisters. It felt like more than just a story.”

Agatha leaned back, cradling her coffee. “It is more than a story.”

Rio looked at her, steady. “So tell me.”

Agatha met her gaze—and the air between them felt charged. Not the kind of static that came before a storm. Something older. Quieter.

Like the forest was holding its breath.

Agatha opened her mouth—probably about to say something devastatingly elegant and enlightening about the Threads—when the front bell jingled, sharp and insistent.

She blinked once, then sighed, setting her coffee down. “Saved by the bell.”

Rio chuckled softly as Agatha stood and padded into the front of the shop. Curious, Rio followed a step behind, sipping from her cup just as a burst of cold air swept in along with a familiar voice.

“Well, look at this! The apothecary is actually open at 9am.”

Mayor Sharon Davis swept into the shop like a cold wind disguised in a burgundy wool coat and matching gloves. Her hair was set in a perfect bob, not a strand out of place, and her lipstick was a shade that said I run things.

“Mayor Davis,” Agatha greeted smoothly, her voice cool but polite. “What a surprise.”

“Agatha,” Sharon said, flashing a smile that didn’t quite reach her eyes. Then she turned to Rio, her expression warming a touch. “Ms. Vidal. You’re still here, I see. Enjoying your little… sabbatical?”

Rio cleared her throat. “Something like that.”

“Hm.” Sharon’s gaze flicked between them. “Well, you picked a fine place to spend it. Can’t beat our small-town charm—or our small-town gossip.”

Agatha didn’t flinch, but Rio could feel the tension settle lightly in the air.

“I just came in for some of that headache tincture you made me last fall,” Sharon went on, unbothered. “Worked better than the garbage Dr. Leland prescribed. Not that I’m telling people you’re more effective than our local clinic, of course.”

“Of course not,” Agatha said smoothly, already turning to the shelves.

Rio lingered behind the counter, eyes dancing with amusement as Sharon glanced around the shop like it might be harboring scandal in its shelves.

“This place smells better than I remember,” the mayor mused. “Did you change the formula?”

“No,” Agatha said, handing over a small bottle. “But I did light a new candle.”

Sharon squinted at it, sniffed. “Smells like pine and something… old. Like church wood.”

“It’s sage and cedar.”

“Well, it suits you.”

Sharon paid in crisp bills, then turned to Rio again, her tone turning faux-casual. “We’re doing a little winter fair in the town square next weekend. Art booths, music, cocoa—all that. You should come. Might do you some good to meet more people.”

“I’ll think about it,” Rio said, smiling tightly.

“Good.” She gave them both one last look, then sailed out the door, the bell jingling brightly behind her.

A pause.

Rio turned to Agatha, wide-eyed. “That woman is terrifying.”

Agatha laughed under her breath as she slipped behind the counter again. “She always knows more than she lets on. It’s her one true gift.”

“She was definitely sniffing for drama.”

“She always is.”

Rio leaned on the counter. “I don’t suppose that headache tincture also works on people who talk too much and smell like city council?”

Agatha smirked. “Only if you drink the whole bottle.”

They laughed—light, easy. And when the silence returned, it wasn’t awkward.

Just expectant.

“I should probably ask you those questions now,” Rio said quietly, her hand curling again around her cup. “About the Threads. About everything. Before anyone else comes in.”

Agatha looked at her, slower this time. Steadier.

“I was hoping you would.”

Agatha turned toward the back, nodding her head for Rio to follow. “Come on. It’s quieter back here. And I doubt Sharon will be the last interruption.”

The back room of the apothecary was smaller than Rio expected—less “secret potion lab” and more “organized chaos.” Dried herbs hung in bunches from the rafters, their scents earthy and sweet, and a large wooden table took up most of the space, cluttered with vials, books, and mortar bowls. The fire crackled softly in the tiny stove set into the wall, lending the room a cozy glow.

Rio perched on a stool, coffee cup still in hand, her grimoire tucked beneath her arm. She placed it gently on the table, fingers grazing the worn cover like it might vanish again if she let go.

“So,” she said, voice softer now. “The Threads. They’re real.”

Agatha sat across from her, folding her hands together. “They are.”

Rio looked down at the book, then back at her. “The Wild Thread… that’s me?”

Agatha hesitated, then nodded. “Yes. It’s… not something you choose, exactly. It’s more like it chooses you. Or wakes up in you.”

Rio swallowed. “It said something about love. And grief. That it usually stirs through those things.”

A slow breath from Agatha. “That’s true. The Threads respond to big things. Not just emotions—but shifts. Change. Loss. They run deep in the bloodline, but sometimes they lie dormant for generations.”

“And they’re not just magic?”

“No,” Agatha said, her voice steady. “They’re something older. Wilder. Magic is just… what people call it when they don’t understand.”

Rio blinked, heart picking up. “So if I am one, and you are too…”

Agatha didn’t look away. “Then we’re tied to something that runs deeper than either of us. And stronger, if we let it.”

Rio glanced down again, the weight of the grimoire grounding her. “My grandmother. I think she might’ve been one, too.”

“Probably,” Agatha said softly. “Most people don’t know what they are until it’s too late. Or until it starts to burn. And even so, it’s so incredibly rare that all three threads manifest in the same generation. That hasn’t happened in over a hundred years as far as we know.”

Rio leaned back slightly, overwhelmed but holding her own. “You’re the Body Thread, right?”

Agatha’s gaze sharpened just slightly. “Yes.”

“And… Wanda’s the Flame?”

“Yes.”

Rio nodded slowly. “So what happens when all three of us are here?”

Before Agatha could answer, the front bell jingled again.

Agatha stood with a grimace that was almost fond. “Hold that thought.”

Rio offered a lopsided smile as Agatha disappeared into the front again. She could hear the low murmur of greetings, some talk about a seasonal tonic, and the unmistakable sound of someone knocking over a display stand.

When Agatha returned a minute later, slightly harried, she paused in the doorway and gave Rio a rueful look.

“I should’ve known today would be busier than usual.”

Rio raised an eyebrow. “It’s the Sharon Davis effect.”

Agatha grinned. “Clearly.”

She stepped back into the room, brushing invisible dust off her sweater. “I’ll probably be stuck up here the rest of the morning… but do you want to meet for lunch? We can finish this conversation properly.”

Rio blinked, a smile blooming despite the whirlwind of thoughts still stirring in her chest. “Yeah. I’d like that.”

“Good.” Agatha gave a small, private smile, the kind Rio was beginning to recognize as rare. “There’s a place just off Main Street with good soup and terrible decor. I’ll text you the address,” she paused, then lifted an eyebrow. “Now that we don’t live in the dark ages anymore and actually have each other’s numbers.”

Rio chuckled, putting the grimoire back in her bag. When she left the apothecary a few minutes later, she felt something twist sweetly in her chest.

The air was cold, but her hands were warm.

And Agatha Harkness had just asked her to lunch.

~*~

Rio was just finishing her second coffee of the day and trying not to read the same paragraph in Eliza’s grimoire for the fifth time when her phone buzzed.

Agatha Harkness

Change of plans. I’m picking you up. There’s a place one town over. Quiet, decent food, and hopefully zero small town eavesdroppers.

Rio

Bossy .

Agatha Harkness

Efficient .

Rio

I’ll be ready in ten.

Fifteen minutes later, Agatha was parked outside the cottage in a sleek, dusty black car that looked like it had once been polished to perfection and then absolutely given up halfway through the last decade. Rio stepped outside, buttoning up her coat, and then slid into the passenger seat.

“You don’t strike me as someone who likes driving out of town,” she said, glancing over at her.

Agatha put the car in drive with a wry smile. “I don’t. But I like interruptions even less.”

Rio laughed softly, tucking her hands into her lap. “Fair.”

The drive took just under twenty minutes, winding through a stretch of pine and fog, the kind of road Rio hadn’t realized she missed until she saw it again. Quiet, muted, like the trees themselves were listening.

Agatha didn’t turn on the radio. She just drove in comfortable silence, the occasional glance out the corner of her eye when Rio shifted or muttered something under her breath while scrolling through her notes app.
