Eventually, they pulled into the gravel lot of a small, ivy-covered inn that looked like it had once been a house—and probably still was, somewhere behind the dining room.

Rio raised a brow as they stepped out into the cold. “This doesn’t look like a soup-and-sandwich type of place.”

Agatha gave her a look over the top of her sunglasses. “They make fresh bread every morning and have a wine list that rivals most places in the city.”

Rio made a low, impressed sound. “You spoil all your friends like this?”

Agatha turned toward her with a lazy smirk. “Only the ones who ask magical questions.”

The inn-turned-restaurant was quiet enough that Rio could hear the soft crackle of the fire and the gentle clink of silverware from a couple at the far end. It smelled like rosemary and melted butter, and the heat from the hearth had finally started to loosen the tension in her shoulders.

She hadn’t realized how tightly she’d been wound until she sat down.

Agatha watched her for a moment, then set her spoon down and leaned in, elbows resting casually on the tablecloth.

“So… we’ve already established that the Thread magic usually stirs when we go through big life changes or emotions. You said you never even heard of this place or Eliza until after the breakup?”

Rio’s throat tightened. “Yeah.”

“You don’t have to talk about it if you don’t want to,” Agatha offered, voice even, unreadable.

Rio stared down at the dark red wine in her glass, the way it caught the low light like ink. She didn’t have to. But somehow, here, with Agatha watching her—not judging, not pushing, just there—it felt like maybe she could.

“I was with her for four years,” she said quietly. “Carol. We met at a concert. She was funny and loud and fearless and—” She exhaled. “I was completely in love with her.”

Agatha didn’t interrupt. She didn’t flinch at the name or the confession.

“And then I found out she’d been cheating on me for months.” Rio’s mouth twisted into something that wasn’t quite a smile. “With some bartender nearly half her age, barely old enough to be in college.”

“I’m sorry,” Agatha said.

Rio shrugged. “It wasn’t just the betrayal. I think part of me had already been unraveling before it happened. I hadn’t drawn anything in months. My publisher was breathing down my neck, and I kept staring at blank pages like they were mocking me.”

Agatha’s brow furrowed, her gaze narrowing just slightly.

“I used to feel like I had this endless well of stories, you know? Like the characters lived in me. But after everything with Carol…” Rio hesitated, fingers tightening slightly around the stem of her glass. “It was like someone turned the faucet off. I had nothing left.”

“And that’s when things started to shift?” Agatha asked gently.

“Yeah.” Rio nodded slowly. “But the first big thing was getting that letter. And I had this feeling deep inside that I should come here, however bizarre it was to just travel across the country to check out some inheritance from someone I’d never even heard of.”

She looked up, meeting Agatha’s eyes.

“You think it was grief?” Rio asked. “Or heartbreak?”

Agatha studied Rio, her expression unreadable for a beat. Then, softly: “I think it was change. You were cracked open by more than just grief or heartbreak. That was just the final straw. And whatever was buried underneath… finally had room to rise.”

Rio went quiet at that, swallowing past the tightness in her throat. The fire hissed behind her, and outside, the sky was a soft, pale gray, hinting at snow again.

Agatha reached out, slow and deliberate, and lightly tapped the side of Rio’s glass with her own. “To cracking open.”

Rio snorted, then smiled. “That’s the most poetic toast I’ve ever gotten from someone who tried to walk out of my cottage instead of explaining how the hell a whole bunch of flowers had just bloomed in a frozen patch of earth.”

Agatha smirked. “Growth.”

~*~

They were halfway through lunch, the kind of meal that stretched without hurry. A breeze stirred through the patio of the little restaurant and the late sunlight hit the edges of Agatha’s dark hair like ink soaked in gold.

She hadn’t said much in the last few minutes. Just listened, eyes fixed on Rio in that way she did—like she was both wary and utterly absorbed.

Rio lowered her wine glass and tilted her head slightly. “What about you?” she asked gently. “What brought you back to Thistle Grove?”

Agatha didn’t answer right away. She looked past Rio, beyond the flower boxes blooming along the railing, out toward the hills. And then she said, quiet and simple, “My mother disappeared when I was twenty-one.”

Rio blinked, surprised. “Disappeared?”

“Vanished,” Agatha said. “No note. No spell. No body. One day she was there, and the next, she wasn’t. I searched everywhere—mundane and magical. Nothing. Just… gone.”

She picked at the edge of her napkin. “She was powerful. One of the last women in town who really understood what the Threads were. And I think it scared people. I think it scared her, too.”

Rio’s voice was soft. “Is that when you gave it up?”

“No.” Agatha’s laugh was dry. “I still thought I could do something good with it back then. I went to med school in New York. Thought, if I could heal with magic, maybe I could do it the regular way too. But it didn’t work. I couldn’t turn it off. Every patient I touched—I could feel it. Whether they’d make it. Whether they wouldn’t.”

She shook her head slowly. “It hollowed me out.”

Rio didn’t interrupt. Just waited, letting the silence unfold.

“I came home,” Agatha said. “Met someone—briefly. Got pregnant. He was long gone before I even showed. And then there was Nicky.”

She smiled then, the kind of smile that cracked her open, beautiful and broken all at once. “He was everything. Smart. Funny. Loud as hell. I thought, this is it. This is what I was meant to protect.”

Rio’s fingers tensed slightly around her glass.

“He got sick when he was five,” Agatha said. “Leukemia. At first I thought—this is the reason I have these powers. This is why I inherited all of it. So I could save him.”

The light shifted, shadows threading over her hands.

“I tried everything,” she whispered. “Spells. Blood rites. Desperate, awful things. But nothing worked. My powers didn’t work on my own flesh and blood and I never understood why. The magic didn’t even flicker. It was like it knew. Like it refused.”

Rio’s eyes shimmered, but she didn’t look away. She knew what was coming.

“Nicky died two weeks before his seventh birthday.” Agatha looked down at the table, her voice hoarse. “And that was it. That was the moment everything broke. I stopped calling myself a witch. I buried my grimoire. I didn’t want anything to do with the Threads or the town or the old myths. None of it saved him. None of it meant anything.”

There was a long silence.

Rio reached out slowly, laying her hand over Agatha’s. Her touch was warm, sure, steady. No pressure. Just presence.

“I’m so sorry,” Rio said.

Agatha blinked fast and looked away. “I don’t know why I’m telling you this.”

“Because I asked,” Rio said gently. “And because you needed to say it.”

Agatha’s mouth curved faintly. “You’re annoyingly good at this listening thing.”

“You’ve met my best friend. I get a lot of practice.”

That pulled a small laugh out of Agatha—tired, but real. “He would’ve liked you,” she said softly. “He would’ve asked a thousand questions about your tattoos.”

Rio gave a sad, crooked smile. “I would’ve answered every single one.”

For a while, they just sat there, their hands resting between them like a bridge, thumbs caressing gently over skin. Not lovers. Not yet. But something unmistakably more than friends.

And when Agatha finally looked up again, her gaze was steadier, softer. “So. You and me. We’ve both got ghosts.”

Rio nodded. “Maybe they brought us here.”

~*~

Agatha hadn’t let go of Rio’s hand, not really. Their fingers had drifted slightly apart, sure, but they still lingered in the space between them, as if even the silence was charged with something waiting to spark.

Rio was the first to speak again, her voice quieter now, a little hesitant. “Can I ask you something?”

Agatha gave her a sidelong glance. “You already did. A lot of somethings.”

Rio smiled faintly. “This one’s different.” She paused. “What does it actually mean to be a Thread?”

Agatha leaned back slightly in her chair. The breeze had picked up again, rustling the wildflowers planted along the railing, stirring the edges of the story like it was listening.

She didn’t answer right away.

“I read some of Eliza’s Grimoire,” Rio said, before the silence could stretch too long. “She wrote about the Three Sisters. How they weren’t born like us. How they came before the land.”

Agatha’s gaze sharpened, a flicker of something behind her eyes—recognition, maybe. Or wariness.

“And that when the world broke them apart,” Rio went on softly, “the last one left behind the Threads. So the ones who came after could find each other again.”

Agatha looked away, out toward the hills. “That story’s older than anyone in town. No one writes it down, not really. It’s just… passed along. Half-whispered.”

“But it’s true,” Rio said.

Agatha’s mouth twitched. “Most dangerous things are.”

Rio sat forward, arms resting on the table now. “I think I’m the Wild Thread. That’s what Eliza wrote about—the one who sings to trees. Makes things grow. Creates.”

Agatha gave her a slow nod. “You are.”

Rio blinked. “You’re not surprised.”

“I’ve felt it since the moment we met,” Agatha said. “When you touched me, it was like the world moved. Flowers blooming out of season. Magic humming like it remembered you.”

Rio exhaled, half in awe. “So… what are you?”

Agatha met her gaze then, steady and solemn. “The Body Thread. The one who feels what others feel. Who heals. Or used to.”

“And Wanda?” Rio asked.

“The Flame,” Agatha said. “She’s always been fire and rage and truth. It burns in her bones. The protector.”

Rio shook her head slowly. “So what happens when all three come together again?”

Agatha hesitated.

“Eliza’s book mentioned something,” Rio said, lowering her voice, “about the land waking. The sisters returning. And the town changing.”

Agatha’s jaw tightened. “That part of the story… it’s more like a warning.”

She paused. “The last time the Threads stirred like this, things broke. The town turned on itself. My mother said the land remembers, and it can wake things that are meant to be dormant. Good and bad.”

Rio’s throat felt tight. “And you think it’s happening now?”

Agatha didn’t answer right away. Then, quietly, “I think it’s trying to. And I think we’re the thread being pulled first.”

Rio looked down at her hands, palms slightly stained with graphite from her sketches earlier that morning. “It doesn’t feel like power. It feels like… weight.”

Agatha’s voice was gentler now. “Because it is. It’s not just magic—it’s memory. The kind that lives in blood. In love. In loss. The kind that demands something back.”

Rio looked up, eyes meeting hers again. “So what do we do with it?”

Agatha stared at her for a long moment. Then she leaned forward, resting her forearms on the table, her face inches from Rio’s.

“We don’t run from it,” she said. “We learn it. We hold it. We decide what it means for us.”

Rio didn’t move.

“I’m not asking you to be anything you’re not,” Agatha added. “But if this power is waking up in you… then I’ll be here. To help. If you want me.”

Rio’s breath caught. “I do.”

It wasn’t romantic. Not quite. But it was more than friendship. A choice, offered and accepted. A step closer.

And maybe that was the real power in the Threads—how they pulled you toward the people who saw you, even when you weren’t ready to be seen.

~*~

They didn’t say much as they left the restaurant.

The check had been paid. The last crumbs of lemon tart were forgotten. And the wind had picked up just enough to make Agatha shrug her coat on tighter as they stepped outside. The air smelled faintly of salt and damp earth, the kind of post-rain crispness that made everything feel a little more real.

Rio slid her hands into the pockets of her jacket. “You want to walk for a bit? I could use some coffee before I turn into the world’s sleepiest witch.”

Agatha smirked. “You had one glass of wine.”

“And I’m a lightweight. Don’t judge me.”

“I would never,” Agatha said, in a tone that implied she always would.

They walked in companionable silence down the main street, which looked like something out of a postcard—early spring bulbs in planter boxes, string lights still up from the winter festival, the faint sound of a guitar somewhere in the distance. This town was smaller than Thistle Grove, but not entirely unlike it. A place with roots. Secrets. Ghosts that probably wandered around unnoticed.

Agatha glanced sideways. “You good?”

Rio gave a little nod, lips pursed. “Just thinking.”

“Dangerous habit.”

“You would know,” Rio shot back with a dry smile.

They ducked into a narrow little coffee shop near the end of the block—bare brick walls, shelves full of mismatched mugs, the smell of fresh espresso curling in the air. Agatha watched Rio order a lavender honey latte and found herself quietly charmed. It felt oddly… tender, this whole day. Even with all the old wounds they’d opened.

When they stepped back outside, drinks in hand, Rio finally said, “So you’re really not afraid of any of this?”

Agatha raised a brow. “You mean the magic? Or the part where the land might rise up and rewrite everything we know?”

Rio exhaled a laugh into her cup. “Both.”

Agatha sipped her black coffee. “I’m not afraid of the Threads, if that’s what you’re asking. I’m afraid of what people do when they realize they can’t control them.”

Rio was quiet for a moment. Then: “I think I’m more afraid of what it’ll ask of me.”

Agatha slowed her pace just slightly. “It might ask for everything,” she said softly. “But it won’t take it unless you offer it. That’s the difference between real power and corruption.”

Rio looked at her, really looked. “How do you know that?”

“Because I’ve lived both.”

There was no drama in the way she said it. No performance. Just truth, plain and heavy and lived-in.

They reached the edge of a small overlook, where the road dropped into a view of rolling hills and the distant gleam of water. Agatha stopped, letting her gaze sweep over the quiet horizon.

“You’re not the only one who was born into this without asking,” she said after a moment. “But maybe we were meant to be asked.”

Rio stood beside her, shoulder brushing hers. “What if I say yes and I don’t know what that means yet?”

Agatha turned toward her, expression unreadable. “Then I’ll stay until you do. And afterwards if you want me to.”

The wind kicked up a little. Rio’s hair caught in it, wild and dark and curling at the edges. Agatha reached up, almost without thinking, and tucked a strand behind her ear.

It wasn’t a kiss. It wasn’t even flirtation. It was something softer. A promise, maybe. Or the beginning of one.

Rio didn’t pull away from Agatha’s hand. If anything, she leaned into the touch, her eyes flicking toward Agatha’s face like she was trying to memorize it under this soft, silvery light. There was something different in her expression now—unguarded, maybe, or just tired of holding everything so close to her chest.

“I don’t really do this,” she murmured.

“Walk?” Agatha asked, a faint smile tugging at the corner of her mouth.

“Let people in.”

“Mm. And yet here I am.”

“Yeah,” Rio said, her voice quieter than before. “Here you are.”

The wind tugged at the hem of her coat, and Agatha took that as a sign—gently hooking her arm through Rio’s as they turned away from the overlook.

They didn’t speak for a few blocks. Just the rhythm of their steps, the occasional clink of to-go cups, the soft hum of early evening settling over everything. The street lights blinked on one by one as they walked, and the world felt far away for a little while—just two women walking slowly through a quiet town, caught in the gravity of something neither one of them fully understood yet.

When they reached the car, Rio paused with her hand on the passenger door.

“Thanks for today,” she said. “Not just lunch. Everything else too.”

Agatha tilted her head. “You mean the devastating emotional unraveling or the moment where you almost believed in fate?”

Rio laughed, and it came out a little hoarse. “All of it.”

Agatha watched her for a beat. “You’re not alone in this, you know.”

“I keep forgetting that,” Rio said, her smile half-apology, half-habit.

Agatha opened the door for her with a slight flourish. “Then I’ll keep reminding you.”

As Rio climbed in, Agatha rounded to the driver’s side. And maybe it was nothing—just the familiar weight of the day, the comfort of someone finally seeing her. But when Agatha sat behind the wheel, Rio reached out without thinking and touched her hand.

Not a grasp. Not a clutch.

Just fingers brushing fingers.

It was fleeting.

But it meant something.

And Agatha didn’t say a word as she drove them home.

 

Chapter 10: Ten

Chapter Notes

Thank you so so so much for your incredible, thoughtful comments! I'm so happy you guys are enjoying this.

By the time they pulled into town, the sky had turned the color of ink-stained parchment. The roads were quiet, the kind of quiet Thistle Grove only ever really achieved in the late afternoon—just before the lights in the shops flickered on, just before the real cold set in.

Agatha slowed the car near Main Street, letting the hum of the engine settle to a soft purr. She glanced at Rio out of the corner of her eye. Her shoulders were more relaxed now, her expression still turned inward, like she was trying to fit new truths into old scars.

“I can drop you at the cottage,” Agatha offered gently, “unless there’s something else you need in town?”

Rio shook her head, turning toward her with a half-smile. “Nah. I think I’ve done enough unraveling for one day.”

Agatha smiled too, but didn’t answer. She just did a U turn and started toward Rio’s cottage.

And then something happened.

Something subtle.

A hum in the air, like the breath before a spell. Agatha didn’t move, didn’t speak. She only watched, breath caught, as Rio blinked down at her lap—where, nestled between her fingers, a flower had appeared.

A single marigold, golden and warm, as if it had been coaxed from the sun itself.

Rio stared at it. “Okay,” she said slowly. “Definitely didn’t mean to do that.”

Agatha arched a brow, amused and fascinated. “Not exactly subtle.”

Rio held it up by the stem, examining it like it might wilt if she breathed too hard. Then, with a little shake of her head and the beginnings of a grin tugging at her lips, she turned to Agatha and offered it to her.

“For you, my lady,” she said with mock-formality, but her voice was soft. Earnest.

Agatha didn’t take the flower right away. She just looked at her—really looked at her. At the flushed curve of her cheek, at the sparks of magic Rio was still trying to pretend weren’t there.

Then, delicately, she took the flower from her fingers.

“And here I thought you didn’t flirt,” Agatha murmured.

“I don’t,” Rio said, but she didn’t sound very convinced.

Agatha tucked the marigold into the pocket of her coat, close to her heart. “Could’ve fooled me.”

The quiet between them held for a beat longer. Long enough to feel like a choice neither of them was quite ready to make.

Then Rio reached for the door handle, cheeks still pink. “Thanks for the ride.”

“Anytime.”

She stepped out, boots crunching against gravel, and Agatha watched her go. Watched the sway of her coat, the line of her shoulders, the way she glanced back once—just once—before heading toward her cottage, the glow of the porch light waiting for her like a promise.

Agatha touched the flower again.

She couldn’t help but smile.

~*~

Agatha didn’t go home.

She turned the car toward the north end of town, tires crunching over the thin layer of frost that had settled while they were away. Her hands were tight on the steering wheel, knuckles white, like she could wring the confusion out of her bones if she just gripped hard enough.

It wasn’t like her to feel unmoored. But ever since Rio Vidal had walked into her life with that unintentional wildness and those haunted, steady eyes, Agatha had been walking around with the unmistakable sense that something had clicked into place inside her—something ancient and bright and deeply inconvenient.

The lights were already on at Wanda’s place.

She pulled into the driveway, killed the engine, and sat in the dark car for a full minute, staring down at the marigold still tucked into her coat pocket. She didn’t even have to touch it. She could feel it. Like it knew something she didn’t.

The door opened before she could knock.

“You look like hell,” Wanda said, stepping aside to let her in.

Agatha breezed past her. “Good to see you too.”

Wanda kicked the door shut, trailing her into the cluttered warmth of her living room. Crystals littered every surface. Candles burned low and steady. The house smelled like rosemary and black tea.

“You’ve got that look,” Wanda said, crossing her arms. “The one that usually means you’re either about to cast something dangerous or run away for six months without telling anyone.”

Agatha didn’t sit. She paced. She ran a hand through her hair. “I don’t know what I’m doing.”

“About Rio?”

Agatha’s head snapped up. “Did I say anything about Rio?”

Wanda smirked. “You didn’t have to.”

She made her way to the sideboard, poured two glasses of something that looked suspiciously like whiskey, and handed one to Agatha. “Drink. Then talk.”

Agatha took a long sip, let the burn chase away the edges of her restlessness. “Something’s happening.”

Wanda arched a brow, silent.

“It’s like—” Agatha hesitated, the words crawling out of her. “When I’m near her, it’s like my power wakes up. Not just wakes up—it stretches. Purrs. Like it’s been waiting. Like it recognizes her.”

Wanda didn’t speak, letting her spiral it out.

“She touched my hand once and a whole garden popped up in the frozen soil under our feet.  And today—today she made a marigold appear out of nowhere. She didn’t even try. It just… happened.”

Agatha pulled the flower from her pocket, held it out. It was still warm. Still alive.

Agatha stared at Wanda, the silence stretching between them. The weight of what she hadn’t said yet pressed down on her chest like a stone. She wanted to pretend she hadn’t just dropped a bombshell in the form of a glowing flower, but it was impossible. There was too much on the line now.

Wanda was still holding the marigold, rolling it between her fingers like she was trying to find the hidden truth within its petals.

“Don’t just stare at it, Wanda. I’m trying to tell you something,” Agatha said, her voice sharp.

Wanda looked up, her expression unreadable. “And I’m listening.”

“It’s… it’s not just about her being a Thread,” Agatha said, her heart beating a little faster. “Now she knows she’s one, that this immense power is just simmering inside her.” She paused, sucking in a breath, the words slipping out before she could stop them. “Now that she knows, things are different. She’s going to want to understand it. She’s going to want to explore it. She might not even understand the danger of it yet.”

Wanda sat back, crossing her arms, her gaze steady. “You’re worried about her?”

Agatha’s jaw clenched. “I’m worried about us. About what happens when two Threads come together—especially two that are already… this close.”

“I thought you said this wasn’t about attraction,” Wanda teased, a knowing glint in her eye.

Agatha shot her a look, half defensive and half pleading. “It’s not just that. But damn it, Wanda, if she’s a Thread, if she’s connected to the land the way I am… there’s no telling what it could do. Or how it could affect her. Us. She might not even realize how dangerous she could be. How dangerous we could be together.”

Wanda tilted her head. “And you’re concerned about her safety, or yours?”

Agatha grimaced. “I don’t know anymore.”

Wanda set the flower on the counter, then turned to face Agatha more fully, her expression no longer playful. “You’re more afraid of what happens when all three Threads converge, aren’t you?”

Agatha didn’t need to answer. Wanda had always been able to read her like a book. The fear that the myth would be true—that the Three Threads would come together in blood, in love, and in magic—and it would wake something they couldn’t control. She didn’t want to admit it, but it was there. A deep, gnawing anxiety that every time she touched Rio, every time they stood close, her magic seemed to hum louder, feel stronger. Rio’s magic, too, was reacting—something primal, alive.

“I don’t know what to do with this,” Agatha admitted, her voice tight. “I don’t know what it means for either of us. If she accepts what she is—if she’s fully awakened as a Thread—then we’re both risking something we might not be able to undo.”

Wanda’s eyes softened, but there was no judgment there. Only understanding. “You’re afraid, Agatha. And you’re not the only one who’s been carrying that burden. We all know how it works—the Threads call to each other. They always have. It’s why the Three Sisters were hunted. The land knows, too.”

Agatha turned away, looking out the window, but the dark trees and snow-dusted streets of Thistle Grove did nothing to calm the turmoil inside her. “It’s not just that. It’s her. Rio. She’s not just a Thread to me. She’s—”

“Someone you care about?” Wanda interrupted, her voice gentle but firm.

Agatha blinked, the words getting caught in her throat. “I didn’t think I could care about anyone like this. Not after Nicky. Not after… all of it. But now? Every time I’m near her, it’s like I’m being pulled toward something I can’t see. And every time I touch her… it feels like everything else fades away.”

There was a long pause. Agatha waited for the judgment, for the teasing, but Wanda was quiet, listening. She knew the weight of what Agatha was carrying.

When Wanda finally spoke, her voice was soft, but heavy with truth. “What are you really afraid of, honey? Of how powerful you can be together or that you might be able to love again?”

Agatha’s heart tightened. It wasn’t a thought she’d let herself fully acknowledge until now. “I’m afraid of losing control. Of dragging her into something she doesn’t even know about, something she won’t be able to outrun once we’re both in it. And I’m scared, Wanda… I’m scared she’s going to want something that could break both of us.”

Wanda stepped closer, putting a hand on Agatha’s shoulder. Her voice was calm, the way it always was when she wanted Agatha to face the truth. “It’s not always about control, Agatha. Sometimes you have to trust the magic. Trust the threads. Let it pull you where it’s meant to.”

Agatha swallowed. “Then what?”

Wanda met her gaze, a faint smile curving her lips. “Then it’s something you’re both going to have to figure out together.”

Wanda was just about to say something else—probably something Agatha wouldn’t want to hear—when the front door opened with a soft creak. A gust of late afternoon wind followed, carrying the scent of pine and the faintest trace of woodsmoke.

“Incoming,” Wanda said with a smile, already turning toward the sound of familiar footsteps.

Viz stepped inside, tall and unassuming as always, his shoulders dusted with snow. He set down his bag near the door and shrugged off his coat. “There’s talk of another cold front coming in,” he said, crossing the room. “You should both be bundled up in wool and staying out of trouble.”

Wanda rose from her perch on the counter to greet him with a kiss, her fingers brushing his cheek fondly. “You say that like trouble isn’t our love language.”

Viz chuckled, then turned to Agatha. He gave her a warm, grounding squeeze to the shoulder—brief but grounding, familiar. “Evening, Aggie. Didn’t mean to interrupt the magic shop talk.”

“You’re not interrupting anything,” Agatha said, voice a little rougher than usual. She was still too in her head, still spinning from the conversation. Her thumb pressed to the smooth edge of the table as if anchoring herself.

“Mm,” Viz murmured, already heading toward the kitchen. “If you say so.”

Agatha’s phone buzzed in her coat pocket. She fished it out almost absently, but the second she saw the name on the screen, something in her chest pulled taut.

Rio Vidal

Thank you for lunch today. And everything else. Really.

There was a second message, sent just a minute later.

Also—look who decided to pose dramatically just for you.

Attached was a photo: Salem sprawled out on what looked like Rio’s sketchbook, front paws tucked under his chin like a feline king, eyes half-lidded in judgment. And nestled right beside him, unmistakably vivid even through the lens, was a flower. A daisy. Bright and impossible, growing from the blank page.

Agatha’s breath caught. The image glowed against her skin.

Another buzz.

I didn’t even mean to again. So… that’s not terrifying at all. But also kind of beautiful? I like this magic thing.

Agatha stared at the photo, then at the messages, her lips twitching into something small and startled and warm. She didn’t even realize Wanda had moved closer until her friend was peeking over her shoulder.

“Ohhh,” Wanda breathed. “That’s adorable. You’re so screwed, babe.”

Agatha elbowed her gently. “Shut up.”

But she didn’t stop smiling.

Another buzz. Agatha glanced down at her phone, already biting back a smile she refused to admit was becoming automatic.

Rio Vidal

P.S. If I conjure another flower and it turns out to be a cactus, I’m blaming you .

Agatha let out a soft, incredulous laugh. Wanda, of course, perked up immediately from across the room.

“What?” she asked. “What’s so funny?”

“Nothing,” Agatha said smoothly, pocketing her phone. “Just someone threatening me with botanical vengeance. As one does.”

Wanda gave her a knowing smirk, but didn’t push this time. Vision came back into the kitchen, sipping from a mug, and Agatha used that as her cue.

“I should get going,” she said, already rising. “I have a date with my bath, my rabbit, and whatever book Scratchy hasn’t chewed the spine off yet.”

Vision gave her a warm pat on the shoulder. “Drive safe, Aggie.”

Wanda wagged her fingers. “Text your girlfriend goodnight.”

“She’s not my—” Agatha began, but Wanda had already turned away, humming to herself like she’d won something.

Agatha shook her head, pulling on her coat. Outside, the wind had softened, the stars peeking between slow-drifting clouds. And as she slid into her car, the phone in her pocket felt heavier than it should have. Not burdensome.

Just… real.

~*~

By the time Billy wandered into the apothecary, dragging his bag behind him like it had personally offended him, Agatha had already inventoried everything on the shelves, brewed a fresh batch of nettle tonic, and was elbows-deep in formulating a new restorative serum.

The morning had been quiet, productive. Just how she liked it.

Well. Almost.

She glanced at her phone again, sitting facedown beside the mortar she was grinding feverfew into. She’d already checked it three times in the last hour, which was pathetic, really. But then, around eight-thirty, it had buzzed.

Rio Vidal

Good morning. I think my magic might be broken. Or drunk. I’ve found three flowers so far—one in my coat pocket, one under the toaster (???), and one stuck to the back of my sketchbook. Explain yourself, Thread-witch .

Agatha had stared at the message for a full minute before allowing herself a quiet chuckle. 

Now, two hours later, the smile kept threatening to rise again every time she remembered the image of Rio pulling a bloom from under her toaster with that wide-eyed, irritated wonder of hers.

“You’re in a good mood,” Billy said, halfway through a yawn. “Should I be worried?”

“You’re always worried when I’m in a good mood,” Agatha replied without looking up. “That says more about you than me.”

“That’s fair,” he said, dropping his bag onto the floor and collapsing onto the stool at the counter. “What’s the potion of the day?”

“Something gentle. Goldenrod, valerian, a touch of lavender. For tension. Nightmares. General existential dread.”

“Love that for us,” Billy said. “Very marketable.”

Agatha arched a brow. “Dream Drip or Nocturne?”

“Mm. Nocturne sounds like a perfume. Dream Drip sounds illegal. Go with that.”

Agatha smiled, despite herself, and made a note in her journal. The moment stretched quietly, broken only by the soft hiss of steam and the faint tinkle of wind chimes from the front window.

“So,” Billy said, not looking up from the plant he was now spinning lazily on the counter, “what’s Rio up to?”

Agatha didn’t react—at least, not outwardly. “Why do you assume I’d know?”

“I saw her name on your phone just now,” he said, casual as anything. “Also, this is Thistle Grove. If three Threads breathe in the same county, the air gets weird.”

Agatha rolled her eyes, keeping her tone neutral. “She’s adjusting. That’s all.”

Billy grinned. “Did she draw anything weird yet? I feel like if I had Wild Thread powers, I’d accidentally animate a possum or something.”

“She hasn’t mentioned possums,” Agatha said, smirking just slightly. “But there have been… flowers.”

Billy’s eyes lit up. “Oooh. Accidental florals. A classic. That means it’s working, right?”

“It means something’s waking up,” Agatha murmured, almost to herself. Then, catching Billy’s look: “Which is good. She needs it. She’s been… through a lot.”

Billy didn’t press, thankfully. Just shrugged and reached for his notebook. “Well, if she conjures a carnivorous vine or a sentient tomato, I want to see.”

Agatha turned back to her potion, stirring once, slowly. “Noted.”

~*~

Agatha was alone again by the time she found it.

Billy had headed out with a lopsided grin and a bag of dried elderflower for one of Wanda’s late-night teas, leaving the shop in a lull of quiet sunlight and cooling herbs. The potion was steeping. The ledger was up to date. The air had settled.

She was clearing the counter when something caught her eye—tucked just behind the register, half-hidden beneath the edge of her journal.

A flower.

Not one of hers.

It was too wild. Too alive.

It looked like something born in a dream: bright violet petals dusted with silver, soft like rain-kissed silk, with a tiny pulse of green at the center—like it still remembered being rooted in soil. It hadn’t been there this morning. She was sure of it.

Agatha didn’t touch it at first.

She just stared, a twist of something sharp and unnamable fluttering in her chest. It was beautiful. And unmistakably Rio.

With the lightest touch, she lifted it free, brought it to her nose. It smelled like summer at midnight. Like salt and lilac and something ancient beneath it all.

And then, as if summoned—

The bell above the door jingled.

“Hey,” Rio said, slightly breathless, a tray with two to-go cups of coffee in one hand and a brown paper bag in the other. “I brought bribery. Coffee. Pastries. A ridiculous croissant that’s trying to collapse in on itself.”

Agatha turned slowly, flower still in her fingers.

She raised one brow. “Have you been planting things in my shop?”

Rio froze mid-step. “Oh god. Did you find one?”

“Vivid purple. Silver edges. Smells like secrets.”

Rio groaned. “That’s like the sixth one today. I think my subconscious is staging a floral uprising.”

Agatha held it out. “Well. It’s a charming uprising. I accept your peace offering.”

Instead of taking it back, Rio smiled and tilted her head. “Actually… that one’s for you.”

Agatha paused, a flicker of something warm tightening low in her stomach.

Rio gave a theatrical little bow. “For you, my lady.”

The silence that followed wasn’t awkward—it was electric.

Agatha accepted the flower without a word, tucking it behind her ear in one graceful motion.

Rio grinned. “Okay, that’s extremely unfair. You pull that off way too well.”

“You say that like it wasn’t your idea,” Agatha said, lips curving.

They stood there for a beat too long, that soft hum between them growing louder, fuller, like the air itself was aware of their edges brushing.

And then Rio cleared her throat, lifting the bag. “So. I brought you the almond croissant. Because I’m hoping we can keep talking about the Threads without getting interrupted by mayors or awkward energy from customers asking for herbal remedies for their failing marriages.”

Agatha smirked, gesturing toward the back. “Come on.”

They slipped into the reading nook near the back, where the windows let in lazy ribbons of sunlight and the old velvet cushions gave just enough to make it feel like sinking into a secret. Agatha watched as Rio set the coffee cups and the pastry bag down on the low table between them.

The couch was small. Or maybe it only felt that way now, with Rio beside her.

They weren’t touching, but gods, they were close. Agatha could feel the heat of Rio’s thigh near hers, the faint brush of her wool coat against Agatha’s sweater whenever one of them shifted even slightly.

Rio reached for her coffee, fingers brushing Agatha’s knuckles for the briefest moment.

Electric. Again.

Agatha didn’t flinch, but she didn’t lean in either. She just held her breath like the moment might deepen if she stayed perfectly still.

They were still curled into the nook, knees pressed close, words slow and quiet like the hush of a forest. Outside, the wind pushed against the windows, the kind of wind that meant rain later. But in here, it was warm. Safe. The scent of coffee and cinnamon hung between them, anchored by the heat of something neither one dared name yet.

Rio sipped her drink, eyes distant now. She’d been quiet for a while, fingers tapping lightly against the side of the cup. Agatha didn’t rush her.

Finally, Rio said, “I had a dream. I forgot to tell you. Sunday night.”

Agatha turned her head slightly, watching her with quiet interest.

“It wasn’t just a dream, I don’t think” Rio added, voice quieter. “Not like the kind you forget when you wake up. This one… felt like it was stitched into my brain.”

Agatha didn’t speak, but something in her stillness shifted. She recognized the tone. That tremor of something bigger moving beneath the words.

Rio glanced at her, then away. “She was there. Eliza.”

Agatha’s heart skipped. “Eliza?”

Rio nodded. “We were in a forest. She said it was good to see me, that I shouldn’t be afraid.”

Agatha let the silence settle again, inviting, but not pressing.

Rio’s voice dropped. “She told me about my mother.”

That made Agatha’s gaze sharpen. “Daisy?”

“Yeah.” Rio swallowed. “Said she was lost. Said the magic pulled her under, that she never came back. Something like that, I don’t know.”

Agatha felt something cold bloom in her chest, something painful and familiar.

“I always thought she just… didn’t want me,” Rio said. Her voice was steady, but her hands weren’t. “But Eliza said she didn’t leave because she didn’t love me. That it wasn’t up to her.”

Agatha let out a slow breath. “That’s a heavy thing to carry.”

“I don’t know if I can,” Rio whispered, eyes on their joined hands. “But I think I want to try.”

Agatha’s thumb moved lightly across her skin, soothing. “You won’t have to do it alone, you know. You’ll always have me and Wanda. We’re in this together.”

Rio looked up at her then. “I know.”

And that truth sat between them, quiet and sure, blooming like a flower neither of them dared name.

Not yet.

Agatha let her fingers linger a little longer against Rio’s wrist, then slowly, as if testing a boundary she wasn’t sure she could cross, let her hand slip into Rio’s fully. Not tight, not assuming—just there. Offered.

Rio didn’t pull away.

Instead, she gave a small, almost shy smile. “Sorry. That was a lot. I didn’t mean to just dump all that on you.”

Agatha shook her head. “No. It wasn’t. I’m glad you told me.”

The quiet settled again, but it wasn’t uncomfortable. It felt like the kind of silence that only came when two people didn’t feel the need to fill space with anything but presence.

Agatha tilted her head slightly, her thumb brushing lightly over the back of Rio’s hand. “So… aside from dream visitations and long-lost mother revelations… how’s your day been?”

Rio exhaled a soft laugh, her shoulders relaxing a little. “Weird, actually.”

“Oh?”

Rio lifted her cup in a small toast. “Woke up, found a flower blooming on my pillow. Perfectly intact, petals and all. No stem. Just… there. Like it decided to show up for breakfast.”

Agatha raised a brow, amused. “How very Thread of you.”

“Oh, it gets better,” Rio said, grinning now. “One fell out of my hoodie pocket while I was brushing my teeth. And I swear I opened the fridge and a little violet popped out of the butter dish.”

Agatha laughed—a rich, unguarded sound—and Rio’s smile deepened like it was reflexive.

Agatha’s voice was lower now. “No. Not normal.”

“But real?”

“Very.”

Neither moved. Neither looked away.

The air between them shimmered with something unspoken but undeniable. And though neither reached for more just yet, it felt like they’d already begun.

And Agatha—who’d sworn off all the things that made her feel too much—had no idea how to survive it.

She cleared her throat and forced herself to focus on anything else. “I… think your magic is responding to your emotions,” she said, voice quieter now, gentler. “It’s common when powers first start to settle. Especially with Wild Threads.”

Agatha huffed a breath of laughter, grateful for the shift in tone. “Basically. You should probably warn people before hugging them. Could trigger a full garden.”

Rio smiled but didn’t laugh, and the way she was looking at her now—like she saw right through the cracks Agatha tried so hard to patch—made something ache deep inside.

“I don’t think it’s just the magic,” Rio said softly. “I think it’s you.”

Agatha stilled.

Rio’s gaze dropped to her lips for a heartbeat—just one—and then flicked back up to her eyes. “When I’m near you, it feels like…” She faltered, like the weight of the words might be too much.

Agatha’s voice was barely audible. “Like what?”

Rio swallowed. “Like I’m waking up.”

Agatha’s heart clenched. She wanted to lean in. Wanted to close the gap and feel Rio’s mouth on hers—slow and searching and new. But something old and jagged twisted in her chest. Memory. Grief. Guilt. She had no right to feel this way again. Not after everything. Not when her power practically begged to unravel in Rio’s presence.

So instead of kissing her, she pulled back just slightly. Just enough to break the spell.

Rio noticed. Agatha saw it in the flicker of her eyes, the way she bit her lip and looked down.

“I’m sorry,” Rio murmured, starting to withdraw.

Agatha stopped her with a gentle squeeze of her hand. “Don’t be. Please. You didn’t do anything wrong.”

“Then why does it feel like I did?”

Agatha let her thumb brush lightly over Rio’s knuckles. “Because I’m… not used to wanting things I might actually get.”

Silence fell again—but this one was different. Heavier. Shared.

Rio moved before Agatha could stop her. She leaned in slowly, her breath a soft whisper against Agatha’s skin. The space between them seemed to stretch and pull, and in that suspended moment, Agatha could almost hear the pounding of her own heart, louder than anything else.

Their lips brushed, light and tentative at first, as though neither of them could quite believe they were doing this, this thing that had been lingering in the air between them for so long. Agatha felt the warmth of Rio’s breath, the tremble of her hand where it still held Agatha’s, and something inside her broke open—like a dam cracking, ready to drown her in everything she’d spent so many years avoiding.

Her power reacted, surged, like it recognized the moment—like it knew this was the kind of thing that could unravel everything. Rio’s magic, still so new and unshaped, met hers in that single brush of lips, and it was like fireworks behind her eyes, an electric pulse racing through her chest.

Gods, it was so much.

And then, in the middle of it—before Agatha could even savor the taste of Rio’s lips on hers, before she could let herself fall into it completely—the bell over the door jingled, the soft sound slicing through the intimacy of the moment like a cold splash of water.

They froze.

For a heartbeat, neither of them moved. Agatha’s lips hovered just above Rio’s, the air between them thick with what was left unsaid. Rio was still so close that Agatha could feel the warmth of her skin, the heat of her breath. It was all she could do not to pull her back in, to ignore the interruption and let everything else fade away.

But it was impossible.

They both broke apart at the same time, their hands falling away from each other with the kind of reluctance that made Agatha’s chest ache. She didn’t look at Rio. Instead, she stood, hurriedly smoothing down her shirt, trying to regain some semblance of composure, some shred of control.

Rio cleared her throat. “Guess that’s our cue,” she muttered, her voice thick with the same mix of frustration and longing Agatha felt.

Before Agatha could answer, the door opened fully, and in stepped a tall woman in a tailored suit, a clipboard in her hand. Agatha didn’t even have to look to know it was the town’s newest official—she’d been looking for a reason to talk to Agatha about her “unlicensed healing” practices for weeks.

“Agatha,” the woman said briskly, glancing at both of them. “I need to speak with you about your apothecary’s operating procedures. It’s about your new shipment of—”

Agatha didn’t hear the rest of the woman’s words, because Rio was already backing away, her face flushed. “I should… go,” she said quickly, not meeting Agatha’s eyes.

But Agatha wasn’t letting her leave that easily.

“Rio—” she started, but the woman with the clipboard cleared her throat again, sharply this time, cutting through the moment.

Rio shot her a look, soft but with a smile that didn’t quite reach her eyes. “We’ll talk later,” she promised.

And just like that, she was gone, leaving Agatha to stare after her, her heart still racing and her magic swirling in a wild, unpredictable dance.

~*~

Agatha didn’t even think about it. Before the woman with the clipboard could say another word, Agatha was already moving, her heart thumping hard in her chest as she rushed toward the door.

“Excuse me,” she muttered to the woman as she passed, her voice tight with barely-contained tension. She couldn’t let Rio slip away. Not now. Not after everything that had happened.

The door swung open with a soft jingle, and Agatha stepped out into the cool air of Thistle Grove. Rio was already several paces away, her back to Agatha as she made her way down the street, her posture stiff, the space between them suddenly feeling like miles.

Agatha’s breath hitched as she quickened her pace, almost frantic. This couldn’t be it. Not like this.

“Rio!” she called, her voice breaking the quiet street. Rio paused, just for a moment, but it was enough. Agatha was already there, just a few steps away, and before she could second-guess herself, she reached out, her hands finding Rio’s cheeks with a certainty that surprised even her.

Rio turned slowly, and that’s when Agatha kissed her—without hesitation, without fear. She kissed her with everything she was holding back, everything she had been feeling for days now. It wasn’t gentle, not this time—it was desperate, hungry, a kiss that demanded attention. Agatha held her with both hands on her cheeks, her thumbs brushing the soft skin as she deepened the kiss, her magic flaring, responding to the pull between them.

When they broke apart, they were both breathless, hearts hammering in their chests.

“Please don’t go,” Agatha whispered, her forehead resting against Rio’s, still holding her face in her hands as though she might lose her if she let go. “Let me just get rid of this nuisance,” she gestured back toward the apothecary, “and then it’s almost time to close anyway. We can go somewhere and talk?”

Rio’s lips were still swollen, and her eyes were full of surprise, the same need that Agatha felt mirrored in her gaze. She didn’t speak at first, just stood there, as if trying to process the kiss—then, finally, she nodded, her voice quiet but sincere.

“I’d like that,” she said, her hand finding Agatha’s, fingers curling around hers. “I’d really like that.”

Agatha walked back into the apothecary, feeling lighter, almost giddy, the lingering warmth of the kiss still buzzing through her. She closed the door behind her with a soft click and, without thinking, reached into her pocket to grab her keys—only to stop short.

A couple of roses had found their way into her pocket, their delicate petals soft against her fingers. She chuckled to herself, shaking her head in disbelief, already imagining how Rio must have conjured them, probably without even realizing it.

“I really don’t know whether to be impressed or a little freaked out,” Agatha muttered, turning the roses over in her hands as she smiled to herself.

Rio, still following her inside, raised an eyebrow at the sound of Agatha’s laugh. “What’s that?”

Agatha’s eyes flicked up to meet hers, and she couldn’t help but laugh even more, a quiet, almost delighted sound. “I found these in my pocket,” she said, showing Rio the roses. “Apparently, you’ve been hiding flowers on me.”

Rio raised a hand, an almost shy smile tugging at her lips, and sure enough, a single delicate flower had appeared in her palm as well—a small daisy, its white petals trembling slightly, as though it was alive with magic.

“Okay, this is getting a little ridiculous,” Rio said, her voice low, but her eyes alight with humor. “I swear I’m not doing this on purpose.”

Agatha’s laughter was soft and warm, and she walked over to Rio, reaching out to gently take the flower from her hand. Their fingers brushed together as she did, the spark of magic between them still palpable, still making her heart race.

“No need to apologize,” Agatha said, her voice soft but playful. “It’s actually… kind of perfect.” She pressed the flower gently into Rio’s hand, her fingers lingering on her palm just for a moment longer than necessary.

They stood there for a beat, the tension between them palpable, both of them aware of the unspoken things that hung in the air, the pull that neither of them could deny.

“Well,” Agatha said after a moment, her voice steady despite the way her heart seemed to be beating louder than normal, “I promised I’d get rid of the nuisance. It’s just… a few more minutes, and we’ll be good to go.”

She offered Rio a smile, one that was warmer now, more genuine. It felt easier, lighter, with Rio standing there, and for the first time, Agatha didn’t feel like she was pretending, or running from something. She felt… open.

Rio returned her smile, her eyes full of something unspoken—something that felt just as right.

“I can wait,” Rio said, her voice soft and teasing. 

Agatha felt the weight of that simple statement, and her smile widened, before she turned back to deal with the last few details of the apothecary. But the moment was never really gone. It lingered, just beneath the surface, both of them knowing there was something far more between them than they’d yet acknowledged.

And for once, Agatha was okay with that.

 

Chapter 11: Eleven

Chapter Notes

I really, really love your thoughtful, in depth comments so much. Thank you, thank you, thank you!

I hope you enjoy this one. Please note the change in rating. Read at your own discretion.

Rio settled herself back into the cozy reading nook, trying to focus on the pages of the book she had picked up. But it was useless. Every time she looked down, her mind kept drifting back to the kiss—the way Agatha’s lips had felt against hers, the warmth of her touch, the energy that seemed to pulse between them.

It was like a current, like something inside her had been awakened, and now it was impossible to turn off. Her fingers traced the spine of the book absently as she replayed the moment over and over in her mind.

She had been caught completely off guard. There was something in Agatha’s eyes before their lips met, an intensity that mirrored her own. It was as though they both knew what was happening, even if they didn’t fully understand it. It felt… inevitable, in a way.

She closed her eyes, trying to shake off the tingling sensation that had settled in her chest. Her breath still felt shallow, her heart a little unsteady. And yet, despite how much it had taken her by surprise, Rio couldn’t help but want more.

More of that connection.

More of that spark.

More of Agatha.

She opened her eyes again, her gaze falling on the small glass of water where Agatha had put the flowers she had conjured earlier. Flowers seemed to appear wherever she went now, as if responding to the pull of something—someone. 

Agatha, maybe. 

She couldn’t explain it, but the more time she spent with the woman, the more her magic seemed to hum, like it was responding to her presence.

And now, after that kiss, she couldn’t help but wonder what else her magic might do when it was tied to Agatha. It wasn’t just her power that had reacted—it was something deeper, something personal. Like the threads that connected them were finally becoming visible, something tangible between them.

Rio looked up as the door to the apothecary creaked open. She didn’t need to see who it was to know—it was Agatha. Her heart skipped, and she felt a little breathless again, even though she had been sitting here, waiting for this moment. She hadn’t expected to feel like this, but now it seemed impossible to stop.

Agatha appeared in the doorway, her eyes meeting Rio’s immediately, and the world outside seemed to disappear for just a second. Rio stood, smiling, trying to act normal, though her pulse was still racing.

“Ready?” Agatha asked, her voice warm but tinged with something else, something unspoken between them.

Rio nodded, her hands still tingling from the memory of their kiss. “Yeah. Let’s go.”

As they stepped out of the apothecary and into the cool evening air, Rio felt that same pull between them, as undeniable as before. The tension hadn’t eased, and if anything, it felt even stronger now. She wasn’t sure where this was headed, but one thing was for sure—she wasn’t ready to let go of whatever was beginning to unfold between them.

They walked together down the quiet street, side by side, and Rio couldn’t help but feel that they were both walking toward something that was already becoming more than either of them had expected.

And maybe, just maybe, it was something they both needed.

As they walked side by side, Rio couldn’t resist the urge anymore. Her fingers reached out and threaded through Agatha’s, gently intertwining their hands. The moment her fingers brushed against Agatha’s, there was a spark—a current that seemed to shoot through her veins and send her pulse racing. Agatha didn’t hesitate. Her fingers tightened around Rio’s, a simple, affectionate squeeze, like she was holding onto something precious.

Rio looked up at her, and there it was again—Agatha’s gaze, warm and open, filled with that unmistakable intensity. It was as though nothing could break through this connection, as though their paths had been aligned long before they’d even met. Agatha didn’t try to hide how she felt anymore. That wall that had been so carefully constructed between them was crumbling, piece by piece, and Rio felt every shift, every crack.

Her heart fluttered in her chest, and her breath caught in her throat. She had never felt anything like this before, this all-encompassing pull, this raw energy that flowed between them effortlessly. It was almost overwhelming.

And it was just a kiss. Just a kiss, and yet Rio couldn’t even begin to imagine what more would feel like. What would sex be like with Agatha? Her body tensed at the thought, her stomach flipping with the possibility. If this was how it felt with just a kiss—how could she even begin to wrap her mind around the depth of what it would be like to be fully with her? To surrender to this connection, to feel everything tenfold as Agatha’s magic and hers collided in the most intimate way?

Her head spun with the thought. What if they were meant to be like this? What if it was always going to be this intense, this undeniable? She could feel Agatha’s power in every touch, in every word, in the way their hands fit together like they were always meant to. And Rio? Her own power was responding in kind. It was like her very soul had been tethered to Agatha’s, and no matter how much she tried to rationalize it, to hold back, it was too late now.

The intensity of it was thrilling, but also terrifying. Rio had always been someone who kept her heart guarded, especially after everything with Carol. But with Agatha, everything felt different. She felt seen, understood, in a way she had never allowed herself to feel before. And damn it, it was scaring the hell out of her.

Agatha’s voice broke through Rio’s racing thoughts, grounding her back in the moment. She turned to Agatha, her hand still securely holding hers, and saw the same quiet intensity in her eyes. It made Rio’s heart skip a beat.

“So,” Agatha said, her tone low and playful, “Do you want to grab something to eat, or should we just head back to my place—or your cottage, if you prefer?”

Rio considered the options, the idea of continuing this quiet, intimate walk with Agatha tempting, but at the same time, the thought of retreating to either of their spaces, away from prying eyes, felt even more appealing. She could feel the anticipation of their next steps building, the tension between them thickening with every second.

“Whichever you prefer,” Rio replied, her voice steady, though her heart was beating a little faster than normal. She wanted to say more—maybe joke, or tease—but the weight of the question hung in the air, both of them clearly aware of what was simmering underneath.

Agatha raised an eyebrow, a smirk tugging at the corner of her lips. “It’s all the same to me, Rio. But I’m thinking something a little more private, you know? Less… distractions.” She glanced around, like the bustling streets of Thistle Grove were suddenly too loud for them.

Rio felt her breath hitch at the word love, but she managed to keep her composure, nodding slightly. “Maybe we can go to my place?”

Agatha’s hand squeezed hers tighter. “Alright then, let’s go.”

As they walked toward Rio’s car, Agatha’s steps slowed for a moment, and she glanced at Rio again, her expression softening. “Is this alright? I know it’s fast, but… I just want to be with you.”

Rio met her gaze, feeling that same pull, the unspoken desire flickering in her chest. “Yeah. It’s perfect,” she said softly, her voice barely above a whisper.

The quiet intimacy of the evening was palpable as Rio led Agatha to her car. It was a short drive from the apothecary to the cottage, but somehow the air felt thicker, like the anticipation between them was enough to fill the space of the entire journey.

Agatha climbed into the passenger seat, a slight smile on her lips, and Rio slid into the driver’s seat, her hands briefly resting on the wheel as she glanced over at Agatha. Her heartbeat was still racing from the earlier kiss, and now, knowing they were about to be alone in a place that Rio had never shared with anyone else, it felt like the next step in some unspoken dance.

The car hummed to life, and Rio eased out onto the quiet roads of Thistle Grove. The evening was settling in—cool air brushing against the windows, the streets empty, save for the occasional streetlamp flickering overhead. The drive was peaceful, and yet, every inch of silence between them was filled with the electric tension of what was to come.

“You okay?” Rio asked, keeping her eyes on the road, though she could feel Agatha’s gaze on her, warm and steady.

Agatha’s smile softened, her eyes flickering briefly to Rio’s hands on the wheel. “I’m more than okay. Just… trying to wrap my head around everything.” Her voice was low, thoughtful, but there was an unmistakable undercurrent of something else, something she didn’t quite know how to express just yet.

Rio felt a surge of emotion. She couldn’t quite place it, but it felt like everything was shifting between them—maybe it was the kiss, maybe it was the way Agatha was looking at her, like she was finally allowing herself to be present in a way she hadn’t before.

“I think we’re both figuring things out,” Rio murmured, trying to focus on the road as they drove through the night. “But… I’m glad we’re doing it together.”

Agatha’s gaze never wavered as she turned her head toward Rio, her voice soft but clear. “Me too. It’s… been a long time since I’ve felt something like this.”

Rio’s chest tightened, a mixture of warmth and fear curling in her gut. She hadn’t realized how much she was yearning for this connection, but with every passing second, it felt more like a dream that was somehow slipping into reality.

As they pulled up to the cottage, the soft glow of the porch light illuminating the front steps, Rio cut the engine. She looked over at Agatha, her heart still beating rapidly. “Here we are,” she said quietly, offering a small, almost shy smile.

Agatha smiled back, her eyes holding a softness that made Rio’s breath catch. She had no idea what would come next, but she knew she wasn’t afraid anymore. Not of Agatha, not of the feelings that were flooding her, and not of what might lie ahead.

Together, they got out of the car and headed toward the cottage, the evening stretching out before them like an unwritten chapter, ready to be filled with whatever they decided to write.

~*~

Salem greeted them at the door like he’d been expecting them, weaving between their legs with a triumphant little trill and a tail held high. Rio laughed, her hand brushing lightly against Agatha’s lower back as they stepped inside. The cottage felt warmer somehow, as if it had been holding its breath for this exact moment.

“Welcome to my humble witch hut,” Rio joked, toying with the light switches as she kicked off her shoes and led Agatha inside. “I promise it’s slightly more organized than my brain.”

Agatha chuckled as her eyes swept across the cozy living room with its mismatched furniture, the books stacked two and three deep on every surface, the half-finished sketches clipped to a corkboard near the windows. “It’s perfect,” she murmured, voice so low it sent something fluttering in Rio’s chest.

“Come on,” Rio said, beckoning her toward the kitchen. “You’re getting dinner whether you like it or not.”

“I never said I didn’t like it,” Agatha replied smoothly, following close behind, the sound of her heels soft against the wood floor. “I’m just curious what a tortured artist-slash-secret witch eats on a Tuesday night.”

Rio shot her a look over her shoulder. “You’re making an awful lot of assumptions for someone standing in my kitchen.”

That earned her a warm, unrepentant grin.

She handed Agatha a glass of wine and then got to work, pulling out fresh herbs, eggs, spinach, and feta from the fridge. “You like omelets?”

“I like watching you take charge in your own kitchen,” Agatha said with a hint of a smirk, sipping her wine.

Rio glanced at her sideways, lips twitching. “You’re impossible.”

“And yet, here I am,” Agatha said, leaning against the counter, watching her move. “Surprised you can cook, though.”

Rio gasped in mock offense. “I’ll have you know I’m excellent at making breakfast for dinner. You should be honored.”

“Honored and turned on, apparently,” Agatha murmured, so quietly Rio almost didn’t hear it.

She looked up sharply, her heart skipping. Agatha’s eyes were fixed on her, and there was no teasing now—just hunger, plain and devastating.

Before Rio could make another joke or cut another vegetable, Agatha was closing the space between them.

The kiss hit her like a storm.

One moment, Rio was slicing a tomato, and the next, Agatha’s lips were on hers—fierce, needy, like she’d been holding back for too long. Rio dropped the knife without a second thought, her hands flying to Agatha’s waist, pulling her in like a magnet she couldn’t resist. The counter pressed into Rio’s hip as Agatha angled her closer, their bodies flush, breath mingling.

The kiss deepened fast, too fast, and Rio felt herself burning up under the weight of it, her magic crackling under her skin like a live wire.

She barely had the sense to whisper between breaths, “Agatha…”

But Agatha was already cupping her face, fingers threading into Rio’s hair as if to anchor her there. As if this was the only place either of them belonged.

The omelet was forgotten. The wine left untouched on the counter.

There was only this—this fire, this ache, this incredible pull between them that felt less like desire and more like destiny breaking open right under their feet.

Rio didn’t remember moving. One second she was gripping Agatha’s waist like a lifeline, and the next she was walking them backward, blindly guiding them out of the kitchen, through the soft-lit living room, mouths barely parting even to breathe.

Agatha’s fingers slipped beneath the hem of Rio’s shirt, just enough to touch skin, and Rio gasped into her mouth, her body sparking like kindling set alight. She wanted to slow down. She wanted to take her time. But the look in Agatha’s eyes—dark, aching, filled with so many years of silence and solitude—made her feel like waiting would be cruel.

The backs of Rio’s knees hit the edge of the couch, and she sank down with a breathless laugh, pulling Agatha into her lap. Their mouths met again with even more urgency, as if every second apart had been a mistake they were desperate to undo.

Agatha straddled her, both hands cupping Rio’s face as she kissed her again—slower now, but no less intense. Her thumbs stroked gently along Rio’s cheekbones, like she was memorizing the shape of her.

“Gods, you feel like…” Agatha whispered, her forehead pressed against Rio’s. “You feel like something I lost and just found again.”

Rio’s hands slid under Agatha’s shirt, palms flat against her back, holding her steady. “You make me feel like I can breathe again,” she whispered, eyes searching Agatha’s. “Like I’m finally coming up for air.”

“And you are?”

“Dr. Mara Greaves,” she replied, producing a card and setting it on the counter. “I’m a folklore researcher from Saint Cyprian University. I’m working on a book about regional magical myths, and Thistle Grove has come up again and again—particularly this persistent legend about the Threads?”

Agatha picked up the card. Ivory paper, embossed lettering. Dr. Mara Greaves, Department of Occult and Folklore Studies. No website. No contact info. Just a discreet symbol in the corner—barely noticeable, unless you knew what to look for.

A circle, divided into three sections. A trinity. A wheel.

Agatha kept her face carefully blank. “Most of the stories around here are just that. Stories.”

“Oh, I know,” Mara said, stepping further in, eyes drifting over the shelves. “But I find it fascinating how consistently the myth appears across different sources. Three women. Three powers. One line broken and restored.” She smiled, a little too pleased with herself. “It’s quite poetic.”

Agatha tilted her head. “Folklore usually is. Romanticized tragedy is a crowd favorite.”

“Of course.” Mara turned, gaze landing on her again. “Still, it’s rare for a town to preserve its myths so intimately. The Threads weren’t just fairy tales, were they? People here remember them.”

“People here remember a lot of things,” Agatha replied coolly. “Doesn’t make them real.”

“No,” Mara agreed. “But sometimes the stories are just cleverly disguised truths. That’s what I’m interested in. The patterns beneath the surface.”

Agatha narrowed her eyes. “You said you just got into town?”

“This morning. I’ll be staying at The Larkspur Inn while I conduct interviews and poke around the library and public records.”

“You won’t find much in there. This town doesn’t write down what it doesn’t want outsiders to see.”

Mara’s smile only widened. “I was hoping you might help with that.”

Agatha gave a dry chuckle. “You were hoping wrong.”

“Well.” Mara slid her hands into the pockets of her coat, her expression never wavering. “Thank you for your time. I’m sure we’ll see each other again. Small towns have a way of looping in on themselves.”

With that, she turned and left, the door closing behind her with a final-sounding click.

Agatha didn’t move for a long moment. The rose in her pocket suddenly felt heavier. Warmer. She took it out and threw it in a basin, setting it on fire until there was nothing but ashes.

Then she flipped the card over—and her stomach clenched.

There was no ink on the back. No embossed signature. But pressed into the cardstock like a seal was a sigil she hadn’t seen in over two decades.

Eliza’s warning rose like a whisper from the grave:

If you ever see that mark again, run. Or burn it. But do not, under any circumstances, let it stay close.

Agatha crushed the card in her hand, her heart pounding now, not with excitement, not with the lingering rush of Rio’s magic—but with dread.

She turned toward the back room, already reaching for her phone.

Time to call Wanda.

~*~

Agatha locked the apothecary with quick, practiced movements, the crushed card still clutched tightly in her hand. She slipped it into her coat pocket, fingers trembling just slightly as she brought out her phone.

She scrolled past a few missed messages from supply vendors and one from Billy—something about his essay on “plants that could kill but probably won’t.”

And then there was her.

Rio.

She hesitated for a second, then typed quickly.

Agatha

Hey, something came up. I need to meet Wanda… little magical situation I want to get ahead of. I’ll explain when I see you. 

If you’re free, you can come meet us. I’d like that. If not, I’ll be a little late. 

She didn’t wait for the reply. She already knew Rio would either show up with that determined look in her eyes or text back in concern laced with sarcasm. Either way, Agatha felt lighter for telling her.

Still holding her phone, she dialed Wanda. The call picked up before the second ring.

“Please tell me this is about those strawberry scones you promised me and not something grim,” Wanda said without preamble.

“Strawberry scones might be less of a threat,” Agatha muttered, glancing around the street as she walked. “You remember that seal Eliza warned us about years ago? The tri-sigil?”

Wanda was quiet for a second. Then: “You saw it?”

“Pressed into a card. Woman came into the shop. Claimed to be a folklore researcher—Dr. Mara Greaves. Saint Cyprian University, apparently.”

Wanda hissed through her teeth. “That sounds like bullshit.”

“I know,” Agatha said, voice tight. “I need to talk. Somewhere quiet.”

“Meet me at the overlook. Ten minutes?”

“Done.”

Agatha hung up, shoving the phone into her coat pocket. The wind had picked up, sharp and sudden, curling around her ankles like a warning. But she squared her shoulders and kept walking, faster now.

The overlook sat on a low hill just outside the heart of Thistle Grove, where the land dipped into forest beyond. They used to come here as teenagers, just the two of them, drinking pilfered wine and talking magic under the stars. It felt right, coming back here for this.

She was almost there when her phone buzzed again.

Rio 

Of course I’ll come. Just tell me where. You okay? Should I bring snacks or backup?

Agatha’s smile broke through her tension.

Agatha

Meet us at the overlook. Know where that is? And always bring snacks AND backup. 

~*~

The overlook hadn’t changed in twenty years—still windswept and wild, the iron arch rusted at the edges, moss curling over the stones like nature’s quiet protection spell. Agatha stood with her arms crossed, the wind tugging at her coat as Wanda approached from the path, her scarf blazing red against the gray horizon.

“Alright,” Wanda said as she neared. “What happened?”

Agatha didn’t speak at first. She stared out at the tree line, fingers curling tighter into the sleeves of her coat.

“A woman came into the shop this morning. Said she was researching local folklore,” she said finally. “Said she’d read about the Threads and wanted to know more.”

Wanda frowned. “That’s not too weird. People ask about the Threads all the time.”

“Before she showed up, a rose appeared on the counter. Not Rio’s. The magic signature was off. Heavy. I burned it the second she left the shop.” Agatha said, turning to face her. “And then there was the Tri-Sigil. She had a calling card with it pressed on the back. Exact engraving. Not a modern interpretation. The original.”

Wanda’s brow furrowed, tension knotting between her shoulders. “That’s not good.”

“I know.” Agatha took a slow breath, rubbing the bridge of her nose. “And I didn’t feel a hint of magic on her. She was too normal.”

Wanda’s mouth twisted. “That’s never a good sign.”

Before either of them could say more, the wind shifted and the soft crunch of gravel sounded behind them. Agatha turned to find Rio walking toward them, cheeks flushed, curls wild around her face. She looked like a woman who had run the last part of the path, concern etched in every line of her beautiful face.

“There you are,” Rio said, breathless but smiling. “I didn’t mean to intrude, but you said I could come find you.”

Agatha moved without thinking, closing the distance to pull her in. “You’re not intruding. I’m glad you came.”

Rio pressed a kiss to her cheek. “You sounded off. What’s going on?”

Wanda cleared her throat. “That’s my cue to let you two talk. I’ll give you a minute.”

But Agatha stopped her with a glance. “No. Stay.”

Rio looked between them, brows knitting together. “Okay. You’re worrying me.”

Agatha gently took Rio’s hand, anchoring herself with the contact. “Earlier this afternoon, someone came into the shop asking about the Threads. Claimed to be a folklore scholar. Which happens. But…” She hesitated, watching Rio’s face. “She knew things she shouldn’t. Symbols she shouldn’t have access to.”

Rio went still. “You think she’s dangerous?”

“I think she might be connected to something dangerous,” Agatha said carefully. “And I didn’t want to bring this up so soon—especially after last night—but… you deserve to know something is shifting in town. And I need you to be careful.”

Rio nodded slowly. “Okay. I trust you.”

Something inside Agatha softened. She hadn’t realized how much she needed to hear those words. “I’ll explain everything. Just not here. Not now.”

Wanda stepped forward again. “We’ll figure out who she is. But Agatha’s right—this woman isn’t just writing a research paper.”

Rio looked at her, then at Wanda, then back. “Alright. Then tell me how I can help.”

Agatha’s hand tightened around hers. “You already are.”

And when Rio smiled—bright, fierce, unshaken—it lit something in Agatha that no threat could dim.

Agatha felt Rio’s fingers twitch gently in hers, a subtle shift that said her brain was already working a mile a minute. Then Rio looked between her and Wanda with a gleam in her eye—half challenge, half promise.

“Well,” she said, tilting her head, “if we’re looking into secret societies and creepy folklore researchers, I might be able to pull in a little help.”

Wanda raised an eyebrow. “Oh?”

Rio grinned. “My best friend, Alice Wu. She’s a PhD in computer science, an insufferable know-it-all, and a terrifyingly good hacker. She once got into the Pentagon’s digital archives just to prove a point.”

Agatha blinked. “She what?”

“It was a very specific point,” Rio added with a smirk. “But the takeaway is—if this folklore researcher left even the tiniest digital trace behind, Alice will find it. And she’ll do it with a smoothie in one hand and a cat in her lap.”

Wanda gave an approving hum. “We like her already.”

Agatha chuckled despite herself. The tension that had been squeezing her chest since Mara Greaves had shown up at the apothecary loosened a fraction. Trusting Rio was starting to feel as instinctive as breathing—and now, the possibility of real help, real allies, felt less like grasping at moonlight.

Agatha squeezed Rio’s hand, brushing her thumb over the back of her knuckles. “Just… promise you’ll be careful. If these are Watchers—if she’s one of them—then poking the hive too hard might get us all stung.”

Rio’s smile was soft but unyielding. “I promise. But I didn’t come all this way to sit on the sidelines while you deal with shadowy creeps alone.”

Wanda looked between them, and for once, didn’t say a word. She just smiled—a little crooked, a little sad—and turned her face to the breeze as if listening for something on the wind.

Agatha had a feeling the winds were shifting indeed.

“Okay,” Rio said, shifting her weight. “Maybe we should move this little council to the cottage. I can throw dinner together while we talk, and it’ll give me a chance to call Alice—get her eyes on this without the magical bits.”

Wanda arched a brow. “You mean the witch-free version of this conspiracy?”

“Exactly,” Rio replied. “Alice is a digital bloodhound. If there’s anything to find about our mysterious friend or her ‘research,’ she’ll find it.”

Agatha gave her a faint smile, the kind that said thank you without needing the words. The idea of being in Rio’s space again, especially after the night they’d shared, felt like the safest place she could be. She needed that safety now.

“Good idea,” Agatha said. “Let me just grab my bag.”

Wanda didn’t move. “You think I’m not coming?”

Agatha blinked. “I figured you had—”

Wanda scoffed, cutting her off with a smirk. “If someone’s sniffing around about the Threads, I’m not sitting this one out. I’ll even bring wine.”

Rio grinned. “Then it’s a party.”

Agatha gave her friend a dry look. “A secret meeting disguised as a dinner party, more like.”

“Same difference,” Wanda said, already heading toward the door. 

Agatha lingered just long enough to catch Rio’s eye again. There was something quiet and reassuring in the look she gave her, like a thread pulling her gently toward steadier ground.

It was happening again—this constant pull between them, electric and soft all at once. But there wasn’t time to dwell. Not yet.

With a little sigh and the familiar flutter in her chest, Agatha turned and followed them out, already bracing herself for whatever the night would bring.

~*~

By the time the sky began to deepen into twilight, Agatha had retrieved an overnight bag from her house, and Wanda had swung by her place for a bottle of wine and a tray of something decadent and chocolatey she swore she didn’t buy for this occasion—though no one believed her.

The lights inside the cottage spilled golden and soft through the windows, welcoming them like something sacred. Rio had left the door unlocked, and the moment Agatha stepped inside, she was wrapped in the smell of garlic, herbs, and roasted vegetables. It grounded her in the best way, like a tether pulling her out of her racing thoughts.

“Shoes off at the door,” Rio called from the kitchen, not looking up from the skillet she was tending. “House rules.”

Wanda snorted but obeyed. “You’re so domestic. It’s disturbing.”

Rio shot her a dry look over her shoulder. “Don’t ruin the illusion.”

Agatha dropped her bag by the door and peeled off her boots, her gaze lingering on Rio’s back—barefoot, sleeves rolled up, the hem of her worn tee rising just slightly when she moved.
